Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Compensation for a Slave's Hand in Usurpation: Capped or Uncapped?
Explaining compensation for a slave's hand in usurpation with proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for compensation of a slave's hand in usurpation and explain why the preferred position caps the amount at 4,995 dirhams.

Core Idea
A slave's limb compensation is based on market value but capped to reflect lower status than a free person.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the compensation for a slave's hand when it is severed by a usurper. While the general principle in usurpation is to consider material value, the diyah framework introduces a comparison with free persons. We will see how the Hanafi scholars reconciled these principles and why the preferred position involves a subtle cap.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the usurper cutting the hand of a slave. The ruling is that the usurper must pay compensation (wajib).
Compensation is obligatory because it is a form of financial liability.
2
State the general principle in usurpation
In usurpation, only the financial value (qimah) of the property is considered, not any human worth. Therefore, the slave's market value is the starting point.
3
Note the analogy with a free person's diyah
For a free person, the diyah for a hand is half the full diyah (5,000 dirhams). Similarly, for a slave, the compensation should be half the slave's market value.
This analogy is based on the hadith of Ibn Abbas setting the diyah of a free person at 10,000 dirhams.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference
The Zahir al-Riwayah position (from the six books) holds that half the market value is due without a cap. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani narrates a cap: if the slave is worth 10,000 dirhams or more, the compensation is 5,000 minus 5 dirhams (4,995 dirhams).
The cap is to show the slave's lower rank compared to a free person.
5
Apply the tarjih (preferral)
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah prefers the capped amount, citing the narration of Muhammad al-Shaybani and the need to be consistent with the scriptural determination of diyah amounts.
The mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school is the capped amount for high-value slaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات/ أنواعها وأحكامها

[
]
( وفي الغصب قيمته ما كانت) ، هذا بالإجماع، فإنَّ المعتبر في الغصب المالية لا الآدمية.
نصف
(وما قدر من دِيَةِ الحَرِّ قُدِّرَ من قيمته ) : أي قيمة العبد، ففي يده قيمته (۳) : أي إن كانت قيمته عشرة آلاف أو أكثر، يجب في يده خمسة آلاف إلا خمسة دراهم. مكلفاً ، ا، وفيه معنى المالية حتى ورد عليه الملك بلا خلاف، والآدمية أعلاها.
فيجب اعتبارها بإهدار الأدنى عند تعذر الجمع بينهما، وضمان الغصب بمقابلة المالية، إذ الغصب لا يرد إلا على المال، وليس القتل بمنزلة استهلاكِ سائر الأموال ؛ لأنَّ ضمان المال يشبه ضمان التجارة، وبقاء العقد يتّبع الفائدة حتى يبقى بعد قتله عمداً، وإن لم يكن القصاص بدلاً عن المالية، فكذلك أمر الدية.
وفي قليل القيمة الواجب بمقابلة الآدمية إلا أنه لا يسمع فيه، فقدرناه بقيمته رأياً بخلاف كثير القيمة ؛ لأنّ قيمة الحرّ مقدّرة بعشرة آلاف كما مر ، نقصنا منها في العبد إظهاراً لانحطاط رتبته، وأما تعيين العشرة فبأثر عبد الله بن عباس ، وهذا كالمروي عن النبي ؛ لأنَّ المقادير لا تعرفُ بالقياس، وليس طريق معرفته إلا بالسماع عن صاحب الوحي. هكذا في «الهداية» حواشيه.
[١]قوله : وفي الغصب ... الخ ؛ صورته رجلٌ غصب عبداً قيمته عشرون ألفاً، فهلك في يده تجب قيمته بالغة ما بلغت هذا بلا خلاف فإنّ المعتبر في الغصب المالية لا الآدمية ؛ لأنَّ الغصب لا يرد إلا على المال كما لا يخفى.
[٢]قوله: نصف قيمته ؛ أي نصفُ قيمة العبد ، وفي «المبسوط») : يجب نصف قيمته بالـغـة مـا بلغت في الصحيح من الجواب، إلا في رواية عن محمد الله : إنه يجب في قطع يده خمسة آلاف إلا خمسة.
Book of blood money / its types and rulings. And in usurpation, its value is whatever it was. And whatever is estimated from the diyah of a free person is estimated from its value, so for his hand, half its value. (And in usurpation, its value is whatever it was) - this is by consensus, for what is considered in usurpation is financial value, not human worth. Half. (And whatever is estimated from the diyah of a free person is estimated from its value): i.e., the value of the slave, so for his hand, its value (3): i.e., if his value is ten thousand or more, five thousand minus five dirhams is obligated for his hand. ... And in it is the meaning of financial value...
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, explaining the compensation for a slave's hand in cases of usurpation. The author opens by stating the foundational principle: in usurpation (ghasp), only the material value of the property is considered, not any human worth. This is agreed upon by consensus. For a slave, the compensation for a lost limb is calculated as half of his market value, just as for a free person the diyah for a hand is half the full diyah (5,000 dirhams). However, the author notes a nuance: if the slave's value is 10,000 dirhams or more, the amount is capped at 5,000 dirhams minus 5 dirhams (i.e., 4,995 dirhams). This reduction is to reflect the lower status of a slave compared to a free person. The cap is derived from a hadith of Ibn Abbas, transmitted by Al-Hidayah author, and is chosen over the uncapped half-value reported in a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani. The passage emphasizes that such measurements cannot be determined by analogy but must be based on revelation. The commentator clarifies that the example given is a slave worth 20,000 dirhams, and the required payment is its full value if it perishes in the usurper's hands, but for a limb, it follows the diyah rules.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property wrongfully without permission.
دية (diyah): Blood-money; monetary compensation for unlawful killing or injury.
قيمة (qimah): Market value of a property, especially a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Hidayah's preferred position, what is the compensation for the hand of a slave worth 12,000 dirhams?
A) 6,000 dirhams
B) 5,000 dirhams
C) 4,995 dirhams
D) 12,000 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The compensation for a slave's hand is half his market value but capped at 4,995 dirhams for high-value slaves to reflect his lower rank compared to a free person.

Bayan as Insha: Diyat for a Partially Freed Slave
Ruling on diya when a conditionally freed slave is killed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of bayan as insha vs izhar to determine liability for diyat when a conditionally freed slave is killed.

Core Idea
Bayan after an event creates freedom at that moment, not retroactively — affecting diyat when a conditionally freed slave is killed.

This ruling from al-Hidayah explores the intersection of usul al-fiqh (bayan) and diyat. When a master says 'one of you is free' but does not specify, and then an injury or killing occurs, the timing of specification determines whether the slave is considered free or enslaved for liability purposes. We will unpack the scenarios and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial scenario
Identify the master's ambiguous declaration ('one of you is free') and the subsequent injury or killing of both slaves before specification.
The key is the timing of specification relative to the harmful event.
2
Apply the bayan principle
Recognize that bayan (clarification) after an injury is treated as insha (creation), not izhar (manifestation). This means the slave is considered free only from the moment of specification, not retroactively.
This is derived from Hanafi usul: where there is still room for creation, bayan is insha.
3
Distinguish between arsh and diya
Arsh (compensation for wounds) goes to the master because at the time of injury the slaves were still owned. Diya (blood money) for a free man goes to heirs if the killing occurs after specification.
For killing, the status at death matters.
4
Analyze sequential vs simultaneous killing
If two killers act sequentially, the first killer pays slave value (since at that moment both were slaves), and the second pays diya of a free man (because the freeing is confirmed by the first death). If simultaneous, both pay slave value due to uncertainty.
The principle of certainty (yaqin) governs: we only oblige what is certain.
5
Compare injury and death scenarios
After injury, there is still room for bayan to create freedom, so bayan is insha. After death, no room for bayan, so bayan is izhar retroactively? Actually the text says after death we consider it pure manifestation because the freeing is certain in one of them.
The difference leads to different liabilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أعتق أحد عبديه فشُجا، فعين أحدهما ، فأرشُهُما للسيد ، فإن قتلهما رجلٌ تجب دية حرّ، وقيمة عبد، وإن قتل كلا رجل فقيمة العبدين ) : أي قال لعبديه : أحدكما حر ثم شُجَّا فبين السيِّدُ أنَّ المراد بأحدهما هذا المعين، فأرشهما للسيد " لما عرف أنَّ البيان إظهار من وجه ، وإنشاء من وجه ، وبعدَ الشَّجةِ يبقى محلا للإنشاء ، فاعتبر إنشاء ، فكأنه أعتق وقت البيان. قوله : تجب... الخ ؛ أي تجب ديةُ حرّ للورثة، وقيمة عبد للمولى. قوله : فقيمة العبدين ... الخ ؛ أي إن قتل كلُّ واحدٍ منهما معاً رجل، فقيمة العبدين واجب ؛ لأنا لم يتيقن بقتل واحدٍ منهما حراً ، وكلّ منهما ينكر ذلك، قال في «الكفاية» : هذا إذا قتلهما معاً ولا يدري أيهما قتل أوّلاً ، أما إذا قتلهما رجلان فإن كان قتلهما على التعاقب فعلى القاتل الأوّل قيمة الأوّل لمولاه، وعلى القاتل بالثاني ديةُ لورثته ؛ لأنَّ العتق تعين. وأما لو قتلاهما معاً فعلى كلّ واحدٍ منهما قيمة عبد ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ من القاتلين إنما قتل أحدهما بعينه والعتق في حق المعيّن كأنه غير نازل ، فكان كل واحد منهما مملوكاً عيناً، وإنما نزل العتق في المنكر، ولا نتيقن أنّ كلّ واحد منهما قاتل لذلك المنكر، وإنما يجب على كلّ واحدٍ منهما القدر المتيقن به وهو القيمة. قوله : فأرشهما للسيد ... الخ ؛ والفرقُ أن البيان إنشاء من وجه، وإظهار من وجه على ما عرف في أصول الفقه، وبعد الشجّة بقي محل للبيان، فاعتبر إنشاء في حقهما، وبعد الموت لم يبق محلا للبيان، فاعتبرناه إظهاراً محضاً، وأحدهما حرّ بيقين، فتجب قيمة عبد ودية حرّ. بخلاف المسألة الثالثة، حيث تجب قيمة العبدين ؛ لأنا لم نتيقن بقتل كلّ واحد منهما حراً، وكلّ منهما ينكر ذلك. كذا في «الهداية».
If a man declares one of his two slaves free, then both are injured, and he specifies one of them, then the arsh (compensation) for both belongs to the master. If a man kills both, the diya of a free man and the value of a slave are obligatory. If a man kills each, the value of two slaves is obligatory. That is, he said to his two slaves: 'One of you is free.' Then both were injured, and the master clarified that he meant this specific one. The arsh for both goes to the master, because it is known that the clarification is a manifestation in one sense and a creation in another sense, and after the injury there remains a place for creation, so it is considered a creation, as if he freed at the time of clarification. He said: 'obligatory...' etc.: That is, the diya of a free man is due to the heirs, and the value of a slave to the master. He said: 'the value of the two slaves...' etc.: That is, if a man kills both simultaneously, the value of the two slaves is obligatory, because we are not certain that either was free, and each denies it. It is said in al-Kifayah: This is if he killed both simultaneously and it is not known which was killed first. As for if two men killed them, if they killed sequentially, then the first killer owes the value of the first to his master, and the second killer owes the diya of a free man to his heirs, because the freeing is confirmed. And if they killed simultaneously, then each killer owes the value of a slave, because each killer killed one specific slave, and the freeing in regard to the specific one is as if not effective, so each is a specific slave, and the freeing applies only to the denied one, and we are not certain that either killer killed that denied one, so each owes only the amount that is certain, which is the value. He said: 'the arsh for both belongs to the master...' etc.: The difference is that the clarification is a creation in one sense and a manifestation in another sense, as known in usul al-fiqh. After the injury, there is still room for clarification, so it is considered a creation regarding both. After death, there is no room for clarification, so we consider it a pure manifestation. One of them is certainly free, so the value of a slave and the diya of a free man are obligatory. This is different from the third case, where the value of two slaves is obligatory, because we are not certain that either was free, and each denies that. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah discusses a ruling from the chapter on diyat (blood money). The case: a man has two slaves and says 'one of you is free'. Before he specifies which one, both slaves sustain wounds (shajja). Then the master specifies one. The question: who receives the arsh (compensation for wounds)? The arsh for both wounds goes to the master because the specification (bayan) is considered a creation (insha) of freedom at the moment of specification, not a retroactive manifestation (izhar). After the wound, the master's statement is effective as a new act of freeing. If a third party kills both slaves after the specification, then the killer must pay diyat of a free man (for the freed slave) and the value of a slave (for the other). If one man kills both simultaneously, he must pay the value of two slaves because we are uncertain which was free at the time of death; each is presumed a slave. The text elaborates on scenarios with two killers and sequential versus simultaneous killing, always applying the principle that certainty (yaqin) determines the obligation. The key legal principle: bayan (clarification) after the event is considered insha (creation), not izhar (manifestation), which affects the status of the slaves at the time of the homicide.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for wounds (jirah) that do not reach the level of full diya.
دية (diya): Blood money paid for homicide (killing) of a free person.
بيان (bayan): Clarification or specification of an ambiguous statement; here, identifying which slave was meant to be free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'one of you is free' to his two slaves, both are killed simultaneously by one man, and the master had not specified which slave he meant, what is the killer liable for?
A) Diya of a free man only.
B) Value of two slaves only.
C) Diya of a free man and value of a slave.
D) Nothing, because the slaves are jointly liable.

Key Takeaway
Bayan after an injury or killing is treated as insha (creation), not izhar (manifestation), so the slave is considered free only from the moment of specification, affecting liability for wounds and homicide.

Master's Options for a Blinded Slave: Balancing Property and Humanity
Dispute on master's options when a slave's eyes are gouged

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions (Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn, Shafi'i) on the master's options when a slave's eyes are gouged, explain the dalil for each, and evaluate the Hanafi tarjih based on the dual nature of slaves.

Core Idea
A slave is both property and human: the master cannot both keep the slave and claim compensation for the defect.

When a slave's eyes are gouged out, who gets what? The master, the criminal, and the slave itself are caught in a legal tension between property law and human dignity. This lesson unpacks the three-school dispute (Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn, Shafi'i) and reveals how Abu Hanifa's position uniquely respects both aspects of the slave's nature, giving the master two stark alternatives: hand over the slave for full value or keep him with nothing.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the two options the master has according to Abu Hanifa: hand over the slave and take full value, or keep the slave and take nothing. Note the absence of a middle option.
This is the 'Hanafi position' in the madhhab dispute map.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's additional option
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad grant the master a third option: keep the slave and claim the decrease in value. Understand their analogy to a torn garment.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Identify al-Shafi'i's position
Al-Shafi'i holds that the master must keep the slave and the criminal pays full value. This treats the slave like a free man injured, ignoring the property aspect.
Cross-madhhab dispute.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opponents
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the slave has two 'shabah' (resemblances) – to property (maliyyah) and to a free human (adamiyyah). Each option respects one shabah. The Sahibayn's view neglects adamiyyah; Shafi'i's neglects maliyyah.
The key is 'al-‘amal bi al-shabahayn'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence under each view regarding the master's ability to recover compensation while retaining the slave.
Under Abu Hanifa, no middle ground; under Sahibayn, middle ground exists; under Shafi'i, no option for the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فقء عيني عبد دَفَعَه سيده وأخذ قيمته، أو أمسكه بلا أخذ النقصان (وفي فقء عيني عبد دَفَعَه سيده وأخذ قيمته، أو أمسكه بلا أخذ النقصان) : أي إن شاء السيد دفع العبد إلى الجاني، وأخذ القيمة ، وإن شاء أمسكه بلا أخذ النقصان، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وقالا : يخير بين الدفع والإمساك مع النقصان، وقال الشافعي : ضمنه القيمة وأمسك الجنة العمياء، فإنه يجعل الضمان في مقابلة الفائت فبقي الباقي على ملكه ، كما إذا فقـاً إحدى عينيه ، وقالا : المالية معتبرة في حق الأطراف، وإنما سقطت في حق الذَّاتِ فقط ، وحكم الأموال ما ذكرنا ، كما في الخرق الفاحش " ، وقال أبو حنيفة له : المالية إن كانت معتبرة فالآدمية غير مهدرة ) ، فالعمل بالشبهين أوجب ما ذكرنا. [ 1 ] قوله : ما ذكرنا ... الخ ؛ إي إن شاء أمسك المال وأخذ ما نقصه، وإن شاء دفع المال وأخذ قيمته. [٢]قوله : كما في الخرق الفاحش ؛ أي من خرق ثوب غيره خرقاً فاحشاً : إن شاء المالك دفع ثوبه إليه وضمنه قيمته ، وإن شاء أمسك الثوبَ وضمنه النقصان. [٣] قوله : فالآدمية غير مهدرة ؛ أي في الذات، وفي الأطراف أيضاً، ألا ترى أنّ عبداً لو قطع يد عبد آخر يؤمرُ المولى بالدفع أو الفداء ، وهذا من أحكام الآدمية ؛ لأنَّ موجب الجناية على المال أن يباع رقبته فيها، ثمّ من أحكام الآدمية أن لا تنقسم على الأجزاء، ولا يتملك الجنّة. ومن أحكام المالية أن ينقسم ويتملك ألفاً في الجنة، فالعمل بالشبهين أوجب ما ذكرناه، يعني بالنظر إلى الآدمية ينبغي أن لا يجب الضمان متوزّعاً، بل بإزاء الفائت لا غير، وبالنظر إلى المالية ليس له أن يأخذ كلَّ بدل العين من إمساك الجنّة، كما أنه ليس له ذلك في المال، وفيما قال إلغاء لجانب الآدمية، حيث جعلاه كالثوب المخروق. وفيما قاله الشافعي هل إلغاء الجانب المالية أصلاً ، حيث جعله كحر، فقئ عيناه ، فوفرنا على الشبهين حظهما وقلنا : إن شاء المولى رفع عبده وأخذ قيمته نظراً إلى المالية، وإن شاء أمسكه ولا شيء له نظراً الى الآدمية. كذا في الجلبي ) ناقلا «العناية».
And regarding the gouging of the eyes of a slave: the master may hand him over and take his value, or keep him without taking (compensation for) the decrease. That is: If the master wishes, he may hand the slave over to the criminal and take the full value, and if he wishes, he may keep him without taking the decrease. This is the view of Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He may choose between handing over and keeping while taking the decrease. Al-Shafi'i said: The criminal must pay the full value and the master keeps the blind eye (the slave), because he considers the compensation in exchange for what is lost, so what remains remains his property, as when one eye is gouged. The two said: The financial aspect is considered with respect to the limbs, but it is only disregarded with respect to the person, and the rule for property is as we mentioned, like the case of a large tear. Abu Hanifa said: If the financial aspect is considered, then the human aspect is not neglected. So giving due weight to both aspects necessitates what we mentioned. He said: 'what we mentioned...' etc.: That is, if he wishes, he may keep the property and take the amount of decrease, and if he wishes, he may hand over the property and take its value. He said: 'as in the large tear': meaning, if someone tears another's garment with a large tear, the owner may hand over the garment and claim its full value, or keep the garment and claim the decrease. He said: 'the human aspect is not neglected': meaning, in the person and also in the limbs. Do you not see that if a slave cuts off the hand of another slave, the master is ordered to either hand over or ransom? This is from the rulings of humanity, because the consequence of a crime against property is that the slave's (criminal's) neck may be sold, and from the rulings of humanity is that it cannot be divided into parts nor can the criminal own it (the punishment?). And from the rulings of property is that it can be divided and the criminal can own a thousand (parts?) in exchange. So giving due weight to both aspects necessitates what we mentioned: meaning, from the perspective of humanity, compensation should not be distributed, but only for what is lost; and from the perspective of property, he cannot take the full value while keeping the property, as is the case with property. What they (the two) said neglects the human aspect, making it like a torn garment. And what al-Shafi'i said neglects the property aspect entirely, making it like a free man whose eyes are gouged. So we gave both aspects their due and said: If the master wishes, he may hand over his slave and take his value, considering the property aspect; and if he wishes, he may keep him and take nothing, considering the human aspect. Thus in al-Jalabi, quoting al-‘Inayah.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage presents a three-way dispute regarding compensation when a slave's eyes are gouged out. It begins by stating Abu Hanifa's position: the master has two options—either hand over the slave to the criminal and collect the full market value (qimah), or keep the slave and collect nothing for the loss in value. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they give the master the choice between handing over and taking the full value, or keeping the slave and taking the amount of the decrease (nuqsan). Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the criminal must pay the full value and the master must keep the now-blind slave, treating the compensation as a replacement for the lost property. The commentary explains the reasoning behind these positions. Abu Hanifa's view balances the dual nature of a slave: as property (maliyyah), the master can take the full value, but as a human being (adamiyyah), the master cannot treat the slave like a torn garment and take both the slave and compensation for the defect. Thus, the master must choose either to hand over the slave (property aspect) or keep him (human aspect). The Sahibayn's view is likened to a torn garment where the owner can either hand it over and claim full value or keep it and claim the decrease. Shafi'i's view treats the slave like a free man, ignoring the property aspect entirely. This is a classic example of how Hanafi fiqh reconciles conflicting principles through the concept of 'shabah' (resemblance) and giving each aspect its due weight.

Key Terms
فقء العين (faq' al-'ayn): Gouging out the eye, a specific injury to the slave's eye.
آدمية (adamiyyah): The quality of being human (ensouled) that distinguishes a slave from inanimate property, affecting legal rulings such as inability to divide compensation or treat as pure object.
مالية (maliyyah): The quality of being property/commodity, which allows valuation, transfer, and compensation in monetary terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what options does the master have when his slave's eyes are gouged out?
A) Keep the slave and claim the decrease, or hand over and claim full value.
B) Hand over the slave and take full value, or keep the slave and take nothing.
C) Keep the slave and the criminal pays full value to the master.
D) Hand over the slave and take nothing, or keep the slave and take full value.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ruling on the master's options reflects a careful balance between the slave's dual nature as property and human, giving the master two exclusive alternatives.

Master's Recourse Against Usurper for Mudabbir's Injuries: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa / Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the master's recourse against the usurper after paying diyah for a mudabbir's injuries, and apply the relied-upon position to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
The master's recourse against the usurper depends on which injury occurred in the usurper's possession.

This passage from Husn al-Dirayah expands on a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute involving a mudabbir (a slave with a promise of posthumous freedom) who commits two wrongful injuries (janiyah)—one while usurped by a ghasib and one while with the master. The master must pay the value of the mudabbir, but the question is: can he then recover part of that from the usurper? The answer differs between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one hand and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other, with practical consequences for the master's final liability.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two scenarios
The passage presents two possible orders: (1) injury with the usurper then injury with the master, or (2) injury with the master then injury with the usurper. Identify which injury is first in each scenario.
The order of injuries determines the master's right of recourse.
2
Identify the master's initial liability
In both scenarios, the master is liable for the mudabbir's value (qimah). Determine the amount: it is the lesser of the mudabbir's value and the blood money (arsh) for the injuries.
This is a special rule for mudabbir: the master pays the minimum.
3
Trace the payment to the first guardian
The master recovers half of the value from the usurper (since one injury occurred with him). Then this half is paid to the guardian (wali) of the first injury, not the second. Understand why the second guardian's claim is subordinate.
The right of the second guardian arises only after the first is satisfied.
4
Analyze the dispute on recovery after payment
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow the master to recover the paid half again from the usurper only if the first injury occurred with the usurper. Muhammad holds that the master keeps that half and does not pay it to the first guardian at all. Trace each reasoning.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the master's net recovery differs.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as supported by al-Zayla'i and al-Inayah. Apply this view to new scenarios.
Always check the order of injuries.

Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah
And he recovers half of it from the usurper, and pays to the first (victim's guardian), then in the first (scenario) he recovers it from the usurper, and in the second he does not.
::
And he recovers half of it from the usurper, and pays to the first (victim's guardian), then in the first (scenario) he recovers it from the usurper, and in the second (he does not): meaning a man usurped a mudabbir (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and the mudabbir committed a wrongful injury (janiyah) while with him, then the usurper returned him to the master, and the mudabbir committed another wrongful injury while with the master; or the case was reversed, meaning the mudabbir committed a wrongful injury while with the master, then a man usurped him and the mudabbir committed a wrongful injury while with the usurper. In both scenarios the master is liable for the value (of the mudabbir) due to the two injuries, then he recovers half of it from the usurper, then he pays this half to the guardian of the first injury, not the second, because the right of the (second guardian) did not arise except when there was already a competing claim, so it did not arise. Then after paying, does he recover it from the usurper or not?
And in al-Itqani: It should be that the obligation of the value (qimah) applies when it is less than the arsh (blood money); because the ruling for the mudabbir's injury is that the lesser of the two is binding on the master. This is from Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His saying: "then he recovers..." means the master recovers half of its value from the usurper, because he guaranteed the value due to the two injuries: half of it was due to (the injury) that occurred while it was with the usurper, and the other half due to its existence with him (the master) from the direction of the usurper, so it is as if he did not return half of the slave. This is from Radd al-Muhtar, quoting al-Zayla'i. [2] His saying: "then he pays..." means the master pays this half, i.e., the half taken from the usurper, and this second payment (of the half to the guardian of the first injury) is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) contrary to Muhammad. This is from Radd al-Muhtar. [3] His saying: "because his right did not arise except..." etc. He said in Radd al-Muhtar: meaning the right of the guardian of the second injury. He said in al-Inayah: And for the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): The right of the first (guardian) is to the entire value, because when the injury occurred while in his (the first guardian's) right, no one competes with him; his right is only diminished by the competition of the second. So when he finds something from the substitute of the slave in the possession of the master free (of encumbrances), he takes it to complete his right... etc.
And the full slave (qinn) in both scenarios is like the mudabbir, but the master pays the (full) slave and the value of the mudabbir.
In the first scenario: he recovers; and in the reverse scenario: he does not. This according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: The half of the value that he recovered from the usurper is surrendered to the master and is not paid to the guardian of the first injury; because (the master) has compensated for what the guardian of the first injury took, so it is not paid to him so that the substitute and the substituted do not come together in the ownership of one person.
For the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): The right of the first (guardian) is to the entire value; because when the injury occurred while in his right, no one competes with him; it is only diminished by consideration of the competition of the second. So when he finds something from the substitute of the slave in the hand of the master free, he takes it from him to complete his right. Then when he takes it, the master recovers it from the usurper, because he took it from him due to a cause that was with the usurper. And he does not recover it in the reverse scenario, because the first injury was in the possession of the master.
(And the full slave in both scenarios is like the mudabbir, but the master pays the [full] slave and the value of the mudabbir): meaning if it is objected that this contradicts what preceded that the mudabbir's injury does not obligate except one value, while here it obligated a value and a half. It is answered: That (preceding) is when the multiple injuries occur in the possession of one person, unlike here. Consider. [1] His saying: "he recovers" means the master recovers the half of the value and does not pay it to anyone; because the full amount (of the right) has reached both guardians. This is from Radd al-Muhtar, quoting al-Itqani. [2] His saying: "And in the reverse scenario, no" means the master does not recover from the usurper a second time; because the first injury was in the possession of his master: meaning what the master pays a second time is only due to it, so he does not recover it from anyone, unlike the first issue; because it (the payment) was due to a cause with the usurper, so he recovers from him as previously. This is what al-Zayla'i mentioned. [3] His saying: "he pays the [full] slave" etc. because it is possible to transfer him from one ownership to another, unlike the mudabbir. The apparent meaning is that he is given the choice between ransoming and paying to the two guardians, then if he pays (the slave), he recovers half of his value from the usurper, etc., as mentioned above.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage deals with the case of a mudabbir (a slave who will become free upon the master's death) who commits a wrongful injury (janiyah) while in the hands of a usurper (ghasib) and then after being returned to the master commits another injury, or the reverse order. The master becomes liable for the value of the slave due to the injuries. He recovers half of that value from the usurper because the half corresponds to the injury that occurred while the slave was with the usurper. The master then pays that half to the guardian (wali) of the first injury, not the second, because the second guardian's right arises only when the first is already competing, so it does not materialize (the slave's value is insufficient to cover both). After paying, the question arises: can the master recover that half again from the usurper? The answer depends on which scenario occurred.

In the first scenario (injury first with usurper then with master), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the master can recover the half from the usurper a second time, because the payment was made due to a cause that existed with the usurper (the first injury). In the opposite scenario (injury first with master then with usurper), the master cannot recover from the usurper because the payment was due to the injury that occurred with the master, not the usurper. Muhammad al-Shaybani differs: he says that the half recovered from the usurper is retained by the master and not paid to the first guardian at all. His reasoning is that the master has already compensated the first guardian (by paying the value initially), and paying the half again would lead to the substitute (the half) and the substituted (the right to diyah) being combined in one person (the guardian), which is impermissible. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond: the first guardian's right was to the entire value of the slave, but it was diminished due to the second guardian's claim. Therefore, when the master obtains something from the slave's substitute (the half), it becomes available to complete the first guardian's right, so he takes it. Then the master recovers from the usurper because the cause (the first injury) was with the usurper, and in the reverse case he does not recover because the cause was with the master.

Finally, the passage compares a full slave (qinn) in both scenarios: if a qinn commits similar injuries, the master pays the slave himself (surrenders him) and the value of the mudabbir (in the parallel case). The ruling for the qinn is similar, but the master can actually hand over the slave because the qinn can be transferred to another owner, unlike the mudabbir (who must eventually be freed).

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whom the master has promised to set free upon the master's death (tadbir).
غاصب (ghasib): One who wrongfully takes and holds another's property (usurper).
جناية (janiyah): A wrongful act causing harm, particularly bodily injury or homicide requiring diyah or qisas.
ولي الجناية (wali al-janiyah): The guardian (heir) of the victim who has the right to claim diyah or qisas.
قن (qinn): A full slave (not mudabbir, not mukatab, etc.) who is the absolute property of the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, in which scenario does the master recover the half from the usurper after paying it to the first guardian?
A) When the first injury occurred while the slave was with the master.
B) When the first injury occurred while the slave was with the usurper.
C) In both scenarios.
D) In neither scenario.

Key Takeaway
The master's right to recover from the usurper for the mudabbir's diyah depends on whether the first injury occurred with the usurper; the relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Liability for a Mudabbar When Ghasb and Jinaaya Repeat
Ruling with proof on mudabbar's crimes during ghasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a mudabbar misappropriated twice and explain the master's liability, recovery mechanism, and evaluate the reported intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
Master pays full value of mudabbar, recovers from ghaasib, gives half to first victim, recovers that half again.

This passage from al-Hidayah deals with a specific scenario: a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is promised upon the master's death) is wrongfully taken (ghasb) twice by the same person, and in each period of wrongful possession, the mudabbar commits a crime causing injury. How does liability fall? The master must compensate both victims, but can recover from the misappropriator because the crimes occurred while in his wrongful possession. There is also a subtle intra-Hanafi difference regarding whether the master must give the recovered amount to the first victim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and scenario
Note that the mudabbar is misappropriated (ghasb) twice by the same person (e.g., Zayd), and in each instance the mudabbar commits a crime (jinaaya) causing bodily injury.
The master retains ownership but cannot sell; the ghaasib is the wrongful possessor.
2
Determine the master's liability
The master is liable for the full value of the mudabbar to be divided equally between the two victims because the mudabbar's tadbir (promise of freedom) restricts the master's right to dispose of the slave, making the value owed.
Value here is the market price minus the consideration of freedom (typically half price).
3
Trace the recovery mechanism
The master pays the full value, then recovers the entire amount from the ghaasib because both crimes occurred in the ghaasib's possession. Then he gives half of that amount to the first victim and recovers that half again from the ghaasib before paying the first victim.
The master can recover from the ghaasib before actually disbursing the half to the first victim, ensuring he does not bear a net loss.
4
Examine the reported intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars report a difference from Imam Muhammad, where the master would keep the recovered amount and not give to the first victim. However, the correct view is that there is no difference; Imam Muhammad included this case in al-Jami' al-Saghir without dissent. The author of al-Hidayah supports the agreed view.
The dispute hinges on whether the first victim's claim is overridden by the master's recovery from the ghaasib.
5
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The master's liability is based on the principle of tadbir preventing full ownership (man' al-raqaba). The ghaasib's liability arises because the crimes occurred in his wrongful possession (al-jinaaya 'inda al-ghaasib).
This is a form of qiyas and istihsan; the master does not bear the ultimate loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مدبَّرٌ غُصب مرتين، فجني في كلِّ مرَّة ، ضَمِنَ سيده قيمة لهما، ورجع بقيمته على الغاصب، ودفع نصفها إلى الأوّل، ورجع به على الغاصب. قال: أي مُدبَّرٌ غصبه زيد مرة فجنى عنده ، ثم ردَّه على المالك ، ثم غصبه فجنى عنده ، فعلى المالك قيمته بينهما نصفين ؛ لأنه منع رقبة واحدة بالتدبير فيجب عليه قيمته ، ثم يرجع بتلك القيمة على الغاصب ؛ لأنَّ الجنايتين كانتا عنده ، فيدفع نصفها إلى الأول، ويرجع بهِ على الغاصب قبل دفع النّصف إلى الأول، وهذا متفق عليه ، وقيل : فيه خلافُ محمد كما في تلك المسألة. [١] قوله : وقيل فيه خلاف محمد الله ؛ يعني قال بعض المشايخ في هذه المسألة خلاف محمد الله أيضاً كما في المسألة الأولى، حتى يسلّم للمولى ما رجع به من القيمة على الغاصب، ولا يأخذ وليّ الجناية الأولى ما بقي من حقه. وقيل : على الاتفاق، ويأخذُ وليّ الجناية الأولى تمام حقه وهو نصف القيمة من المولى إذا رجع على الغاصب قيل : هذا هو الصحيح ؛ لأنَّ محمداً لله ذكر هذه المسألة في «الجامع الصغير بلا خلاف كذا ذكره فخر الإسلام الله له في شرحه فعلى هذا يحتاج محمد ه إلى الفرق بين المسألتين، وقد ذكره صاحب الهداية»
A mudabbar was misappropriated twice, and he committed a crime in each instance. His master was liable for a value for both, and he recovered its value from the misappropriator, and he paid half of it to the first (victim), and recovered it from the misappropriator. He said: meaning, a mudabbar was misappropriated by Zayd once and committed a crime in his possession, then he returned him to the owner, then he misappropriated him and he committed a crime in his possession. So the owner owes his value between them in halves, because he prevented one neck by tadbir, so his value is due from him. Then he recovers that value from the misappropriator because the two crimes occurred in his possession. So he pays half to the first, and recovers it from the misappropriator before paying the half to the first. This is agreed upon, and it is said there is a difference of opinion from Muhammad as in that issue. [1] His statement: and it is said there is a difference from Muhammad, meaning some shaykhs said that there is a difference from Muhammad in this issue as in the first issue, such that he delivers to the master what he recovered of the value from the misappropriator, and the guardian of the first crime does not take what remains of his right. And it is said it is agreed upon, and the guardian of the first crime takes his full right which is half of the value from the master when he recovers from the misappropriator. It is said: this is correct, because Muhammad mentioned this issue in al-Jami' al-Saghir without difference. As Fakhr al-Islam mentioned in his commentary. Accordingly, Muhammad needs to differentiate between the two issues, and the author of al-Hidayah mentioned it.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) who is wrongfully taken (ghasb) twice by the same person. In each instance of wrongful possession, the mudabbar commits a crime (jinaayah) causing injury to someone. The master must pay the entire value of the mudabbar for both victims, then recover that full value from the misappropriator (ghaasib). He then pays half of that value to the first victim and recovers that half from the ghaasib before paying the first victim. This is the agreed-upon ruling from the fuqahaa, though a report suggests Imam Muhammad had a different view, similar to the earlier dispute about the regular slave (qin). According to that minority view, the master would keep the recovered amount and not give it to the first victim. However, the correct view according to the commentators is that there is no difference from Imam Muhammad here, citing his inclusion of this case in al-Jami' al-Saghir without any dissent. The passage emphasizes that the master's liability arises because the mudabbar's value is owed due to the prevention of his full freedom (tadbir). The recovery from the ghaasib is because the crimes occurred while the maddubar was in his wrongful possession.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death; his value is limited to his market price minus the consideration of freedom.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation of property; the misappropriator is liable for damages caused during his possession.
جناية (jinaaya): A crime causing bodily harm or injury; here it triggers compensation (diyah or arsh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He keeps the value and does not give anything to the victims.
B) He recovers the full value from the ghaasib, then gives half to the first victim, and recovers that half from the ghaasib.
C) He gives the full value to the second victim only.
D) He divides the value equally among all victims and takes nothing from the ghaasib.

Key Takeaway
The master pays the full value of the mudabbar for both crimes, recovers from the misappropriator, and must give half to the first victim, recovering that half as well.

Ghasb of a Free Child: Liability for Death
States the ruling for liability when a free child dies in the possession of a ghāsib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on ghasb of a free child to distinguish between natural and external causes of death, and explain the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
A ghāsib is not liable for natural death of a free child; the child's 'āqilah pays diyah for external death.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling on the death of a free child wrongfully held (ghasb). The passage distinguishes between natural death (sudden or fever) and external death (lightning or snake bite), assigning liability only in the latter case to the child's 'āqilah. It also introduces the legal concept of istihsan (juristic preference) which overrides the strict qiyas that would deny any liability. Understanding this ruling helps you see how Hanafi jurists balance textual principles with equitable considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios of death
The passage describes two categories: death from natural causes (sudden or fever) and death from external causes (lightning strike or snake bite). List them.
Natural causes = faj'ah (sudden) or humma (fever).
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For natural death, the ghāsib is not liable (lam yadman). For external death, the child's 'āqilah must pay the full diyah (diyah). Note: the ghāsib himself is not directly liable; the liability falls on the 'āqilah.
Diyah is blood money paid to the heirs.
3
Identify the opposing view and its basis
The text mentions that the pure qiyas (analogy) would dictate no liability at all, because ghasb cannot be established for free persons. This is the position of Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam al-Shafi'i.
Qiyas: since free persons are not owned, wrongful possession is not legally recognized, so no basis for liability.
4
Understand the Hanafi istihsan argument
The Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) to hold the 'āqilah liable for external death. The reasoning: the child died due to an external cause while in the ghāsib's custody, making it akin to indirect causation (tadil).
This is an expansion probe: the text only references qiyas; the istihsan is implicit in the ruling.
5
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Per the Hanafi view, the 'āqilah pays diyah. Per the qiyas view, no one pays. This difference matters in jurisprudence and actual compensation.
Thamarah al-khilaf: the practical legal effect of the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن غصب صبيا حراً فمات معه فجأة أو بحمى، لم يضمن، وإن مات بصاعقة أو نهش حية ضمن عاقلتُه الدِّية، والقياس أن لا يضمن بالغصب وهو قولُ زفرَ والشَّافعى ) ؛ لأنَّ الغصب في الحر لا يتحقق
And whoever misappropriates a free child and he dies with him suddenly or from fever, he is not liable. And if he dies from a lightning bolt or a snake bite, his 'aqila are liable for the diyah. And the analogy (qiyas) is that he should not be liable due to misappropriation, and that is the statement of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, because misappropriation regarding a free person is not realized.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on ghasb (wrongful possession) of a free child who dies in the gh?sib's custody. The ruling distinguishes between natural death (sudden or fever) and death from an external cause (lightning strike or snake bite). In the first case, the gh?sib bears no liability. In the second case, the child's 'aqila (paternal relations responsible for paying blood money) must pay the full diyah. The text then notes that strict analogy (qiyas) would dictate no liability at all, because ghasb of a free person is not legally recognized—free persons are not property. This is the position of Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi school, however, adopts istihsan (juristic preference) in making the 'aqila liable for external causes, since the child died under circumstances that implicate the gh?sib's custody, even though he is not directly responsible. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing textual evidence, qiyas, and considerations of equity.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation of property; here applied to a free person analogically but technically not applicable to free persons.
عاقلة (‘āqilah): The group of paternal relatives (or tribe) who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a person who commits manslaughter.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of a victim of manslaughter or homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The ghāsib directly.
B) The child's 'āqilah.
C) No one, because ghasb of a free person is not recognized.
D) The child's father.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for the death of a free child wrongfully held depends on the cause: natural death yields no liability, external death obligates the child's 'āqilah to pay diyah, based on istihsan overriding qiyas.

Liability for Death of a Free Child Wrongfully Possessed
Ruling with proof on liability for death of a wrongfully possessed free child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a wrongdoer's ʿāqilah is liable for the death of a free child and distinguish it from cases where no liability arises.

Core Idea
Liability for death during ghasb arises from transgressive causation (tasbīb), not from the ghasb itself.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi law concerning the death of a free child while wrongfully possessed. The general principle is that ghasb of a free person is not recognized, yet the wrongdoer may still be liable for the child's death if it results from a location-specific danger caused by the wrongful transfer. We will explore the Hanafi proof (istihsan), the opposing view (qiyas), and the practical conditions that determine liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the baseline ruling
State the act: wrongful possession (ghasb) of a free child. Note the baseline: no liability for sudden death or fever. Then identify the exception: liability for death by lightning or snakebite.
The ruling distinguishes between location-specific and non-location-specific causes.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof: istihsan
The Hanafi position relies on istihsan (juristic preference) rather than qiyas. The wrongdoer is liable not for the ghasb but for the causative destruction (itlāf tasbīban) by transferring the child to a dangerous place.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here because of the transgressive element.
3
Identify the conditions for liability
The transfer must be to a place where lightning, snakes, or predators are present, and the death must be from those specific dangers. The wrongdoer must have acted transgressively (mutʿaddī) and removed the guardian's protection.
This is analogous to digging a pit on a road: the condition of the cause takes the place of the cause.
4
Apply the distinction: location-specific vs. non-specific causes
Sudden death and fever are not location-specific, so no liability. But if the child dies from a cause that varies by location (lightning, snakebite), liability falls on the ʿāqilah. Even if fever is prevalent in the new location, liability may apply.
The key is whether the risk is inherent to the location.
5
Examine intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad distinguish between an entrusted slave and entrusted money; Abū Yūsuf holds liability in both. This affects when a child (or slave) can be held liable for property destruction.
The dispute does not affect the death liability, which all Hanafis agree upon regarding causation.

Source Text

And whoever wrongfully possesses a free child and the child dies with him suddenly or from fever, he is not liable. But if he dies from a lightning bolt or snakebite, his ʿāqilah is liable for the blood money. The analogy is that he is not liable for the wrongful possession itself, and that is the saying of Zufar and al-Shāfiʿī, because wrongful possession of a free person is not established, analogous to a child who is entrusted with a slave and kills him: if he destroys wealth without entrustment he is liable, but if he destroys after entrustment he is not. The basis of istiḥsān is that he is not liable for the wrongful possession but for causing destruction by transferring him to a place where lightning or snakes occur. (As in a child entrusted with a slave who kills him, for if he destroys wealth without entrustment he is liable, and if he destroys after entrustment he is not.) The verb awdaʿa is transitive to two objects: you say 'I entrusted Zayd with a dirham.' So the passive verb [awdaʿa] is attributed to the first object, which is the child, so the deposit is with him. [Regarding] his statement: 'to a place where lightning or snakes occur': that is because lightning, snakes, and predators are not in every place, so it is possible to protect [the child] from them. So if he transfers him there while he is transgressing in that transfer, and he has removed the guardian's protection, it is attributed to him because the condition of the cause takes the place of the cause when there is transgression, like digging in the road. Unlike sudden death or fever because that does not vary by location; indeed, if he transfers him to a place where fever and diseases are prevalent, we say that he is liable, so the blood money is obligatory upon the ʿāqilah of the wrongdoer because it is causative killing. This is in al-Hidāyah. [Footnote 2:] That is, the ʿāqilah of the wrongdoer is liable just as the ʿāqilah of the child is liable if he kills the entrusted slave, and he is not liable if the child destroys the entrusted property. Here there are four possibilities: the thing destroyed is either entrusted to the child or not entrusted, and this thing is either a slave or other. The author did not mention the ruling of a slave not entrusted due to its obviousness, which is liability. And the difference between an entrusted slave and entrusted money according to Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad. Abū Yūsuf and al-Shāfiʿī said: the entrusted child is liable in both cases. According to this, if a slave who is interdicted is entrusted with money and the child consumes it, the child is not taken for liability immediately according to Abū Ḥanīfah, but is taken after emancipation. According to Abū Yūsuf and al-Shāfiʿī, he is taken immediately. Similarly is the difference in acknowledgment (iqrār) regarding a slave and a child, and likewise in return. Then Muḥammad stipulated in al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr that the child be sane, and in al-Jāmiʿ al-Kabīr he placed the issue for a child of twelve years.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the liability of a person who wrongfully possesses (ghasb) a free child. If the child dies suddenly or from a common fever while with the wrongdoer, the wrongdoer is not liable for his death. But if the child dies from a lightning strike or snakebite, the wrongdoer's ʿāqilah (tribal guarantors) is liable for the blood money (diyah). The text says that by analogy (qiyās), there should be no liability because the concept of ghasb does not apply to free persons. This is the view of Zufar and al-Shāfiʿī. However, the Hanafi position relies on istiḥsān (juristic preference): the wrongdoer is liable not for the ghasb itself but for causing the death by transferring the child to a dangerous place. The conditions for liability are clarified: the wrongdoer must have been transgressing by moving the child to a location where such dangers are present, and thereby removing the guardian's protection. Sudden death or fever are not location-dependent, so no liability. The commentary uses an analogy: a child (sabī) who is entrusted with a slave and kills him. If the slave is not entrusted, the child is liable; if entrusted, he is not. Similarly, the ʿāqilah of the wrongdoer is liable here. The text also addresses a dispute among Hanafi imams: Abū Ḥanīfah and Muḥammad distinguish between an entrusted slave and an entrusted property (money), while Abū Yūsuf and al-Shāfiʿī hold the child liable in both cases. Further differences emerge regarding when the child is taken for payment. Muḥammad requires that the child be sane and at least twelve years old for liability.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The group of male relatives (tribe or clan) who are liable to pay diyah for accidental or quasi-intentional killing.
تسبيب (tasbīb): Causation that is indirect; the act is not the direct cause but is connected through a chain.
إيداع (īdāʿ): The act of entrusting property (wadīʿah) to someone for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why is the wrongdoer liable for the death of a free child from a snakebite but not from a fever?
A) Because fever is a natural cause, while snakebite is caused by the new location
B) Because the child was free, so no liability applies
C) Because the wrongdoer acted with intent in both cases
D) Because fever is not considered a cause of death

Key Takeaway
The ʿāqilah is liable for diyah when a wrongfully possessed free child dies from a location-specific danger (lightning, snakebite) caused by transgressive transfer, but not from non-specific causes like fever.

Liability of a Minor for Destroying a Deposit: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on minor's liability for destroying deposited property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a minor's liability for destroying a deposit and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad from Abu Yusuf/Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A minor is not liable for destroying a deposit unless it is a slave or he was authorized; the owner forfeits inviolability by depositing with a child.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a minor who destroys a deposited item (other than a slave) owes compensation. The disagreement hinges on the nature of inviolability ('isma) and the minor's legal capacity. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze how ownership, guardianship, and contractual handover interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for a slave deposit
The passage begins by stating that if the deposit is a slave, the minor is liable for killing it by consensus. Note that the blood money is on the minor's aqila (agnatic relatives). This is the baseline case.
Slave has inherent inviolability like a free person's life.
2
State the dispute for non-slave property
For other property, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold no liability; Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold immediate liability. This is the core ikhtilaf.
The school's mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
3
Analyze the opposing proof (Abu Yusuf/Shafi'i)
They argue that the minor destroyed property that is inviolable (ma'sum) in right of the owner, so compensation is obligatory, just as if the minor was authorized or if the deposit was a slave.
This is a qiyas based on the owner's right.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (Qulna)
The Hanafi response explains that for non-slave property, inviolability is established as a right of the owner. By placing it in the minor's hand (which is not a protective hand), the owner forfeited that inviolability. The minor has no authority over himself to incur liability. Contrast with a slave: the slave's inviolability is inherent, not based on the owner's right.
The key distinction is the source of 'isma: owner's right vs. inherent.
5
Apply the ruling to different scenarios
If the minor was authorized by his guardian (for trade or safekeeping), he is liable by consensus. If he destroyed someone else's property without it being a deposit, he is liable by consensus. The dispute only applies to unauthorized minor deposits of non-slave property.
This shows the subtlety of 'isma and wilaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If it is a slave, he is liable for killing it, and if it is other property, he is not liable according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but he is liable according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i because he destroyed inviolable property. We said: Other than a slave is inviolable due to the owner's right, and he has lost it by placing it in the hand of the minor. As for the slave, its inviolability is due to his own right, for he is retained on original freedom regarding blood. Evidence that the non-rational (minor) is liable by agreement; and because authorization is not considered in him, but his act is considered. Thus in al-Takmilah. [1] His saying: he is liable for killing; meaning the blood money is on the minor's aqila by consensus. Thus in al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: because he destroyed property; meaning valuable inviolable property as a right of the owner, so compensation is obligatory upon him, as if the deposit were a slave, or if the minor was authorized in trade or for safekeeping by the guardian, and likewise if he destroyed other than what was in his hand and it was not inviolable due to established authority of consumption. [3] His saying: we said; its clarification: The minor destroyed non-inviolable property, so he is not held to compensate it, as if he destroyed it with its permission and consent; and this is because inviolability is established as a right for him, and he has forfeited it by placing it in a hand that is preventing, so it remains inviolable only if he appoints someone else in his place for safekeeping, and there is no such appointment here; because he has no authority over the minor to oblige him, nor does the minor have authority over himself to undertake, unlike the authorized one; because he has authority over himself like an adult. And unlike when the deposit is a slave; because his inviolability is due to his own right, or he is retained on original freedom regarding blood, so his inviolability is for his own right, not for the owner; because the owner's inviolability is only considered in what he has authority to consume, so that he can enable another to consume by authorization, and the master does not have authority to consume his slave, so he cannot enable another to do so, so his authorization is not considered, and the minor is liable for consuming it, unlike other properties. (1) Takmilat al-Bahr (8:445). (2) Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p.615). He said in al-'Inayah: If the minor consumes (the deposit), it is considered: if he was authorized to trade, or if he was under interdiction but he accepted the deposit with his guardian's permission, he is liable by consensus; if he was under interdiction and accepted without his guardian's order, then there is no liability upon him according to the Imam and Muhammad, neither at present nor after attainment of maturity. Abu Yusuf said: He is liable at present. And they are unanimous that if he consumed the property of another without having a deposit with him, he is liable at present. This is a good division, thus in al-Takmilah (2).
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability of a minor who destroys a deposited item (wadi'ah). The discussion is from the commentary on the Hidayah, likely from the Book of Diyat. The author first addresses the case where the deposited item is a slave. If the minor kills the slave, he is liable, and the blood money is on his aqila by consensus. If the deposit is other property, the minor is not liable according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, but he is liable according to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam al-Shafi'i. The reasoning given: Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue that the minor destroyed property that is inviolable (ma'sum) in right of the owner, so compensation is due. The Hanafi response (Qulna) explains that the inviolability of non-slave property is due to the owner's right, but the owner forfeited that inviolability by placing it in the minor's hand, because the minor's hand is not protective. As for the slave, his inviolability is inherent to himself (like a free person's blood), so the owner's act of depositing cannot remove it; hence the minor is liable. The commentary then clarifies that if the minor was authorized by his guardian (either for trade or for safekeeping), he is liable by consensus. If he was under interdiction and accepted the deposit without guardian permission, then the ruling is as above: no liability according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but liability according to Abu Yusuf. Finally, if the minor destroys someone else's property without it being a deposit, he is liable by consensus. This shows the subtlety of 'isma (inviolability) and wilaya (authority) in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty, being under guardianship.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A deposit; property entrusted to someone for safekeeping without compensation.
العصمة (al-'isma): Inviolability; legal protection of property or life that makes its violation compensable.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to compensate for damage or destruction of property.
التسليط (al-taslit): Authorization to use or consume property; giving someone control over it.
الولاية (al-wilaya): Legal authority over oneself or others; here refers to the minor's lack of authority over himself to consent to liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if an unauthorized minor destroys a deposited item that is not a slave, what is the ruling?
A) The minor is liable immediately.
B) The minor is liable after reaching puberty.
C) The minor is not liable at all.
D) The minor is liable only if the deposit was a slave.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that an unauthorized minor is not liable for destroying a deposit except a slave, due to the owner's forfeiture of inviolability.

Diyat — Blood Money

Definition and Ruling of Qasama in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines Qasama, its ruling, conditions, and evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Qasama, list its conditions and pillar, and explain the ruling when the oaths are completed or refused.

Core Idea
Qasama is a collective oath of fifty men that imposes blood money on a locality to preserve blood inviolability.

Qasama is a unique procedure in Islamic criminal law for cases where a body is found with signs of homicide but the killer is unknown. This lesson unpacks its linguistic and legal definitions, the essential conditions and pillar, and the ruling that follows.

Guided Path
1
Define Qasama linguistically and legally
Start with the linguistic root: qasama is derived from 'aqsama (to swear). The legal definition is a set of oaths sworn by fifty men from a locality where a dead body is found with signs of violent death and the killer is unknown.
Linguistic definition is from al-Mughrib and Miraj al-Diraya.
2
Identify the cause and pillar
The cause (sabab) is the existence of the killing. The pillar (rukn) is the oath itself, administered on the tongue of each of the fifty men, with the specific wording: 'By Allah, I did not kill him and I do not know his killer.'
The oath must be completed to fifty, either by fifty individuals or by repetition.
3
List the conditions for validity
The conditions (shurut) are: the oath-takers must be adult, sane, free; the corpse must be found with a wound or trace of beating/strangulation etc.; and the number of oaths must reach fifty.
If fewer than fifty men swear, the oath is repeated among them until the count reaches fifty.
4
Determine the ruling under different scenarios
If the fifty oaths are completed, the judge rules for the obligation of blood money (diya) on the locality. If the accused refuse to swear, they are imprisoned until they swear if the guardian claims intentional killing; if the guardian claims accidental killing, the judge immediately rules for diya upon refusal.
The ruling varies based on the type of claim by the guardian.
5
Acknowledge the evidence and cross-madhhab difference
The specification of fifty men is proven by famous hadiths (mashhoor). The Hanafi school does not require lawth (strong presumption) unlike the Shafi'i school, making the procedure applicable more broadly.
The difference affects when qasama is triggered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القسامة
میت به جرح أو أثر ضرب أو خنق أو خروج دم من أذنه أو عينه وجد في محلة
قوله : باب القسامة ; لَمَّا كان أمرُ القتيل في بعض الأحوال يؤول إلى القسامة ذكره في آخرِ الدِّيات في باب على حدة، اعلم أنَّ القسامة في اللغة اسم وضع موضع الإقسام، كذا في «المغرب». وقال في «معراج الدراية»: القسامة لغة : مصدر أقسم كما لا يخفى عن من له دراية بعلم الأدب. أما في الشريعة : فهي أيمان يقسم بها أهلُ محلة أو دار وغير ذلك وجد فيها قتيل به جراحة أو أثر ضرب أو خنق، ولا يعلم من قتله، يقسم خمسون رجلاً من أهل المحلة، يقول كل واحدٍ منهم بالله ما قتلته، ولا علمت له قاتلاً.
وسببها وجود القتل كما ذكرنا.
وركنُها : إجراء اليمين على لسان كلّ واحدٍ من الخمسين : بالله ما قتلته ولا علمتُ له قاتلاً كما سيجيء. وشرطها : بلوغ القسم وعقله وحريته، وأن يكون الميت الموجود على الكيفية المذكورة، وتكميلُ اليمين خمسين، فإن لم يبلغ المقسمون هذا العدد، تكرّر عليهم اليمين، حتى يبلغ الخمسين. وحكمها : القضاء بوجوب الدِّيَةِ بعد الحلف، والحبس إلى الحلف إن أبوا إذا ادعى الولي العمد والحكم بالدية عند النكول إن ادعى الولي الخطأ. ومن محاسنها : حفظ الدماء وصيانتها عن الإهدار ، وخلاص من يتهم بالقتل عن القصاص، وتعيين الخمسين ثبت بالأحاديث المشهورة. كذا في مجمع الأنهر
Chapter of Qasama. A dead person found in a locality with a wound or trace of beating or strangulation or blood coming out of his ear or eye. Explanation: The chapter of Qasama. Since the matter of the slain in some circumstances leads to Qasama, he mentioned it at the end of diat in a separate chapter. Know that Qasama linguistically is a noun placed in the position of swearing, as in al-Mughrib. And it is said in Miraj al-Diraya: Qasama linguistically is a verbal noun of 'aqsama, as is not hidden from one who has knowledge of literature. As for in Sharia: it is oaths sworn by the people of a locality or house etc. where a slain person is found with a wound or trace of beating or strangulation, and his killer is not known. Fifty men from the locality swear, each one of them says: By Allah, I did not kill him, and I do not know his killer. Its cause: the existence of the killing as we mentioned. Its pillar: administering the oath upon the tongue of each of the fifty: By Allah, I did not kill him and I do not know his killer, as will come. Its conditions: that the oath-takers be adult, sane, free, and that the dead person found be as described, and completion of the oath fifty. If the oath-takers do not reach this number, the oath is repeated upon them until it reaches fifty. Its ruling: the judge rules for the obligation of the blood money after the oath, and imprisonment until the oath if they refuse if the guardian claims intentional killing, and the ruling of blood money upon refusal if the guardian claims accidental killing. Among its benefits: preserving blood and protecting it from being wasted, and freeing the accused from retaliation. And the specification of fifty is proven by famous hadiths. End quote from Majma' al-Anhur.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Qasama in the Hanafi law of retaliation (diyat). The commentator first explains why this topic is placed at the end of the book: because it deals with an exceptional situation where the killer is unknown. He then gives a linguistic definition of qasama, noting that it derives from 'aqsama (to swear), and a legal definition: it is a set of oaths sworn by the residents of a locality where a body is found with signs of violent death but no known perpetrator. The cause (sabab) for qasama is the very existence of the killing. The pillar (rukn) is the oath itself, spoken by each of the fifty men. The conditions (shurut) include the capacity of the swearers (adulthood, sanity, freedom), the specific condition of the corpse, and completing the number fifty by repetition if necessary. The ruling (hukm) is that after the oaths, the judge imposes the blood money (diya) on the locality; if they refuse to swear, they are imprisoned until they do, and if they continue to refuse, the judge rules for diya. The wisdom (mahasin) is to preserve blood inviolability and protect the accused from false retaliation. The number fifty is established by well-known hadiths, indicating the authority of this ruling.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasama): In Sharia: oaths sworn by fifty men from a locality where a dead body with signs of homicide is found, each swearing he did not kill and does not know the killer.
اليمين (al-yamin): The oath sworn by each of the fifty men, phrased as 'By Allah, I did not kill him and I do not know his killer.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The oath is invalid and the case is dropped.
B) The oath is repeated among the available swearers until the number fifty is reached.
C) The heir may demand the oath from the remaining men on behalf of others.
D) The judge may reduce the required number to thirty.

Key Takeaway
Qasama is a wajib collective oath of fifty men that imposes diya on a locality, with specific conditions and scenarios, proven by famous hadiths.
