Conditions of the Corpse and Oath Wording in Qasama
Specifies corpse condition and oath wording in Qasama

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acceptable conditions of the corpse that trigger Qasama and apply the correct plural wording of the oath.

Core Idea
Qasama requires a complete or majority corpse; the oath must be in plural to avoid falsehood in joint killing.

In the previous lesson, we learned the basic scenario of Qasama. This lesson details two critical aspects: what state the corpse must be in for Qasama to be valid, and how each swearer must phrase his oath. The Hanafi school imposes strict conditions on the corpse out of respect for human dignity, and mandates a unique plural wording to cover all possibilities of killing, including joint killing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the corpse conditions
List the acceptable states of the corpse: found complete, body without head, majority of the body, or half with the head. If less than half is found (even with head), Qasama does not apply.
The ruling treats the majority as the whole to honor human dignity.
2
Understand the basis of the ruling
The conditions are based on textual evidence (hadith) and the principle that the majority is given the ruling of the whole.
This is a concession for practicality and respect.
3
Analyze the oath wording
Each of the fifty men swears: 'By Allah, we did not kill him, nor do we know a killer for him.' The plural is used even if swearing alone.
The plural covers the possibility that he killed alone (negating group killing) or with others.
4
Contrast with alternative wording
If one swore 'I did not kill' and killed with others, he would lie because each accomplice is considered a full killer in joint murder.
Thus the plural wording is mandatory to avoid this falsehood.
5
Note the absence of lawth condition
Unlike the Shafi'i school, the Hanafi school does not require lawth (a sign of guilt) for Qasama to be applied.
Lawth is defined as a secret or rancor indicating suspicion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بدنه بلا رأس، أو أكثره، أو نصفه مع رأسهِ لا يعلمُ قاتله، وادعى وليه القتل على أهلها أو بعضهم حُلّف خمسون رجلا منهم، يختارهم الولي : بالله ما قتلناه ، ولا علمنا له قاتلاً ) ، لا الولي ) ، ثمّ قُضي على أهلها بالدية) : أي بديته فالألفُ واللام يقوم مقام ضمير يعود إلى المبتدأ ، وهو ميت، هذا عندنا.
[١] قوله : أو بدنه ; قال أخي جلبيه : عطف على ميت، يعني ميت وجد بجميع أجزائه مكملة، أو وجد بدنه بلا رأس، أو وجد أكثره من أي جانب كان ، أو نصفه مشقوقاً بالطول. وأما إن وجد أقل من نصفه ومع الرأس لا ; لأنّ هذا حكم عرف بالنص، وقد ورد به في البدن، ولكن للأكثر حكم الكل، فأجرينا عليه أحكامه تعظيما للآدمي. كذا في «الهداية». [٢] قوله : ما قتلناه ولا علمنا له قاتلاً ; قال في «الكفاية» : هذا على طريق الحكاية عن الجمع، وأما عند الحلف، فيحلف كلُّ واحدٍ منهم بالله ما قتلناه ; لجواز أن يكون قاتلا وحده ، وينفي بلفظ الجمع أن يكون قاتلا معا الجماعة. إن قيل : يجوز أنه قتل مع غيره، فيجري على يمينه : بالله ما قتلت كما في عكسه. قلنا : لا كذلك ; لأنه إذا حلف بالله ما قتلت، وكان قتل مع غيره كان كاذباً في يمينه ، فإنَّ الجماعة متى قتلوا واحداً يكون كل واحد منهم قاتلاً ; ولهذا يجب القصاص على كلّ واحدٍ منهم في العمد والكفارة في الخطأ.
[٣]قوله : لوث ; يقال : بينهم لوث ; أي سر أو حقد، مأخوذ من قولهم : لوّتَ
Or his body without head, or the majority of it, or half with his head, his killer not known, and his guardian claims killing against its people or some of them, fifty men from them are sworn, chosen by the guardian: By Allah, we did not kill him, nor do we know a killer for him, not the guardian. Then it is judged upon its people for the blood money: meaning his blood money; the alif and lam substitute for a pronoun referring to the subject, which is a dead person; this is according to us. [1] His saying: 'or his body'; said my brother Jalabiyyah: it is a conjunction to 'dead', meaning a dead person found with all his complete parts, or his body found without a head, or most of it from whichever side, or half split lengthwise. But if less than half is found and with the head, no; because this ruling is known by text, and it has been reported regarding the body, but the majority has the ruling of the whole, so we apply its rulings to honor the human. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: 'we did not kill him nor do we know a killer for him'; said in al-Kifayah: this is by way of narration from the group, but when swearing, each one of them swears 'by Allah, we did not kill him'; because it is possible that he killed alone, and he negates with the plural wording that he killed together with the group. If it is said: it is possible that he killed with others, so his oath should be 'by Allah, I did not kill' as in the opposite. We say: not so; because if he swears by Allah 'I did not kill', and he killed with others, he would be lying in his oath, for when a group kills one, each one of them is a killer; hence retaliation is obligatory upon each of them in intentional killing and expiation in accidental killing. [3] His saying: 'lawth'; it is said: between them is lawth, i.e., a secret or rancor, derived from their saying: 'lawwata'.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on Qasama by detailing two specific aspects: the acceptable condition of the corpse and the exact wording of the oath. First, the corpse must be found either complete, or without the head, or with the majority of the body, or half with the head. If less than half is found, even with the head, Qasama does not apply. This is based on textual evidence from hadith, and the ruling treats the majority as the whole out of respect for human dignity. Second, the oath must be phrased in the plural 'we did not kill him' even though each individual swears alone. The reason is that a person might have killed alone, and the plural negation covers the possibility of killing with others without lying. If one swore 'I did not kill' but killed with others, he would be lying because each accomplice is fully considered a killer (as in joint murder). The passage finally defines 'lawth' as a secret or rancor, which is a term used by Imam al-Shafi'i as a requirement for Qasama (though the Hanafi school does not require it). The commentator notes that this is our (Hanafi) position.

Key Terms
اللوث (al-lawth): Defined as 'a secret or rancor' indicating a sign of guilt or strong presumption that the accused killed.
ما قتلناه (ma qatalnahu): The plural oath 'we did not kill him' used by each swearer, even if swearing alone, to cover the possibility of acting alone or with others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) No, because less than half is found.

Key Takeaway
Qasama requires the corpse to be whole, majority, or half with head, and each swearer must use the plural oath to avoid falsehood in cases of joint killing.

Qasamah with Lawth: Hanafi, Shafi'i, and Maliki Positions
Inter-madhhab dispute on oath procedure in qasamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on qasamah with lawth, differentiate it from the Shafi'i and Maliki positions, and identify the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In qasamah, Hanafi swears defendants only; Shafi'i/Maliki swear guardian first, and may impose qisas.

This passage presents the Hanafi procedure for qasamah when a body is found with circumstantial evidence (lawth). It contrasts with the Shafi'i and Maliki schools on who takes the oath and whether retaliation (qisas) is possible. Understanding these differences is crucial for recognizing the operative ruling in Hanafi fatwa and the practical outcomes in such cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the act (qasamah), the conditions (body found whole or most of it, lawth present), and the ruling: fifty men from the accused locality swear, then diyah is imposed on the locality.
The guardian does not swear; the defendants always swear and pay diyah regardless of claim type.
2
Analyze the conditions and oath wording
Note that the passage specifies when the ruling applies (body with head, or most of body, or half with head) and explains the oath wording in plural to deny participation in killing.
The Hanafi view is that the majority takes the ruling of the whole out of respect for the human being.
3
Follow the Shafi'i and Maliki positions
For Shafi'i: if lawth exists, the guardians swear fifty oaths first, then diyah is imposed on the defendants. Malik: if lawth and claim is for intentional killing, qisas; if no lawth, his school resembles Hanafi but with different oath repetition.
The passage mentions two different rulings for Shafi'i (diyah in both cases) and Maliki (qisas possible).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and rebuttal
The Hanafi view holds that the guardian's oath is unnecessary because lawth creates suspicion against the locality, not the guardian. The defendants' oath clears them of direct guilt but diyah remains as collective liability.
The commentator indicates that the disagreement revolves around two points: who swears first and whether the defendants' oath exonerates them fully.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi: locality always pays diyah after swearing; under Shafi'i: guardian swears first, and if he refuses, defendants swear and may go free; under Maliki: qisas possible if intentional claim with lawth.
The practical application of qasamah differs significantly between schools in terms of liability and potential for capital punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات / أنواعها وأحكامها

أو بدنه بلا رأس، أو أكثره، أو نصفه مع رأسهِ لا يعلمُ قاتله، وادعى وليه القتل معي على أهلها أو بعضهم . حُلّفَ خمسون رجلاً منهم ، يختارهم الولي : بالله ما قتلناه ، ولا علمنا له قاتلاً، لا الولي، ثمّ قُضِيَ على أهلها بالدِّية

أو بدنه " بلا رأس أو أكثره، أو نصفه مع رأسهِ لا يعلمُ قاتله، وادعى وليه القتل على أهلها أو بعضهم حُلّف خمسون رجلا منهم، يختارهم الولي : بالله ما قتلناه ، ولا علمنا له قاتلاً ) ، لا الولي ) ، ثمّ قُضي على أهلها بالدية) : أي بديته فالألفُ واللام يقوم مقام ضمير يعود إلى المبتدأ ، وهو ميت، هذا عندنا.

[1] قوله : أو بدنه ؛ قال أخي جلبيه : عطف على ميت، يعني ميت وجد بجميع أجزائه مكملة، أو وجد بدنه بلا رأس، أو وجد أكثره من أي جانب كان ، أو نصفه مشقوقاً بالطول. وأما إن وجد أقل من نصفه ومع الرأس لا ؛ لأنّ هذا حكم عرف بالنص، وقد ورد به في البدن، ولكن للأكثر حكم الكل، فأجرينا عليه أحكامه تعظيما للآدمي. كذا في «الهداية».

[2] قوله : ما قتلناه ولا علمنا له قاتلاً ؛ قال في «الكفاية) : هذا على طريق الحكاية عن الجمع، وأما عند الحلف، فيحلف كلُّ واحدٍ منهم بالله ما قتلناه ؛ لجواز أن يكون قاتلا وحده ، وينفي بلفظ الجمع أن يكون قاتلا معا الجماعة. إن قيل : يجوز أنه قتل مع غيره، فيجري على يمينه : بالله ما قتلت كما في عكسه. قلنا : لا كذلك ؛ لأنه إذا حلف بالله ما قتلت، وكان قتل مع غيره كان كاذباً في يمينه ، فإنَّ الجماعة متى قتلوا واحداً يكون كل واحد منهم فـ قاتلاً : ؛ ولهذا يجب القصاص على كلّ واحدٍ منهم في العمد والكفارة في الخطأ.

[3] قوله : لوث ؛ يقال : بينهم لوث ؛ أي سر أو حقد، مأخوذ من قولهم : لوّتَ

وقال الشافعي : إن كان هناك لوث : أي علامة القتل على واحد بعينه، أو ظاهر يشهد للمدَّعي من عداوة ظاهرة، أو شهادة واحدٍ عدل ، أو جماعة غير عدول بأنَّ أهلَ المحلّة قتلوه استحلف الأولياء خمسين يميناً أنَّ أهل المحلة قتلوه ، ثم يقضي بالدِّيةِ على المدعى عليه سواء كان الدعوى بالعمد أو بالخطأ.

وقال مالك : يقضي بالقَوَدِ إن كان الدعوى بالعمد، وهو أحد قولي الشَّافِعِي ، وإن لم يكن به لوث فمذهبه مثل مذهبنا"، إلا أنه لا يكرر اليمين، بل يردُّها على الولي

الماء : إذا كدره، ولوّث ثيابه بالطين؛ أي لطّخه، فيلوث. كذا في «العناية». [1]قوله : على واحد بعينه ؛ مثل أن يوجد لقربه رجل معه سيف، أو في يده شيء من آلة القتل، وعليه آثار القتل ، فذلك لوث يوجب القسامة لولائه. كذا في «الجلبي» ناقلا عن «العناية».

[2]قوله : أو ظاهر يشهد ؛ أي ظاهر الحال، يشهد المدعي بأن العداوة بين المقتول وأهل محلة ظاهرة.

[3] قوله : مثل مذهبنا ... الخ ؛ أي في بداية يمين المدعي عليه غير أنه إن لم يكمل أهل المحلة خمسين لا تكرّر اليمين عليهم، وفيه نظر؛ لأنّ مذهب مالك والشافعي له ليس كذلك، بل يردّ على الأولياء كما في النكول عنده ، فالاختلاف في موضعين في تحليف المدعي أولاً ، وفي براءة أهل المحلة باليمين.

فالحاصل أنه إذا وجد ظاهر [يشهدا للمدعي عند الشافعي له يحلف المدعي، فإن حلف أنهم قتلوه خطأ، فله الدية، وإن حلف أنهم قتلوه عمداً فعليهم القصاص في قول، والدية في قول، فإن نكل المدعي عن اليمين حلف المدعى عليهم. فإن حلفوا برئوا، ولا شيء عليهم ، وإن نكلوا فعليهم القصاص في قول،
Book of Blood-Money: Its Types and Rulings

Or his body without a head, or most of it, or half of it with his head, whose killer is unknown, and his guardian claims the killing [is] with me upon its people or some of them. Fifty men from among them are sworn in, chosen by the guardian: 'By Allah, we did not kill him, nor do we know of a killer for him,' not the guardian, then the blood-money is adjudged against its people.

Or his body without a head or most of it, or half of it with his head, whose killer is unknown, and his guardian claims the killing upon its people or some of them: fifty men from among them are sworn in, chosen by the guardian: 'By Allah, we did not kill him, nor do we know of a killer for him,' not the guardian, then the blood-money is adjudged against its people: meaning its blood-money, so the definite article stands in place of a pronoun referring back to the subject, and it is the deceased. This is according to us.

[1] His statement: 'or his body'; my brother al-Jalabi said: it is an conjunction to 'deceased', meaning a deceased found complete in all his parts, or his body found without a head, or most of it found from any side, or half of it split lengthwise. As for if less than half is found, even with the head, then no; because this ruling is known by explicit text, and it has been transmitted concerning the body, but the majority takes the ruling of the whole, so we apply its rulings to him out of respect for the human being. Thus in al-Hidayah.

[2] His statement: 'We did not kill him nor do we know of a killer for him'; he said in al-Kifayah: this is by way of narration about the group, but at the time of swearing, each one of them swears 'By Allah, we did not kill him' because it is possible that he killed alone, and he negates with the plural form that he killed with the group. If it is said: it is possible that he killed with another, so he would be swearing 'By Allah, I did not kill' as in the opposite. We say: not so; because if he swears by Allah I did not kill, and he killed with another, he would be lying in his oath, for whenever a group kills one, each of them is a killer, and therefore retribution is obligatory on each of them in intentional killing and expiation in accidental.

[3] His statement: 'lawth'; it is said: between them is lawth, i.e., a secret or rancor, derived from their saying: he sullied the water when he muddied it, and sullied his clothes with mud, i.e., he soiled them, so he sullies. Thus in al-Inayah.

And al-Shafi'i said: If there is lawth, i.e., a sign of murder upon a specific person, or an appearance that testifies for the claimant from manifest enmity, or testimony of one upright witness, or a group of non-upright witnesses that the people of the quarter killed him, he swears the guardians fifty oaths that the people of the quarter killed him, then the blood-money is adjudged against the defendant whether the claim is for intentional or accidental.

And Malik said: He adjudges with retaliation if the claim is for intentional, and it is one of the two sayings of al-Shafi'i. And if there is no lawth, then his school is like our school, except that he does not repeat the oath, but rather returns it to the guardian.

Water: when he makes it muddy, and he sullies his clothes with mud, i.e., he soils them. Thus in al-Inayah. [1] His statement: 'upon a specific person'; such as that found near him a man with a sword, or in his hand something from the tools of murder, and on him traces of murder, so that is lawth that necessitates qasamah for his protector. Thus in al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Inayah.

[2] His statement: 'or an appearance that testifies'; i.e., the apparent situation testifies for the claimant that the enmity between the deceased and the people of the quarter is manifest.

[3] His statement: 'like our school...etc.'; i.e., regarding the beginning of the oath of the defendant, except that if the people of the quarter do not complete fifty, the oath is not repeated upon them. There is consideration in this; because the school of Malik and al-Shafi'i is not so, but rather it is returned to the guardians as in the case of refusal according to him. So the difference is in two places: in swearing the claimant first, and in the exculpation of the people of the quarter by oath.

So the conclusion is that if there is an appearance testifying for the claimant according to al-Shafi'i, the claimant swears. If he swears that they killed him accidentally, he gets blood-money. If he swears that they killed him intentionally, then they are subject to retaliation in one saying, and blood-money in another saying. If the claimant refuses the oath, then the defendants swear. If they swear, they are exonerated and nothing is upon them. If they refuse, then they are subject to retaliation in one saying.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the law of qasamah when a dead body is found with signs of murder (lawth). The Hanafi position is stated first: when a body is found whole, or without a head, or with most of it, or half with the head, and the killer is unknown, and the guardian accuses the people of the locality, fifty men from that locality are chosen by the guardian to swear that they did not kill the victim and do not know his killer. The guardian himself does not swear. Based on their oath, blood-money (diyah) is imposed on the locality. The commentary clarifies the scope: if less than half the body is found (even with the head), the ruling does not apply because the text specifies a majority. The Hanafi rule is that the majority takes the ruling of the whole out of respect for the human being. The wording of the oath is also explained: each man swears in the plural form 'we did not kill' to deny participation with anyone, even if he alone killed, because if a group kills, each is a killer and subject to retribution or expiation. The term 'lawth' is defined as a sign of guilt, such as enmity or weapon evidence.

Then the Shafi'i position is presented: if lawth exists (specific suspicion, manifest enmity, or testimony of one upright or several non-upright witnesses), the guardians (first-degree heirs) swear fifty oaths that the people of the quarter killed him, and then diyah is imposed regardless of whether the claim is for intentional or accidental killing. The commentator notes that Shafi'i also imposes diyah in both cases. The Maliki position is then given: if the claim is for intentional killing and lawth exists, Malik judges retaliation (qisas), which is one of Shafi'i's two opinions. If no lawth, Malik's school agrees with the Hanafi school in substance but differs in that the oath is not repeated; instead, if the defendants do not complete fifty, the oath is returned to the guardian (as in the case of refusal). The commentator points out that the disagreement revolves around two points: first, whether the claimant (guardian) must be sworn initially (Shafi'i and Maliki yes, Hanafi no), and second, whether the defendants' oath suffices to exonerate them (Hanafi yes; if they refuse, they are liable for diyah or qisas according to Shafi'i/Maliki). Thus, the practical outcome differs: under Hanafi, the defendants are always sworn, and if they swear, they pay diyah; under Shafi'i, the guardian swears first, and if he swears, the defendants are liable; if he refuses, the defendants swear and are exonerated; if they also refuse, they are liable for qisas in one opinion.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasāmah): A procedure where fifty oaths are taken from the inhabitants of a locality when a murdered body is found and the killer is unknown; collectively they swear innocence and pay diyah.
لوث (lawth): A sign or circumstantial evidence pointing to guilt, such as personal enmity, possession of a weapon, or testimony of unreliable witnesses.
دية (diyah): Blood-money, a financial compensation paid to the victim's heirs in cases of homicide or bodily injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when a body is found with lawth and the guardian accuses the locality, who takes the fifty oaths?
A) The guardian of the victim
B) Fifty men from the accused locality, chosen by the guardian
C) The judge
D) Fifty witnesses from the locality

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi qasamah places the oath and liability on the accused locality; the guardian does not swear, and diyah is always imposed if the oath is taken.

Qasama in Case of Lawth: Hanafi vs Malik and Shafi'i
Explains Hanafi ruling on qasama with lawth and disputes with Malik and Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qasama in cases of lawth and distinguish it from the Maliki and Shafi'i positions regarding the outcome (diya vs qisas).

Core Idea
In qasama, Hanafis impose diya, not qisas, even when the claim is intentional.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on qasama (compurgation) when strong circumstantial evidence (lawth) exists. The central issue is whether such oaths lead to blood money (diya) or retaliation (qisas). The Hanafi school maintains that only diya is due, while Malik and al-Shafi'i (in one view) permit qisas for intentional claims. We will explore the definition of lawth, the mechanics of qasama, and the proofs each school relies upon.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the specific ruling of the Hanafi school regarding qasama when lawth exists: heirs swear fifty oaths, then defendants pay diya regardless of intent.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Malik and al-Shafi'i (in one saying) differ: they allow qisas if the claim is intentional.
This is the main point of khilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the basis for their position: lawth strengthens the claim, so the heir's oath can establish qisas.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: the oath is on the denier, and qasama is to reveal truth, not to establish punishment; hadiths show combination with diya only.
Key principle: al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: Hanafi leads to diya; Maliki/Shafi'i can lead to qisas in intentional claims.
This affects the remedy available to the heirs.

Source Text

على واحد بعينه، أو ظاهر يشهد للمدَّعي من عداوة ظاهرة، أو شهادة واحدٍ عدل ، أو جماعة غير عدول بأنَّ أهلَ المحلّة قتلوه استحلف الأولياء خمسين يميناً أنَّ أهل المحلة قتلوه ، ثم يقضي بالدِّيةِ على المدعى عليه سواء كان الدعوى بالعمد أو بالخطأ. وقال مالك : يقضي بالقَوَدِ إن كان الدعوى بالعمد، وهو أحد قولي الشَّافِعِي ) ، وإن لم يكن به لوث فمذهبه مثل مذهبنا"، إلا أنه لا يكرر اليمين، بل يردُّها على الولي الماء : إذا كدره، ولوّث ثيابه بالطين؛ أي لطّخه، فيلوث. كذا في «العناية». (۳) [٢]قوله : أو ظاهر يشهد ؛ أي ظاهر الحال، يشهد المدعي بأن العداوة بين المقتول وأهل محلة ظاهرة. [٣] قوله : مثل مذهبنا ... الخ ؛ أي في بداية يمين المدعي عليه غير أنه إن لم يكمل أهل المحلة خمسين لا تكرّر اليمين عليهم، وفيه نظر؛ لأنّ مذهب مالك والشافعي له ليس كذلك، بل يردّ على الأولياء كما في النكول عنده ، فالاختلاف في موضعين في تحليف المدعي أولاً ، وفي براءة أهل المحلة باليمين. فالحاصل أنه إذا وجد ظاهر [يشهدا للمدعي عند الشافعي له يحلف المدعي، فإن حلف أنهم قتلوه خطأ، فله الدية، وإن حلف أنهم قتلوه عمداً فعليهم القصاص في قول، والدية في قول، فإن نكل المدعي عن اليمين حلف المدعى عليهم. فإن حلفوا برئوا، ولا شيء عليهم ، وإن نكلوا فعليهم القصاص في قول، والدية في قول، وإن حلفوا لا دية عليهم" لنا : أن البينة على المدعي، واليمين على مَن أنكر، فاليمين عندنا ليظهر القتل، بتحرزهم عن اليمين الكاذبة فيقروا ، فيجب القصاص، فإذا حلفوا حصلت البراءة عن القصاص، وإنما تجبُ الدِّيَة لوجود القتيل بين أظهرهم ، وإِنَّهُ لجمع بين الدِّيةِ والقَسَامة في حديث رواه سهل، وحديث رواه زياد ابن ، وكذا جمع عمر .
On a specific individual, or an apparent [circumstance] that testifies for the claimant from open enmity, or the testimony of one upright person, or a group of non-upright people that the people of the locality killed him—then the heirs are made to swear fifty oaths that the people of the locality killed him, then judgement is given for blood money against the defendant whether the claim is for intentional or mistaken killing. Malik said: Judgement is for retaliation if the claim is for intentional killing, and that is one of the two sayings of al-Shafi'i; and if there is no lawth, then his school is like our school, except that he does not repeat the oath, rather he returns it to the guardian. The water: if he makes it turbid, and soils his clothes with mud; i.e., stains it, he soils. Thus in al-`Inayah. [1] His saying: on a specific individual; such as if there is found near him a man with a sword, or in his hand something of the tool of killing, and on him are traces of the killing, so that is lawth that obligates qasama for his clan. Thus in al-Jalabi quoting from al-`Inayah. (3) [2] His saying: or an apparent circumstance that testifies; i.e., an apparent circumstance that testifies for the claimant that the enmity between the killed and the people of the locality is apparent. [3] His saying: like our school... etc.; i.e., regarding the beginning of the oath of the defendant, but if the people of the locality do not complete fifty, the oath is not repeated upon them, and there is consideration in it because the school of Malik and al-Shafi'i is not like that, rather it returns to the guardians as in the case of refusal according to him, so the difference is in two places: in making the claimant swear first, and in the acquittal of the people of the locality by oath. So the conclusion is that if there is an apparent circumstance [that testifies for the claimant] according to al-Shafi'i, the claimant swears: if he swears they killed him by mistake, he gets blood money; if he swears they killed him intentionally, then upon them is retaliation in one saying, and blood money in another saying. If the claimant refuses the oath, the defendants swear. If they swear, they are acquitted and nothing is upon them; if they refuse, then upon them is retaliation in one saying, and blood money in another saying. And if they swear, no blood money is upon them. Our proof: that the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies; thus the oath according to us is to reveal the killing, by their caution against the false oath so they confess, so retaliation becomes obligatory. If they swear, acquittal from retaliation is obtained; and blood money is only obligatory due to the presence of the killed among them; and indeed it is combining between blood money and qasama in a hadith narrated by Sahl, and a hadith narrated by Ziyad ibn, and likewise `Umar combined.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary explaining the Hanafi ruling on qasama (compurgation) in cases where lawth (strong circumstantial evidence) exists. The passage is an inter-madhhab dispute, presenting the Hanafi position, then the positions of Malik and al-Shafi'i, and finally the Hanafi proof. The author begins by defining lawth: a specific individual near the victim with a weapon or traces of killing, or an apparent enmity between the victim and the people of the locality, or testimony of one upright person or a group of non-upright persons. In such cases, the Hanafi school holds that the heirs (first guardians) must swear fifty oaths that the people of the locality killed him. After this, judgment is given for blood money (diya) against the defendants, regardless of whether the claim was for intentional or mistaken killing. Malik, however, says that if the claim is intentional, judgment is for retaliation (qisas)—this is also one of the two sayings of al-Shafi'i. If there is no lawth, Malik's school agrees with the Hanafi school, except that he does not repeat the oath (i.e., he does not require fifty oaths from the heirs but returns the oath to the guardian). The commentator then notes the difference: in the beginning of the oath, and in the acquittal by oath. He elaborates the Shafi'i view: if there is an apparent circumstance for the claimant, the claimant swears; if he swears it was mistaken, he gets diya; if he swears it was intentional, there are two opinions—qisas or diya. If the claimant refuses, the defendants swear and are acquitted; if they refuse, again two opinions. The Hanafi response (proof) is given: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' The oath (qasama) is used to reveal the truth; if the defendants swear they are innocent, they are acquitted from qisas, but diya is still due because the victim was found among them. This is supported by the hadith of Sahl ibn Abi Hathmah and the hadith of Ziyad ibn Abi Maryam, and the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab combining between diya and qasama.

Key Terms
اللوث (al-lawth): Strong circumstantial evidence pointing to guilt, such as finding a suspect with a weapon near the victim, open enmity, or testimony of one upright or multiple non-upright witnesses.
القَسَامَة (al-qasāmah): The swearing of fifty oaths by the heirs (or the defendants) to establish liability in homicide cases where lawth exists.
القَوَد (al-qawad): Retaliation (qisas) in homicide, meaning the death penalty for the perpetrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when lawth (strong circumstantial evidence) is present and the heirs swear fifty oaths that the people of the locality killed the victim, what is the judgment against the defendants?
A) Qisas (retaliation) if the claim was intentional
B) Diya (blood money) whether the claim was intentional or mistaken
C) No liability if the defendants also swear
D) A fine and imprisonment

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, qasama (fifty oaths) in cases of lawth (strong evidence) results only in diya (blood money), never qisas, even for intentional claims.

Qasamah: Who Begins the Oath? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling on qasamah with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on who begins the oath in qasamah, explain the Hanafi proof, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
In qasamah, the accused swears first, not the claimant; blood money is still due even after oaths.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the procedure of qasamah. The core issue is whether the accused or the claimant should begin the oaths. Understanding this dispute reveals fundamental principles: the role of the oath on the denier, the combination of qisas and diyah, and the weight of apparent evidence (zahir).

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the exact point of disagreement: who swears first in qasamah? The Hanafi school holds that the accused (locals) swear first. The Shafi'i school holds that the claimant (wali) swears first.
This is not about whether qasamah is performed, but the order of oaths.
2
Present the Hanafi position and evidence
State the Hanafi view: the accused swear, and if they do, they are free from qisas but diyah is still obligatory. The proof: hadith of Sahl and Ziyad, and Umar's practice showing the accused swore first.
The Hanafi principle: al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara (the burden of proof is on the claimant, the oath on the denier).
3
Examine the Shafi'i counter-position
Al-Shafi'i argues that the claimant should swear first because the apparent evidence (zahir) supports him, analogous to the possessor of a hand in a dispute who swears first. This would lead to qisas if the claimant swears.
The Shafi'i proof: the hadith 'fa-yuqsimu minkum khamsun' indicates the claimant's oath.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and tarjih
The Hanafi response: the indication (dalalah) in the Shafi'i proof has ambiguity. Qisas is incompatible with that ambiguity, but wealth (diyah) is combined with the oath. Therefore, diyah is obligatory even after the accused swear. The Hanafi view is relied upon (mufta bihi) as in al-Hidayah.
The Hanafi school does not rely exclusively on the zahir here because the context is blood, where caution is required.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, if the accused swear, diyah is due from the locality. In the Shafi'i school, if the accused swear, no diyah is due. This affects the rights of the victim's family.
The Shafi'i school also has procedures if the accused refuse to swear, but the core difference is the allocation of the initial oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات/ أنواعها وأحكامها
[1] وإن حلفوا لا دية عليهم" لنا : أن البينة على المدعي، واليمين على مَن أنكر، فاليمين عندنا ليظهر القتل، بتحرزهم عن اليمين الكاذبة فيقروا ، فيجب القصاص، فإذا حلفوا حصلت البراءة عن القصاص، وإنما تجبُ الدِّيَة لوجود القتيل بين أظهرهم ، وإِنَّهُ لجمع بين الدِّيةِ والقَسَامة في حديث رواه سهل، وحديث رواه زياد ابن ، وكذا جمع عمر .
[٢] قوله : لا دية عليهم؛ إلى هاهنا قول الشافعي له ودليله في البداية بيمين المدعي قوله الله : للأولياء : «فيقسم منكم خمسون أنهم قتلوه»؛ ولأن اليمين تجب على من يشهد له الظاهر؛ ولهذا تجب على صاحب اليد ، فإذا كان الظاهر شاهداً للولي يبدأ بيمينه، وردّ اليمين على المدعي أصل له كما في النكول، غير أنّ هذه دلالية فيها نوع شبهة، والقصاص لا يجامعها ، والمال يجب معها ؛ فلهذا وجبت الدية. كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
[١] قوله : وحديث رواه زیاد ابن مريم ... الخ ؛ فحديث سهيل ما ذكر، وأما حدیث زیاد ابن مريم فما روى خصيف عن زياد بن أبي مريم له أنه قال : جاء رجل إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «إنّي وجدت أخي قتيلاً في بني فلان ، فقال : اختر من شيوخهم خمسين رجلاً فيحلفونَ باللهِ ما علمنا له قاتلا ولا قتلنا ، فقال الرجل : وليس لي من أخي إلا هذا قال : نعم ومئة من الإبل». كذا في «الكفاية»(۳).
[11] قوله : كرّر الحلف عليهم.... الخ ؛ لما روي أنّ عمر ه لَمَّا قضى في القسامة وأتى إليه تسعة وأربعون رجلاً فكرر اليمين على رجل منهم، حتى تمت خمسين، ثمّ قضى بالدية. وعن شريح والنخعي له مثل ذلك ؛ ولأنَّ الخمسين واجب بالسنّة، فيجب إتمامها ما أمكن، ولا يطلب فيه الوقوف على الفائدة لثبوتها بالسنية، ثم فيه استعظام أمر الدم. هكذا في «الهداية»(۳).
[٢] قوله : ومن نكل منهم....الخ ؛ لأنَّ اليمين فيه مستحقة لذاتها ؛ تعظيماً لأمر الدم ؛ ولهذا يجمع بينه وبين الدية، بخلاف النكول في الأموال ؛ لأنَّ اليمين بدلّ عن أصل حقه ؛ ولهذا يسقط ببدل المدعي ، وفيما نحن فيه لا يسقط ببدل الدية.
فقال رسول الله الله الحويصة ومحيصة وعبد الرحمن تحلفون وتستحقون دم صاحبكم، قالوا : لا قال فتحلف لكم يهود قالوا ليسوا مسلمين فوداه رسول الله له من عنده فبعث إليهم بمئة ناقة حتى دخلت عليهم الدار قال سهل : لقد ركضتني منها ناقة حمراء» في «موطأ مالك» (۲: ۸۷۷)، وسنن النسائي (٣: ٤٨٣) واللفظ له، وأصله في «صحيح البخاري» .(1101 :)
The Book of Diyat/Types and Rulings. [1] And if they swear, no diyah is upon them. For us: The proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies. So the oath according to us is to reveal the killing, by their abstaining from false oaths they confess, so qisas becomes obligatory. So if they swear, they become free from qisas, and diyah is obligatory only because of the presence of the killed person among them, and it is combining between diyah and qasamah in a hadith narrated by Sahl, and a hadith narrated by Ziyad Ibn, and similarly Umar combined. [2] His saying: 'No diyah upon them' up to here is the saying of al-Shafi'i, and his proof in al-Bidayah is the oath of the claimant, his saying: 'Let fifty of you swear that they killed him'; and because the oath is obligatory on one whom the apparent evidence supports; therefore it is obligatory on the possessor of the hand. So if the apparent evidence supports the wali, he begins with his oath, and returning the oath to the claimant is an origin for him as in nukul. However, this indication has some ambiguity, and qisas is not compatible with it, and wealth is obligatory along with it. Therefore diyah became obligatory. This is stated in al-Hidayah. [1] His saying: 'And the hadith narrated by Ziyad Ibn Maryam...' As for the hadith of Sahl, it is what has been mentioned. As for the hadith of Ziyad Ibn Maryam, it is what Khusayf narrated from Ziyad Ibn Abi Maryam: He said: A man came to the Prophet (PBUH) and said: 'I found my brother killed among Banu so-and-so.' He said: 'Choose fifty of their elders, and let them swear by Allah that we do not know a killer for him, nor did we kill.' The man said: 'I have nothing for my brother except this.' He said: 'Yes, and a hundred camels.' This is stated in al-Kifayah. [11] His saying: 'Repeat the oath upon them...' Because it is narrated that Umar, when he gave a judgment in qasamah and forty-nine men came to him, repeated the oath upon one of them until it completed fifty, then he gave judgment with diyah. And from Shuraih and al-Nakha'i the like of that. And because fifty are obligatory by the Sunnah, so their completion is obligatory as much as possible, and stopping at the benefit is not sought because it is established by the Sunnah, and then there is magnifying the matter of blood. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: 'And whoever among them refuses...' Because the oath here is obligatory for its own sake, to magnify the matter of blood; therefore it is combined with diyah, unlike nukul in financial matters, because the oath is a substitute for the origin of his right; therefore it falls with the substitute of the claimant, and in what we are discussing, it does not fall with the substitute of diyah. Then the Messenger of Allah said to Huwayyisa, Muhayyisa, and Abd al-Rahman: 'Swear and you will be entitled to the blood of your companion.' They said: 'No.' He said: 'Then the Jews will swear for you.' They said: 'They are not Muslims.' So the Messenger of Allah paid the blood money from his own side and sent them a hundred she-camels until they entered their house. Sahl said: 'A red she-camel of them kicked me.' In Muwatta Malik (2:877) and Sunan al-Nasa'i (3:483) and the wording is his, and its origin is in Sahih al-Bukhari.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on qasamah (oaths in cases of murder in a locality). It begins by stating the ruling: if the accused swear that they did not kill, then no diyah (blood money) is upon them. The Hanafi proof is that the burden of proof lies on the claimant and the oath on the denier. The oath is administered to reveal the truth, as the accused would avoid false oaths and confess. If they swear, they are free from qisas (retaliation). However, diyah is still obligatory because the victim was found among them, and this combination of diyah and qasamah is proven by a hadith of Sahl and another of Ziyad Ibn Maryam, and also by the practice of Umar. Then the text addresses the view of al-Shafi'i, who holds that the claimant (wali) should start with the oath because the apparent evidence (zahir) supports him, similar to the case of the possessor of a hand in a dispute. The Hanafi response is that while there is some ambiguity, qisas is incompatible with it, but wealth (diyah) is obligatory along with it. The passage also explains the repetition of oaths: if there are not fifty men, the oath is repeated on some men until fifty are completed, as practiced by Umar. If someone refuses to swear, he is imprisoned until he swears, because the oath here is for its own sake to magnify blood, unlike in financial cases where refusal leads to the claimant's oath and judgment. The hadith of the Prophet indicates that the victim's family did not swear, so the Prophet paid diyah from his own wealth.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasamah): Oaths taken by fifty men from a locality where a murder victim is found, to deny knowledge or involvement.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid to the victim's heirs in lieu of qisas.
اليمين (al-yamin): Oath taken by the accused to deny guilt; in qasamah, it is repeated to complete fifty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the consequence if the accused in qasamah complete their oaths?
A) They are free from both qisas and diyah.
B) They are free from qisas but diyah becomes obligatory.
C) Qisas becomes obligatory if they swore falsely.
D) The case is dismissed and no compensation is paid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the oath in qasamah does not negate diyah; it only negates qisas, because the presence of the victim makes blood money wajib.

Ruling on Qasamah When Accusing a Specific Person Outside the Locality
Ruling on qasamah when accusing a specific outsider

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasamah when the heir accuses a specific outsider, and explain why oaths must be completed by repetition and refusal leads to imprisonment.

Core Idea
The oath in qasamah magnifies blood sanctity; thus fifty oaths must be completed even by repetition, and refusal leads to imprisonment.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in the law of qasamah (compurgation) for homicide: what happens when the heir names a single suspect who is not from the locality where the body was found? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the locality is absolved from the oath obligation, but if the required fifty oath-takers are not available, the oath is repeated on individuals to complete fifty, and anyone who refuses is imprisoned until he swears. The author supports this with Prophetic hadith and the practice of Caliph Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage begins with the condition: 'If he claims against one person from other than them' — i.e., the heir accuses a specific individual not from the locality.
2
Note the fall of qasamah from the locality
When a specific outsider is accused, the qasamah obligation drops from the locality. The people of that locality are no longer required to swear.
This is a key Hanafi rule.
3
Understand the completion of fifty oaths
If the fifty oath-takers are not present in the locality (e.g., only 49 men), the oath is repeated on some individuals until fifty oaths have been sworn. This is based on Umar's practice.
The oath is not dropped due to scarcity; it must be completed.
4
Grasp the imprisonment for refusal (nukul)
Anyone who refuses to take the oath is imprisoned until he swears. Unlike property cases, the oath is not a substitute for a right but inheres in the act of swearing itself.
Imprisonment continues until compliance, not a fixed term.
5
Examine the evidence
The author cites the Prophet's instruction to choose fifty elders from the accused tribe and Umar's repetition of oaths to complete the fifty. These establish the wajib nature and the method.
The evidence is ahad hadith (zanni thubut), so the obligation is wajib, not fard.

Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling of qasamah (compurgation) in homicide cases. The author presents the ruling: if the heir claims murder against a specific person who is not from the locality (the place where the body was found), the qasamah obligation drops from the people of that locality. If the required fifty oath-takers are not available in the locality (e.g., only forty-nine), the oath is repeated on some individuals until fifty are completed. The one who refuses to swear is imprisoned until he does so. The commentary provides the evidence: the Prophet's ﷺ instruction to choose fifty elders from the accused tribe to swear they did not kill, and the practice of Umar (رضي الله عنه) who repeated an oath on a single man to complete fifty. The reason given is that the fifty oaths are wajib by Sunnah, so they must be completed as much as possible, and the oath is for its own sake to magnify the sanctity of blood. Unlike property cases where the oath is a substitute for the proof, here it is combined with blood-money, so refusal does not drop the demand.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): A procedure in homicide cases where fifty men from the accused locality swear that they did not kill the victim, to avoid blood-money.
نكل (nakal): Refusal to take the oath after being required to do so.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The qasamah is dropped entirely.
B) The forty-nine men swear, and the missing oath is ignored.
C) The oath is repeated on some men until fifty oaths are completed.
D) The accuser must bring one man from outside.

Key Takeaway
When a specific outsider is accused in a qasamah case, the locality's obligation to swear drops, but if oaths are needed (e.g., against the locality), they must be completed to fifty even by repetition, and refusal leads to imprisonment.

Conditions for Qasamah and Rulings on Corpse Found on Animal
Lists who is exempt from qasamah and when no qasamah or diyah is due, plus rulings for a corpse on an animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which qasamah is not applicable and identify the liable party when a corpse is found on an animal.

Core Idea
Qasamah is restricted to sane free men; a corpse without external marks is natural death, not homicide.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary clarifies who is eligible to participate in qasamah and when blood-money is not due. It also addresses unique scenarios such as a corpse found on an animal or between two villages. Understanding these rules is crucial for correctly applying the Hanafi law of blood-money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories where qasamah is not applicable
List the persons and corpse conditions that negate qasamah/diyah: child, insane, woman, slave, and a corpse with no mark or blood from natural orifices.
The child and insane are excluded due to incapacity for valid oaths; woman and slave due to lack of nusrah (support).
2
Explain the legal reason (ta'lil) for each exemption
For child/insane: they cannot give valid oaths. For woman/slave: they are not from ahl al-nusrah. For corpse without marks: it is considered natural death.
The rationale is based on the nature of qasamah as both oath and support.
3
Apply the rule to the case of a corpse found on an animal
Determine who is liable: the driver/leader/rider's 'aqila, not the locality. This shows the principle of direct causation.
If multiple handlers, they all guarantee.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute about naming specific individuals
Standard position: naming does not absolve the rest. Abu Yusuf's non-standard qiyas would absolve them. The mufta bihi is the standard view.
The difference is recorded in non-standard riwaya and not acted upon.
5
Derive the ruling for a corpse found between two villages
The nearer village is responsible, based on the hadith of the Prophet burying the slain in the nearer village.
This applies when the animal has no known handler.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Blood-Money: Types and Rulings. No qasamah upon a child, an insane person, a woman, or a slave. Neither qasamah nor blood-money (is due) for a corpse that has no mark (of injury) on it, or from whose mouth, anus, or penis blood has come out. And a miscarried fetus whose creation is complete is like an adult. And regarding a slain person found on an animal that a man is driving, his blood-money is paid by the man's 'aqila, not the people of the locality. Likewise if he was leading the animal or riding it. If they are together (driver, leader, rider), they all guarantee (the blood-money). And regarding an animal between two villages on which there is a slain person, the blood-money is on the nearer of them. No qasamah upon a child, an insane person, a woman, or a slave. Neither qasamah nor blood-money (is due) for a corpse that has no mark (of injury) on it, or from whose mouth, anus, or penis blood has come out. Because blood comes out from these organs without anyone's action, unlike the ear and eye. ('And what has complete creation is like an adult') meaning a miscarried fetus found with complete creation and marks of beating, it is like an adult. ('And regarding a slain person found on an animal that a man is driving, his blood-money is paid by the man's 'aqila, not the people of the locality. Likewise if he was leading it or riding it, if they are together they all guarantee') meaning the driver, the leader, and the rider. ('And regarding an animal between two villages on which there is a slain person, the blood-money is on the nearer of them.') And this that we have mentioned is when the heir claims murder against all the people of the locality, and likewise if he claims against some of them not by their specific names, whether the claim is for intentional or accidental murder, because they are not distinct from the rest. But if he claims against some by their specific names, that he killed his relative intentionally or accidentally, the answer is the same; the general answer in al-Quduri indicates this, and the answer in al-Mabsut is likewise. And from Abu Yusuf, according to a non-standard narration, that in analogy the qasamah and blood-money fall from the rest of the people of the locality. Its completion is in al-Hidayah. [1) He said: 'No qasamah upon a child, etc.' — As for the child and the insane, they are not among those capable of valid speech, and the oath is valid speech. As for the woman and the slave, they are not among those capable of giving support (nusrah), and the oath is upon its people. [2] He said: 'concerning a corpse, etc.' — Because he is not a slain person; as for a slain person in custom, it is one whose life expires due to an action directly performed by a living person. So this is one who died a natural death because blood exits from these organs. (3) He said: 'upon the nearer of them' — because it is reported that the Prophet ﷺ was brought a slain person found between two villages, so he ordered that he be buried in the nearer of them.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe listing conditions for qasamah and diyah. It first states that certain individuals are not eligible to be oath-takers: a child (under puberty), an insane person, a woman, and a slave. The reason given is that a child and insane person cannot give valid oaths (they lack full legal capacity), and women and slaves are not from 'ahl al-nusrah' (those who support in blood-money cases) because the oath is a form of support. Then it rules that no qasamah or blood-money is due for a corpse that has no visible wound or from whose natural orifices (mouth, anus, penis) blood has come out, because such death is presumed natural (without external intervention). An exception is made for a miscarried fetus with complete creation and signs of beating, which is treated like an adult. Next, it addresses a corpse found on an animal. If a man is driving the animal, his 'aqila (tribal guarantors) pay the blood-money, not the locality. If there are multiple persons interacting with the animal (driver, leader, rider), they all share liability. If the animal is between two villages, the nearer village is responsible. The passage ends with a note that this is when the heir claims against all the locality or against some without naming them. If the heir names specific individuals, the same applies. A non-standard narration from Abu Yusuf holds that in analogy, naming specific individuals absolves the rest; this opinion is recorded in al-Hidayah but not mufta bihi.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): The swearing by fifty men to clear the locality of murder suspicion.
دية (diyah): Blood-money paid to the heirs of the victim.
عاقلة ('aqila): The group of paternal relatives who pay blood-money on behalf of the killer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, who among the following is NOT eligible to be an oath-taker in qasamah?
A) A free Muslim man
B) A woman
C) A slave
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Qasamah is not required for those lacking legal capacity or nusrah, nor for corpses with no visible injury, and liability for a corpse on an animal falls on the handler's 'aqila.

The Ruling on Qasamah and Diyah When a Dead Body is Found in a House: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for a corpse found in a house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on qasamah and diyah when a body is found in a house, identify the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.

Core Idea
When a body is found in a house, the relied-upon Hanafi view is that no qasamah or diyah is due because possession (yad) deems the death self-inflicted.

Imagine a body is discovered inside someone's home with no witnesses or evidence of who caused the death. In Hanafi fiqh, the question arises: is the house owner liable for qasamah (oath-swearing) and diyah (blood money)? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on this precise issue. We will explore the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his distinguished disciples, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar, and see which view the school ultimately adopted as the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario and the core question
The passage is about a slain person found in a house. The question is whether qasamah (fifty oaths) and diyah (blood money by the 'aqila) are required. Note that this is a case of uncertainty: no known killer.
Qasamah is a special oath procedure used when there is evidence (lawth) but not conclusive proof.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that qasamah is wajib on the finder and diyah on his 'aqila, provided ownership of the house is proven. He argues that the house belongs to the heirs at the time the body is found, so the 'aqila of the heirs must pay.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the house is in the possession of the heirs (since the owner is dead), so they are liable.
3
State the Sahibayn and Zufar's position and their reasoning
Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar say there is no liability at all (no qasamah, no diyah). Their evidence: the house is in the hands (possession) of the finder at the moment of discovery, so it is as if the victim killed himself, rendering the death 'hadar' (unavenged).
The phrase 'al-haqq hadha' in al-Hidayah indicates this view is correct.
4
Identify the mufta bihi and the additional condition about the cry
The relied-upon view is that of the Sahibayn and Zufar: no liability. An additional condition is mentioned: if the victim's cries could have been heard by the household and they could have helped but failed, they may be considered negligent and liable; otherwise not. This is a refinement but does not override the general rule.
This condition affects only the hypothetical scenario of negligence, not the basic dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وجد في دار رجل فعليهِ القَسَامة ، وَتَدي عاقلتُه إن ثبت أنها له بالحجة ، وعاقلة ورثته إن وجد في دار نفسه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، فإِنَّ الدَّارَ حال ظهور القتل للورثة ، فالدِّية على عاقلتهم، وعندهما وعند زفر : لا شيء فيه ، والحق هذا ) ؛ لأنَّ الدَّارَ في يدِهِ حالَ ظهور القتل، فيجعلُ كَأَنَّهُ قتل نفسَهُ فَكانَ هدراً، وإن كانت الدار للورثة ، فالعاقلة إنما يتحملون ما يجب عليهم تخفيفاً لهم، ولا يمكن الإيجاب على الورثة للورثة. وقيل : هذا محمول على ما إذا كان بحيث يبلغ أهله الصوت إذا كان لا يبلغهم الصوت فلا شيء عليهم ؛ لأنه إذا كان بحيث يبلغه الصوت يلحقه الغوث، فيمكنهم النصرة، وقد قصروا، وإذا كان في موضع لا يسمعون صوته لا يلزمهم النصرة، فلا ينسبون إلى التقصير، فلا يجعلون قاتلين تقديراً. كذا في «الهداية»
If a slain person is found in the house of a man, then qasamah is upon him, and his 'aqila pays the blood money, provided it is established by proof that it is his house. And the 'aqila of his heirs if he is found in his own house. This is according to Abu Hanifa, because the house at the time of appearance of the killing is for the heirs, so the blood money is upon their 'aqila. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Zufar: there is nothing upon him, and this is the correct view, because the house is in his hand at the time of the appearance of the killing, so it is as if he killed himself, so it is wasted. And if the house belongs to the heirs, then the 'aqila only bear what is obligated upon them as a concession for them, and it is not possible to obligate heirs to heirs. And it is said: this is interpreted as the case where the cry can reach his people. If it does not reach them, then nothing upon them, because if it reaches them, help can reach him, and they can give support but were negligent; if it is a place where they cannot hear his voice, they are not required to give support, so they are not considered negligent, and they are not deemed as killers constructively. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding qasamah (compurgation) and diyah (blood money) when a dead body is found in a house. The ruling is from the book of diyat in the Hanafi school. Abu Hanifa holds that if the body is found in a person's house, that person must perform qasamah (swearing fifty oaths) and his 'aqila (male paternal relatives) must pay the diyah, provided it is proven by evidence that the house belongs to him. If the body is found in his own house (i.e., his residence is owned by his heirs?), then the 'aqila of the heirs pay. The reasoning for Abu Hanifa is that the house belongs to the heirs at the time the body is discovered, so the liability is on their 'aqila. However, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) along with Zufar disagree: they say there is no qasamah and no diyah at all. Their reasoning is that since the house was in the possession of the owner when the body appeared, it is as if he killed himself, making the death unavenged (hadar). The text declares this as the correct view. An additional nuance is mentioned: the hand (possession) is considered evidence of ownership, but it is probabilistic; if the 'aqila deny ownership, they are not liable until proof is established. The passage also discusses the condition of whether the victim's cries could have been heard. If the location was such that the people of the house could have heard and helped but did not, they are considered negligent and liable; if not, they are not liable. This is based on the principle that they were capable of giving aid but failed. The discussion is found in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasāmah): An oath taken fifty times by the claimant or defendant in cases of homicide where there is no evidence, to establish liability or exonerate.
العاقلة (al-ʿāqilah): The male paternal relatives (asabah) of the killer who are responsible for paying diyah (blood money) collectively.
الدية (ad-diyah): Blood money paid as compensation for homicide or bodily injury, in lieu of qisas (retaliation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when a dead body is found in a person's house?
A) Qasamah and diyah are obligatory upon the house owner and his 'aqila.
B) There is no qasamah and no diyah; the blood is wasted.
C) Only qasamah is required, but not diyah.
D) The 'aqila of the victim must pay diyah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a dead body is found in a house, the relied-upon view is that no qasamah or diyah is due because the owner's possession (yad) is considered evidence that the victim killed himself.

Ruling on Qasama for a Body Found in a House
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when body found in house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on qasama liability when a body is found in a house and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa imposes qasama and diyah; the Sahibayn and Zufar hold no liability — and this is the relied-upon view.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school: what happens when a dead body is discovered in someone's house? Does the homeowner bear responsibility? The classical Hanafi masters disagreed, and understanding their reasoning sharpens our grasp of how liability (daman) is assigned in homicide cases. We will examine the positions, proofs, and the final tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling sought
The passage rules on a slain body found in a man's house. Determine the act (finding the body) and the potential liability (qasama and diyah).
The scenario involves two possibilities: the house belongs to the accused or to the deceased's heirs.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its proof
Abu Hanifa holds that if the house is proven to be the accused's, he must perform qasama and his 'aqilah pays diyah. If found in the deceased's house, the 'aqilah of the heirs pays. His reasoning: the house at the time of discovery is for the heirs.
The exact proof is based on possession and ownership at the time of discovery.
3
State the opposing view of the Sahibayn and Zufar
They hold that nothing is due because the house was in the possession of the deceased at the time of discovery, so it is as if he killed himself (suicide).
The passage explicitly calls their view 'al-haqq hadha' (the correct one).
4
Analyze the additional condition from al-Hidayah
The commentary adds that if the place is remote such that the victim's cries for help cannot be heard, the 'aqilah are not liable due to inability to assist. This condition modifies the scenario.
This condition relates to the duty of assistance (al-nusrah).
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position
The text considers the view of the Sahibayn and Zufar as the 'correct' one, and thus the relied-upon position for fatwa is that no qasama or diyah is due from the homeowner.
This tarjih is based on al-Hidayah and later commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وجد في دار رجل فعليهِ القَسَامة وَتَدي عاقلتُه إن ثبت أنها له بالحجة، وعاقلة ورثته إن وجد في دار نفسه. هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، فإن الدار حال ظهور القتل للورثة، فالدية على عاقلتهم. وعندهما وعند زفر: لا شيء فيه، والحق هذا؛ لأن الدار في يده حال ظهور القتل، فيجعل كأنه قتل نفسه فكان هدرًا. وإن كانت الدار للورثة، فالعاقلة إنما يتحملون ما يجب عليهم تخفيفًا لهم، ولا يمكن الإيجاب على الورثة للورثة. وقيل: هذا محمول على ما إذا كان بحيث يبلغ أهله الصوت، إذا كان لا يبلغهم الصوت فلا شيء عليهم؛ لأنه إذا كان بحيث يبلغه الصوت يلحقه الغوث، فيمكنهم النصرة، وقد قصروا، وإذا كان في موضع لا يسمعون صوته لا يلزمهم النصرة، فلا ينسبون إلى التقصير. كذا في «الهداية». وقال أخي جلبي: جواب عن قول الأعظم: فإن الدار حال ظهور القتل فيها للورثة، وتلخيصه أن الدية لو وجبت على مالك الدار التي وجد القتيل فيها وهو الوارث في زعم الأعظم، ولا يستحق دية المورث إلا وارثه فيلزم أن يجب على الوارث للوارث، وهو ممتنع، وإن انتقلت إلى العاقلة أولاً. كذا فهم من تقرير «العناية».
If a dead body is found in a man's house, then upon him is the qasama, and his 'aqilah pays the blood money if it is proven by evidence that the house is his. And the 'aqilah of his heirs [pays] if it is found in his own house. This according to Abu Hanifa: because the house at the time of the discovery of the killing is [presumed] for the heirs, so the blood money is upon their 'aqilah. According to the two companions and Zufar: there is nothing upon him, and this is corrent; because the house is in his possession at the time of discovery, so he is treated as if he killed himself, thus it is wasted. If the house belongs to the heirs, the 'aqilah only bear what is due upon them as a lightening for them, and it is not possible to impose [liability] on the heirs for the heirs. It is said: This is interpreted as when [the place] is such that his voice reaches his family; if his voice does not reach them, then nothing is upon them; because if it reaches them, help can reach him, so they could have assisted, and they were negligent; but if in a place where they cannot hear his voice, they are not required to assist, so they are not considered negligent. Thus in al-Hidayah. Akhi Jalabi said: It is a response to the statement of al-A'dham: The house at the time of discovery belongs to the heirs. The summary is that if blood money were due on the owner of the house in which the slain is found, and he is the heir according to al-A'dham's claim, and none but the heir is entitled to the blood money of the deceased, then it would entail that the heir owes to the heir, which is impossible, even if it first transfers to the 'aqilah. This is understood from the explanation of al-'Inayah.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling of qasama when a slain body is found in a house. The matn states that if found in another's house, the owner must take oaths and his 'aqilah pays diyah if ownership is proven. If found in his own house, the 'aqilah of his heirs pays. This is Abu Hanifa's position. Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar hold that nothing is due, because the house is in the deceased's possession, so it is as if he killed himself. The commentary then addresses the case where the house belongs to the heirs of the deceased: liability cannot shift to them because that would mean they owe to themselves. A further condition is mentioned from al-Hidayah: if the place is far and his cries cannot be heard, there is no liability due to inability to help. The later commentator Akhi Jalabi explains the reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasāmah): An oath taken by the accused refuting responsibility for a killing when circumstantial evidence points to him
العاقلة (al-ʿāqilah): The group of male relatives (asabah) who pay blood money on behalf of the killer
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of the victim in cases of homicide or injury

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, if a dead body is found in a man's house, what is the ruling?
A) The man must perform qasama and his 'aqilah pays diyah
B) No liability because the house was in the possession of the deceased
C) The man pays diyah directly from his own wealth
D) The 'aqilah of the deceased's heirs pays diyah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative ruling is that a homeowner is not liable for a body found on his property, because the death is attributed to the deceased's own possession.

Qasama: Original Landowners vs. Current Residents
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qasama liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on qasama liability and state the relied-upon position of the school.

Core Idea
Qasama falls on original landowners (ahl al-khittah), not tenants or buyers — custom establishes their responsibility for defense.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about who must swear qasama when a dead body is found in a locality. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad restrict liability to the original landowners (ahl al-khittah), while Imam Abu Yusuf extends it to all current residents. Understanding this disagreement and its basis in custom is key to applying the rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
Identify the core ruling: qasama is upon ahl al-khittah, not upon residents or buyers. Note the reasoning: defense of the locality is customary upon them.
The term 'khittah' refers to the original land allocation for building.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf holds that qasama is upon all residents (ahl al-sukkan) because management authority (wilayat al-tadbīr) arises from both ownership and tenancy.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that buyers and renters have a stake in management, so they are equally responsible for protection and thus liable for qasama.
This is based on analogy (qiyas) between ownership and tenancy in the context of tadbīr.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The text notes that Abu Hanifa based his view on the custom he observed in Kufa, where original landowners were responsible for defense. Custom (ʿurf) is a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school.
The response is not a refutation but a reliance on a stronger evidence (custom) over analogy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a body is found, Abu Hanifa only holds original owners liable; Abu Yusuf includes tenants and buyers. The relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
If all original owners sell, the buyers take over responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسامة على أهل الخطة دون السكان والمشترين، فإن باع كلهم فعلى المشترين. هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله، فإن نصرة البقعة على أهل الخطة. وعند أبي يوسف: هي عليهم جميعاً؛ لأن ولاية التدبير كما يكون بالملك يكون بالسكنى، والمشتري وأهل الخطة سواء في التدبير. وقيل: أبو حنيفة رحمه الله بنى هذا على ما شاهد بالكوفة. فإن وجد في دارين قوم لبعض أكثر، فهي على الرؤوس؛ لأن صاحب القليل والكثير سواء في الحفظ والتقصير. قوله: على أهل الخطة؛ وهي المكان المختط لبناء دار. قوله: دون السكان؛ أراد بهم من يسكن الدار بالإجارة أو الإعارة. قوله: فإن نصرة الخطة على أهل البقعة متعارف.
The qasama is upon the people of the khittah, not upon the residents and buyers. If they all sell, then upon the buyers. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad - may Allah have mercy on them - because the defense of the locality is upon the people of the khittah. According to Abu Yusuf: it is upon all of them, because the authority of management is by ownership as well as by tenancy, and the buyer and the people of the khittah are equal in management. It is said: Abu Hanifa - may Allah have mercy on him - based this on what he observed in Kufa. If a body is found in two houses some of whose people have more [dwellings] than others, then it is upon the heads; because the one with few and the one with many are equal in protection and negligence. His statement: 'upon the people of the khittah' - it is the area marked out for building a house. His statement: 'not upon the residents' - he means those who live in the house by rent or loan. His statement: 'because the defense of the khittah is upon the people of the locality' is a customary matter.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage discusses who bears qasama when a body is found in a locality. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that qasama is limited to the original landowners (ahl al-khittah), not renters or buyers. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that management authority arises from both ownership and tenancy, so all residents should share liability. The text notes that Abu Hanifa's ruling was based on the custom of Kufa, where original landowners defended the area. If all original owners sell, the buyers take over responsibility. If a body is found in multiple houses with different numbers of owners, the qasama is divided equally per head, not per property, because all are equally responsible for protection. The commentary clarifies that 'khittah' refers to the allocated land, 'sukkan' means renters or borrowers, and the custom of defense is well-known.

Key Terms
الخطة (al-khittah): The original land allocation given by the imam to a tribe or group for building
السكان (al-sukkān): People who dwell in a house by rent or loan, not ownership
التدبير (al-tadbīr): Management and protection of the locality

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, who is liable for qasama when a body is found in a locality?
A) All residents including renters and buyers
B) Only the original landowners (ahl al-khittah)
C) Only the heirs of the deceased
D) The ruler of the locality

Key Takeaway
Qasama is upon the people of the khittah, as they are the ones responsible for defense by custom.

Qasamah on Ahl al-Khittah: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains qasamah liability on landowners vs residents and buyers with intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qasamah liability for ahl al-khittah, residents, and buyers, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf based on the role of custom.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, qasamah falls on original landowners, not tenants or buyers—custom determines who manages the neighborhood.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a crucial rule in diyat: when a slain person is found in a property, who bears the collective oath? The author presents the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, distinguishing between original owners, residents, and buyers, and then notes Abu Yusuf's dissent. The dispute hinges on whether management authority exists through ownership alone or also through residence. The passage also covers the per capita distribution when multiple owners have unequal shares.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is qasamah? It is a collective oath wajib on a locality when a slain person is found. The ruling specifies who is liable.
The passage sets the liable parties.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
Qasamah is on ahl al-khittah (original landowners), not on residents or buyers unless all owners sell.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds all (original owners, residents, buyers) liable because management authority exists through both ownership and residence.
Identify the difference in proof.
4
Analyze the role of custom (urf)
Abu Hanifa's view is based on the observed custom in Kufa, where only original owners managed the neighborhood. Custom specifies the application of general principles.
Understand how urf operates as a source of law.
5
Apply the per capita rule
When multiple owners have unequal shares, qasamah is per capita, not per share, because all share equally in guarding and negligence.
This is analogous to shuf'ah per capita.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسامة على أهل الخطّة دون السكان والمشترين، فإن باع كلهم فعلى المشترين ، فإن وجد في دارين قوم لبعض أكثر، فهي على الرؤوس
قال: والقسامة على أهل الخطة) : أي ملاك الأرض، (دون السكان) والمشترين، فإن باع كلهم فعلى المشترين ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله ، فإنَّ نصرة " البقعة على أهل الخطة، وعند أبي يوسف : هي عليهم جميعاً؛ لأنَّ ولاية التدبير كما يكونُ بالملكِ يكونُ بالسكنى ، والمشتري وأهل الخطة سواء في التدبير ، وقيل : أبو حنيفة الله بنى هذا على ما شاهد بالكوفة. (فإن وجد في دارين قوم لبعض أكثر، فهي على الرؤوس)؛ لأنَّ صاحبَ القليل والكثير سواء في الحفظ والتقصير.
[۱] قوله : على أهل الخطة؛ وهي بكسر الخاء المعجمة : المكان المختط لبناء دار أو غيرها من العمارات والمغارات على أصحاب الأملاك القديمة التي كانوا يملكونها قبل فتح الإمام البلدة، وقسمها بين الغانمين، فإنه يخط خطـة التمييز لأنـصبائه. كذا في «النهاية».
[٢] قوله : دون السكان؛ أراد بهم من يسكن الدار بالإجارة أو الإعارة. كذا في «العناية».
