[0] قوله : فهي على الرؤوس؛ لأن صاحب القليل يزاحم صاحب الكثير في التدبر فكانوا سواء في الحفظ والتقصير، فيكون على عدد الرؤوس بمنزلة الشفعة، فإنها على عدد الرؤوس لا قدر الأنصباء عندنا. كذا في «الكفاية».
And the qasamah is upon the people of the plot (ahl al-khittah) to the exclusion of the residents and the buyers. If all of them sell, then it is upon the buyers. If a slain person is found in two houses belonging to a people, some having more shares, then it is upon the heads (per capita). [Commentary:] He said: 'And the qasamah is upon the people of the plot': meaning the owners of the land, to the exclusion of the residents and the buyers. If all of them sell, then it is upon the buyers. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), for the protection of the place is upon the people of the plot. According to Abu Yusuf: it is upon all of them together, because the authority of management exists by residence just as it exists by ownership, and the buyer and the people of the plot are equal in management. It is said: Abu Hanifa built this upon what he witnessed in Kufa. (If a slain person is found in two houses belonging to a people, some having more shares, then it is upon the heads) because the one with little and the one with much are equal in guarding and negligence. [Footnote 1] His saying: 'upon the people of the plot' — al-khittah (with a kasra on the kha) is the place designated for building a house or other structures and caves, belonging to the owners of the ancient properties that they owned before the Imam conquered the town and divided it among the warriors, for he draws lines to distinguish the shares. This is from al-Nihayah. [Footnote 2] His saying: 'to the exclusion of the residents' — he means those who reside in the house by lease or loan. This is from al-Inayah. [Footnote 3] His saying: 'for the protection of the plot is upon the people of the spot' is customary. If you ask: what is the difference between the neighborhood and the house? If a buyer and a plot owner find a slain person in the house, they are equal in qasamah and blood money, with no difference by consensus; but regarding the neighborhood, there is a difference. I say: the distinction is custom, for custom holds that buyers rarely compete with plot owners in managing the guarding of the neighborhood, but the situation of a house is not like that. [Footnote 4] His saying: 'upon what he witnessed in Kufa' — meaning he witnessed the custom of the people of Kufa in his time, that the plot owners in every neighborhood are the ones who manage the neighborhood and buyers do not share that with them, so he based the answer on what he witnessed. This is from al-Nihayah. [Footnote 0] His saying: 'then it is upon the heads' — because the owner of little competes with the owner of much in management, so they are equal in guarding and negligence; thus it is per capita, like the right of pre-emption, which is per capita and not according to the value of shares according to us. This is from al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability for qasamah (a collective oath to establish responsibility for a slain person) in the context of a found corpse. The author first states the general rule: qasamah falls on the original landowners (ahl al-khittah), not on residents (tenants) or buyers. If all original owners sell, then it shifts to the buyers. The commentary explains that this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, holding that qasamah is on all (original owners, residents, and buyers) because the authority to manage the property exists through residence as well as ownership, so all are equal in that regard. It is mentioned that Abu Hanifa based his ruling on the actual practice he observed in Kufa, where only original owners managed the neighborhood. The passage then covers a second scenario: if a corpse is found in two houses owned by different people with unequal shares, the qasamah is distributed per capita (by number of owners), not by the size of their shares. The reasoning is that all owners are equal in the duty of guarding and in potential negligence, analogous to the rule of pre-emption (shuf'ah) in the Hanafi school. The commentary further defines key terms: 'khittah' refers to the original allotted land for building; 'residents' are those living there via lease or loan. The distinction between a neighborhood and a single house is based on custom: buyers rarely participate in neighborhood management, but in a house, they do. Thus, the ruling differs by context.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasamah): A collective oath taken by the people of a locality to defend against liability for a slain person found among them, or to establish who is responsible.
الخطة (al-khittah): The original land allocated by the Imam for building upon conquest; the original owners of that land.
السكان (al-sukkan): Residents who live in the property by lease (ijarah) or loan (i'arah), i.e., non-owners.
المشترين (al-mushtarin): Buyers who have purchased the property from the original owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), upon whom does qasamah fall if a slain person is found in a property that still has its original owner (ahl al-khittah) and also a tenant?
A) On the original owner only.
B) On the tenant only.
C) On both the original owner and the tenant equally.
D) On neither.

Key Takeaway
Qasamah liability is primarily on the original landowners, not residents or buyers, based on custom—this intra-Hanafi dispute illustrates how observed practice (urf) can determine legal responsibility.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Liability for Qasamah in Sold Property: The Yad vs. Milk Dispute
Rulings on qasamah liability with an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qasamah liability in a sale with and without option, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
In qasamah, liability follows possession (yad), not ownership (milk) according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute over who bears blood money when a corpse is found on sold property before possession. The key is whether liability attaches to the person with physical control (yad) or the legal owner (milk). We'll explore the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, and see how the ruling applies to other locations like ships and mosques.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and General Rule
Read the main text: when a house is sold but not yet taken possession, the blood money is on the seller's 'aqilah. In a sale with an option, it is on the 'aqilah of the one in whose hand the property lies.
Pay attention to the distinction between absolute sale and sale with option.
2
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that liability follows the hand (yad). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that liability follows ownership (milk). For a sale with no option, they say the buyer is owner, so his 'aqilah is liable. For a sale with option, the ultimate owner is considered.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the Proofs (Qiyas)
Abu Hanifa argues that the ability to guard comes from having the hand, not ownership. One can guard without owning but cannot guard without possession. The Sahibayn argue that guarding is a duty of the owner because authority to guard derives from ownership.
The debate is a classic case of qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Apply to Other Locations
The text extends the rule: on a ship, liability is on those in it (passengers, sailors, owners present); in a mosque of a neighborhood, on inhabitants; between two villages, on the closer one. All are linked by the idea of those who have effective control.
These follow the same principle of yad.
5
Note the Tarjih and Mufta Bihi
The commentary in al-Hidayah supports Abu Hanifa's view as the stronger analogy. Thus the operative fatwa (mufta bihi) is that liability follows possession.
This means in practice, the seller's 'aqilah is liable before possession, even in an absolute sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1].
( فإن بيعت ولم تقبض فعلى عاقلة البائع، وفي البيع بخيار على عاقلة ذي اليد)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وقالا : إن لم يكن فيه خيار فعلى عاقلة المشتري ، وإن كان فعلى عاقلة من تصير له سواء كان الخيار للبائع أو للمشتري. ( وفي الفلكِ على من فيه " ، وفي مسجدِ محلة على أهلها، وبين القريتين على أقربهما
[١]قوله : فإن بيعت... الخ؛ صورته : اشترى رجل داراً ولم يقبضها حتى وجد فيها قتيل، فالدية على عاقلة البائع، وإن كان في البيع خيار لأحدهما فالدية على عاقلة من في يده ، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة .
وقالا : إن لم يكن فيه خيار، فهي على عاقلة المشتري، وإن كان فيه خيار فهي على عاقلة من تصير له؛ لأنه إنّما أنزلَ قاتلاً باعتبار التقصير في الحفظ، ولا يجب الحفظ إلا على من له ولاية الحفظ والولاية تثبت بالملك، ولكون ولاية الحفظ مستفادة بالملك، كانت الدية على عاقلة صاحب الدار دون المودع؛ لعدم ملكه، وإن كان له يد، والملك هنا للمشتري قبل القبض في البيع البات، وفي البيع الذي فيه الخيار يعتبر قرار الملك.
له : إنّ القدرة على الحفظ باليد بملك اليد لا بالملك، ألا ترى أنه يقتدر على الحفظ باليد بدون الملك ولا يقتدر بالملك بدون اليد، وفي البات: اليد للبائع قبل القبض، وكذا فيما فيه الخيار لأحدهما قبل القبض؛ لأنه دون البات.
ولو كان البيع في يد المشتري والخيار له فهو أخص الناس به تصرفاً، ولو كان الخيار للبائع فهو في يده مضمون عليه بالقيمة كالمغصوب، فتعتبريده إذ بها يقدر على الحفظ. كذا في «الهداية»(۱).
[٢] قوله : على مَن فيه؛ من الركاب والملاحين؛ لأنَّ الفلك في أيديهم، ولفظ فيه يشمل أربابها، حتى تجب على الأرباب الذين فيها، وعلى السكان.
If it (the house) is sold and not taken possession, then the blood money is upon the 'aqilah (paternal relatives) of the seller. In a sale with an option, it is upon the 'aqilah of the one in whose hand (the property) is. In a ship, it is upon those in it. In a mosque of a neighborhood, it is upon its inhabitants. And between two villages, it is upon the closer one.
[Commentary:] (If it is sold and not taken possession, then upon the 'aqilah of the seller; in a sale with an option, upon the 'aqilah of the one in whose hand it is.) This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If there is no option in it, it is upon the 'aqilah of the buyer; if there is an option, it is upon the 'aqilah of the one to whom it will eventually belong, whether the option is for the buyer or the seller. (And in a ship, upon those in it; and in a mosque of a neighborhood, upon its inhabitants; and between two villages, upon the closer one.)
[Footnote 1] His saying: 'If it is sold...' etc.; its scenario: a man bought a house and did not take possession of it until a slain person was found in it; then the blood money is upon the 'aqilah of the seller. If there is an option for either in the sale, then the blood money is upon the 'aqilah of the one in whose hand it is. And this is according to Abu Hanifa.
The two said: If there is no option in it, then it is upon the 'aqilah of the buyer; if there is an option, then it is upon the 'aqilah of the one to whom it will eventually belong, because a person is considered a killer by reason of negligence in guarding, and guarding is only obligatory on the one who has authority to guard, and authority is established by ownership. Since the authority of guarding is derived from ownership, the blood money is upon the 'aqilah of the owner of the house and not upon the depositary (who has only possession), because he does not own it, even though he has a hand. And ownership here is for the buyer before taking possession in an absolute sale, but in a sale with an option, the final ownership is considered.
(Abu Hanifa) said to them: The ability to guard is through the hand by possession of the hand, not by ownership. Do you not see that one can guard by hand without ownership, and cannot guard by ownership without the hand? In an absolute sale, the hand is with the seller before possession, and likewise in a sale with an option for either before possession, because it is lesser than an absolute sale. If the sale is in the hand of the buyer and the option is for him, then he is the most specific person in terms of disposition; if the option is for the seller, then it (the property) is in his hand as a surety for the price like a usurped object, so his hand is considered because by it he can guard. This is in al-Hidayah.
[Footnote 2] His saying: 'upon those in it': meaning the passengers and sailors, because the ship is in their hands; and the expression 'those in it' includes its owners, so it is obligatory upon the owners who are in it and upon the residents.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on who is liable for blood money (via 'aqilah) when a corpse is found in various locations, with a focus on the role of possession (yad) versus ownership. The main text outlines the rule: if a house is sold but not yet taken possession of, liability falls on the seller's 'aqilah. If there is a sale with a condition (khiyar), then it falls on the 'aqilah of the one who has physical control (dhu al-yad). For a ship, it's on those on board; for a mosque, on the inhabitants of the neighborhood; for land between two villages, on the closer one. The commentary then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that in a sale with or without option, before possession, the 'aqilah of the seller or the one with the hand is liable. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) distinguish: if no option, the buyer's 'aqilah is liable (because ownership has transferred); if there is an option, the 'aqilah of the one who will ultimately own is liable (since ownership is not final). The reasoning is that negligence in guarding is the basis, and guarding is owed by the one with ownership. Abu Hanifa counters that the ability to guard comes from having the hand (yad), not ownership; you can guard without ownership but not without the hand. Thus, in an absolute sale, the seller still has the hand before possession, so he is liable; in a sale with an option, the hand remains with the one who has it (seller or buyer depending on terms). The footnote adds that on a ship, the liability is on those who have actual control—passengers, sailors, and owners actually present.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The paternal relatives (asabah) who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a person liable for unintentional killing.
البيع بخيار (al-bay' bi-khiyar): A sale contract with an option (khiyar) for one or both parties to rescind within a specific period.
اليد (al-yad): Physical possession or control over the property, which is the basis for the ability to guard.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a house is sold but the buyer has not yet taken possession, and a body is found there, whose 'aqilah is liable?
A) The buyer's 'aqilah.
B) The seller's 'aqilah.
C) Both equally.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for blood money from a corpse found on property follows possession (yad), not ownership, according to the relied-upon position.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Liability for Corpse Found in Sold Property: The Yad vs Milk Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for corpse found in various locations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding blood money liability for a corpse found in a sold but undelivered property, and apply the rule of physical possession (yad) vs ownership (milk) to determine whose 'aqila bears the obligation.

Core Idea
Liability for a corpse follows physical possession, not ownership — the hand that can preserve bears the blood money.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about who must pay blood money (diyah) when a dead body is found on property that has been sold but not yet handed over. Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on whether the duty of preservation arises from ownership or from physical control. Their disagreement extends to various contexts: ships, mosques, and villages. Understanding this dispute reveals the Hanafi method of juristic reasoning (ta'lil) and how the effective cause ('illa) of liability is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and general ruling
The base text states that for a sold and undelivered house, the blood money is on the seller's 'aqila; for a sale with khiyar, it is on the 'aqila of the one in possession. Understand this initial ruling before analyzing the dispute.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Note that Abu Hanifa holds the above ruling, while the Sahibayn split: if no khiyar, it is on the buyer's 'aqila; if there is a khiyar, it is on the 'aqila of the one for whom the property ultimately settles.
The Sahibayn's view is based on ownership as the source of the duty to preserve.
3
Analyze the underlying proofs (adilla)
Sahibayn base liability on ownership (milk) because the duty to preserve (wilayat al-hifz) is derived from ownership. Abu Hanifa bases it on physical possession (yad) because only the hand can actually preserve. Work through the analogical reasoning: ability to preserve is tied to the hand, not ownership.
Abu Hanifa's key argument: preservation is possible with yad without milk, but not with milk without yad.
4
Apply the rule to other locations
The text extends the principle to ships (liability on those on board — passengers and sailors — because they have yad), a mosque of a district (liability on inhabitants because they use and preserve it), and between two villages (liability on the nearer village due to proximity and presumed control).
The same logic of yad and ability to preserve applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بيعت ولم تقبض فعلى عاقلة البائع، وفي البيع بخيار على عاقلة ذي اليد، وفي الفلك على من فيه ، وفي مسجد محلة على أهلها ، وبين القريتين على أقربهما. فإن بيعت ولم تقبض فعلى عاقلة البائع، وفي البيع بخيار على عاقلة ذي اليد، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وقالا: إن لم يكن فيه خيار فعلى عاقلة المشتري، وإن كان فعلى عاقلة من تصير له سواء كان الخيار للبائع أو للمشتري. وفي الفلك على من فيه، وفي مسجد محلة على أهلها، وبين القريتين على أقربهما. قوله: فإن بيعت... الخ؛ صورته: اشترى رجل داراً ولم يقبضها حتى وجد فيها قتيل، فالدية على عاقلة البائع، وإن كان في البيع خيار لأحدهما فالدية على عاقلة من في يده، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا: إن لم يكن فيه خيار، فهي على عاقلة المشتري، وإن كان فيه خيار فهي على عاقلة من تصير له؛ لأنه إنما أنزل قاتلاً باعتبار التقصير في الحفظ، ولا يجب الحفظ إلا على من له ولاية الحفظ والولاية تثبت بالملك، ولكون ولاية الحفظ مستفادة بالملك، كانت الدية على عاقلة صاحب الدار دون المودع؛ لعدم ملكه، وإن كان له يد، والملك هنا للمشتري قبل القبض في البيع البات، وفي البيع الذي فيه الخيار يعتبر قرار الملك. له: إن القدرة على الحفظ باليد بملك اليد لا بالملك، ألا ترى أنه يقتدر على الحفظ باليد بدون الملك ولا يقتدر بالملك بدون اليد، وفي البات: اليد للبائع قبل القبض، وكذا فيما فيه الخيار لأحدهما قبل القبض؛ لأنه دون البات. ولو كان البيع في يد المشتري والخيار له فهو أخص الناس به تصرفاً، ولو كان الخيار للبائع فهو في يده مضمون عليه بالقيمة كالمغصوب، فتعتبريده إذ بها يقدر على الحفظ. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: على من فيه؛ من الركاب والملاحين؛ لأن الفلك في أيديهم، ولفظ فيه يشمل أربابها، حتى تجب على الأرباب الذين فيها، وعلى السكان.
If it is sold and not taken into possession, then (the blood money) is upon the 'aqila of the seller. In a sale with a condition (khiyar), it is upon the 'aqila of the one in possession (dhu al-yad). On a ship, it is upon those on it. In a mosque of a district, upon its inhabitants. Between two villages, upon the closer one. (Then the commentary repeats the text and explains:) This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If there is no khiyar, it is upon the 'aqila of the buyer; if there is khiyar, it is upon the 'aqila of the one for whom (the property) becomes settled, whether the khiyar belongs to the seller or the buyer. (Commentary continues:) The statement 'If it is sold... etc.': its scenario: A man bought a house and did not take possession until a corpse was found in it, so the blood money is upon the 'aqila of the seller. If there is a khiyar for one of them, then the blood money is upon the 'aqila of the one in whose hand (the property) is. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions said: If there is no khiyar, it is upon the 'aqila of the buyer; if there is khiyar, it is upon the 'aqila of the one for whom (the property) becomes settled, because the (victim) is considered killed due to negligence in preservation, and preservation is only obligatory upon one who has authority (wilayah) of preservation, and authority is established by ownership. Since the authority of preservation is derived from ownership, the blood money is upon the 'aqila of the owner of the house, not upon the depositary (mudi'), because he does not own it, even though he has possession (yad). Ownership here belongs to the buyer before taking possession in an absolute sale (al-bay' al-batt), and in a sale with khiyar, the settlement of ownership (qarar al-milk) is considered. According to Abu Hanifa: Ability to preserve is through the hand by possession of the hand (yad), not by ownership. Do you not see that one can preserve through the hand without ownership, but cannot preserve through ownership without the hand? In an absolute sale, the hand (possession) is with the seller before taking possession, and similarly in a sale with khiyar for one of them before taking possession, because it is less than an absolute sale. If the sale is in the buyer's hand and the khiyar is his, then he is the most entitled to it in terms of disposition. If the khiyar is for the seller, then it is in his hand and he is liable for its value like a usurped item (maghsab), so his hand is considered because through it he is able to preserve. As in Al-Hidayah. The statement 'upon those on it': i.e., the passengers (rukab) and sailors (mallahin), because the ship is in their hands, and the word 'those on it' includes its owners (arbab) so that it is obligatory upon the owners who are on it and upon the residents.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi commentary (sharh) discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for a corpse found in various locations. The text first states the ruling: if a house is sold but not yet delivered to the buyer, and a corpse is found there, the blood money (diyah) is on the 'aqila of the seller. If the sale includes a khiyar (option), then it is on the 'aqila of the person who has physical possession (dhu al-yad). For a ship, it is on the 'aqila of those on board; for a mosque of a district, on the inhabitants; and between two villages, on the closer village. The commentary then expands: Abu Hanifa (the Imam) holds the above view. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differentiate: if there is no khiyar, the liability falls on the buyer's 'aqila because ownership is transferred immediately; if there is a khiyar, it falls on the 'aqila of the person for whom the property is ultimately settled, regardless of who holds the khiyar. The reason for the dispute is the basis of liability: negligence in preservation. The two companions argue that the duty to preserve is rooted in ownership (milk), so liability follows ownership. Abu Hanifa argues that effective preservation is possible only through physical possession (yad), not through legal ownership alone. He illustrates: a person can preserve an item in their hand without owning it, but cannot preserve an item they own if it is not in their hand. In an absolute sale, the hand is with the seller before delivery, so the seller's 'aqila is liable. If the buyer has the khiyar, he is the most entitled to dispose of the property, so his hand is considered. If the seller has the khiyar, the property remains in his hand and he is liable for it as if it were a usurped item (maghsab), so his hand is the basis of liability. The commentary also clarifies that on a ship, liability is on the passengers and sailors (and owners if present) because they have physical control. The same applies to a mosque: the inhabitants of the district are liable because they are the ones who use and maintain it. Between two villages, the closer village is liable because of proximity and presumed control.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('āqila): The male paternal relatives (asabah) of a person who are collectively responsible for paying blood money (diyah) in cases of quasi-intentional killing or negligence.
يد (yad): Physical possession or control over an item; the basis for the duty to preserve and thus liability.
ولاية الحفظ (wilāyat al-ḥifẓ): The authority or jurisdiction to preserve and protect property, derived from ownership or possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, whose 'aqila is liable when a corpse is found in a house sold with a khiyar option that remains unresolved?
A) Seller's 'aqila
B) Buyer's 'aqila
C) 'Aqila of the one for whom the property is settled
D) Both equally

Key Takeaway
Blood money liability for a corpse found on property depends on who has physical possession (yad) and thus the ability to preserve, not merely legal ownership.

Liability for a Corpse Found in an Owned Market: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on liability for corpse in various public and private spaces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on liability (diyah) for a corpse found in various places and explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the owned market.

Core Idea
Ownership of a place determines liability, but intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf arises on owner vs residents.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya comments on Al-Hidāyah's rulings on qasāmah and diyah. It establishes that for a corpse found in an owned market, the blood money falls on the owner according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but on the residents according to Abu Yusuf. For public places like streets and mosques, the Bayt al-Mal pays. The commentary extends these principles to ships and market rows using analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general categories of places
The passage distinguishes between owned markets, non-owned places (public thoroughfare, prison, congregational mosque), and places like market rows and districts. Note the default ruling for each category.
Non-owned/public places → no qasāmah, diyah from Bayt al-Mal.
2
Isolate the intra-Hanafi dispute on owned markets
Compare the positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the owner liable; Abu Yusuf holds the residents liable. Identify the underlying reasoning: ownership vs actual residence/use.
3
Expand to analogous cases
The passage uses analogies: a ship with a known owner vs passengers, and a market row where residents spend the night. Apply the same principle of who has the duty of preservation.
The ship analogy: if owner known, he is liable; if not, passengers.
4
Apply the underlying principle: negligence in preservation
Liability arises from negligence in maintaining the place. The one who has authority and responsibility to preserve the place is liable. This explains the ikhtilaf on who that is in an owned market.
The ikhtilaf turns on who is considered negligent: owner (failing to supervise) or residents (failing to keep safe).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي سوق مملوك على المالك، وفي غير مملوك والشارع والسجن والجامع لا قسامة، والدية على بيت المال. وفي سوق مملوك على المالك، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وعند أبي يوسف على السكان، وفي غير مملوك والشارع والسجن والجامع لا قسامة، والدية على بيت المال، أما عند أبي يوسف فالقسامة على أهل السجن؛ لأنهم سكان. قال في «الكفاية»: ذكر شيخ الإسلام: قال بعض المشايخ: إنما تجب على الركاب إذا لم يكن للسفينة مالك معروف، فإن كان فالقسامة عليه. انتهى. وكذا على من يمدها المالك وغيره في ذلك سواء، كما في الدابة، بخلاف المحلة والدار؛ لأنهما لا تنتقلان. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: وفي سوق مملوك على المالك؛ قال أخي جلبي: إنه قيل: إذا وجد قتيل في صف من السوق فإن كان أهل ذلك الصف يبيتون في حوانيتهم فالدية عليهم، وإلا فعلى ملاكها. كذا في «التبيين». قوله: والشارع؛ أي وجد قتيل في الشارع، وهو الطريق الأعظم فلا قسامة، والدية على بيت المال؛ لأنه للعامة لا يختص به واحد منهم في النهاية، وإنما أراد به أن يكون نائياً عن المحال. أما الأسواق التي تكون في المحال فهي محفوظة بحفظ أهل المحلة، فيكون القسامة والدية على أهل المحلة وكذا في السوق اتفاقي إذا كان من يسكنها في الليالي، أو كان لأحد منهما دار مملوكة تكون القسامة والدية عليه، إذ يلزمه صيانة ذلك الموضع، فيوصف بالتقصير، فيجب عليه بموجب التقصير.
And in an owned market, it is upon the owner. In a non-owned (place), the public thoroughfare (al-shari'), the prison, and the congregational mosque (al-jami'), there is no qasāmah, and the blood money is upon the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). Regarding 'In an owned market, it is upon the owner': this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, it is upon the residents. Regarding non-owned places, the public thoroughfare, the prison, and the congregational mosque: there is no qasāmah, and the blood money is upon the Bayt al-Mal. However, according to Abu Yusuf, the qasāmah is upon the inhabitants of the prison because they are residents. It is stated in Al-Kifāyah: Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that some scholars said: it (qasāmah) is only obligatory upon the passengers if the ship has no known owner; if it does, the qasāmah is upon him. End quote. Similarly, upon the one who moors the ship, whether the owner or another, it is the same, like an animal, unlike the district and house because they are not movable. As in Al-Hidayah. The statement 'In an owned market, it is upon the owner': my brother Jalbī said: it is said that if a corpse is found in a row of the market, if the people of that row spend the night in their shops, the blood money is upon them; otherwise, upon the owners (mālikah). As in Al-Tabyīn. The statement 'the public thoroughfare': i.e., a corpse is found in the public thoroughfare, which is the main road; then there is no qasāmah, and the blood money is upon the Bayt al-Mal because it belongs to the general public, not specific to any one individual in the end. It (the thoroughfare) is meant to be remote from districts. As for markets that are in districts, they are preserved by the preservation of the district's inhabitants; so the qasāmah and blood money are upon the inhabitants of the district. Similarly, in a market that is established (ittifāqī) if there are those who spend the night there, or if someone has an owned house in it, the qasāmah and blood money are upon him, because he is obliged to maintain that place, so he is described as negligent, and thus it is obligatory upon him due to negligence.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of liability (diyah and qasāmah) for corpses found in various places. The base ruling states: In an owned market (sūq mamlūk), the blood money is upon the owner. In non-owned places (ghayr mamlūk), the public thoroughfare (al-shāri'), the prison, and the congregational mosque (al-jāmi'), there is no qasāmah (oath-taking procedure) and the blood money is paid from the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). The commentary then expands: According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, liability for a corpse found in an owned market falls on the owner of the market. According to Abu Yusuf, it falls on the residents (sukkān) of that market. For non-owned places, all three agree that the Bayt al-Mal pays, but Abu Yusuf holds that in a prison, the qasāmah is upon the prisoners (or their 'aqila) because they are residents. The commentary then cites Al-Kifāyah: regarding a ship, if it has no known owner, the passengers are liable; if there is an owner, he is liable. This is analogous to an animal that is moored. This differs from a district or house because those are fixed. The commentary also quotes a view from Al-Tabyīn: In a market, if the shopkeepers sleep in their shops at night, they are liable; otherwise, the owners are liable. Finally, it clarifies that the public road is for everyone, so no specific person is negligent, hence the Bayt al-Mal pays. In contrast, if a market is within a district, the inhabitants of the district are responsible because they maintain it. Similarly, if someone has a house in an area, they are responsible for maintaining the area, so they bear liability.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasāmah): A legal procedure where fifty oath-takers swear that the accused caused death, used in cases where there is strong suspicion but no witnesses; it establishes liability for blood money.
بيت المال (bayt al-māl): The public treasury of the Muslim state, responsible for paying blood money in cases where no specific party is liable.
سوق مملوك (sūq mamlūk): A market that is owned by a specific person (as opposed to a public market).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, upon whom does the blood money fall if a corpse is found in an owned market?
A) The owner of the market
B) The residents (sukkān) of the market
C) The Bayt al-Mal
D) The seller of the goods

Key Takeaway
In owned markets, liability falls on the owner (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) or residents (Abu Yusuf), while public places like streets and mosques are covered by Bayt al-Mal.

Qasamah for a Found Corpse: Owner vs. Residents
Ruling on qasamah and diyah for corpse in private vs public places, with intra-Hanafi dispute and ship analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on qasamah liability for a found corpse in owned vs. public spaces and apply the ruling to analogous cases like a ship.

Core Idea
Liability for qasamah falls on the one in control—owner for fixed property, owner/passengers for mobile property like a ship.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition addresses a practical scenario: a corpse is found and the perpetrator is unknown. Who bears the blood-money? The answer depends on the location—whether it is owned, public, or mobile. The Hanafi imams differ on whether the owner or the residents bear the qasamah. Let us unpack the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify location types and basic rule
Note that for owned places (souq mamlook) the owner is liable; for public places (street, prison, mosque) there is no qasamah and diyah is on bayt al-mal.
Abu Yusuf differs on the prison (he imposes qasamah on inmates).
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad place liability on the owner of the market; Abu Yusuf places it on the residents. The difference hinges on who is considered the effective controller (owner vs user).
The mufta bihi position is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
3
Examine the ship analogy
Al-Kifayah cites Shaykh al-Islam: on a ship, if the owner is known, qasamah is on him; if unknown, on the passengers. This follows the same principle of control.
Ships are movable, unlike fixed property, so the analogy extends but with adjustments.
4
Compare fixed vs. mobile property
The passage distinguishes neighborhoods/houses (fixed) from ships/animals (mobile). Fixed property always points to the owner; mobile property may shift liability to users if the owner is unknown.
This reflects the principle that control (al-yad) determines liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي سوق مملوك على المالك) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وعند أبي يوسف على السكان، وفي غير مملوك والشارع " والسجن والجامع لا قسامة ، والدية على بيت المال، أما عند أبي يوسف الله فالقسامة على أهل السجن ؛ لأنهم سكان. قال في «الكفاية») : ذكر شيخ الإسلام ل له : قال بعض المشايخ: إنّما تجب على الركاب إذا لم يكن للسفينة مالك معروف، فإن كان فالقسامة عليه. انتهى. وكذا على من يمدها المالك وغيره في ذلك سواء، كما في الدابة، بخلاف المحلّة والدار ؛ لأنهما لا تنتقلان. كذا في «الهداية».
And in a privately owned market, [the liability is] upon the owner — this according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; according to Abu Yusuf, upon the residents. And in a non-owned [place], the street, the prison, and the mosque, there is no qasamah, and the diyah is upon the treasury. But according to Abu Yusuf [said] Allah, the qasamah is upon the people of the prison because they are residents. He said in al-Kifayah: Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that some mashayikh said: it is only obligatory upon the passengers if the ship has no known owner; if it does, then qasamah is upon him. End. And similarly upon whoever [the owner] extends it, and others in that are equal, like an animal, unlike a neighborhood and a house because they do not move. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability (qasamah and diyah) when a corpse is found in different locations. The main ruling: if a corpse is found in a privately owned market (souq mamlook), Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the owner is liable, while Abu Yusuf holds that the residents are liable. For non-owned places like public streets, prisons (in Abu Hanifa's view), mosques, and the like, there is no qasamah, and the diyah is paid from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). Abu Yusuf differs on the prison: he says the prisoners/residents should bear qasamah. The text then cites al-Kifayah quoting Shaykh al-Islam regarding a ship: the qasamah is on the passengers if the owner is unknown; if known, on the owner. This is analogous to an animal, but not to a fixed neighborhood or house, because those do not move. The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad for the market, and their view for the prison (no qasamah) is also mufta bihi.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): Collective oath taken by the inhabitants of a locality to deny knowledge of a murder, or to establish liability if they refuse.
دية (diyah): Blood-money paid by the liable party (perpetrator or aqila) to the victim's heirs.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslims, used for diyah when no specific party is liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, who is liable for qasamah when a corpse is found in a privately owned market?
A) The residents of the market
B) The owner of the market
C) The public treasury
D) No one

Key Takeaway
When a corpse is found in an owned location, the owner is liable for qasamah; in unowned public places, the treasury pays diyah; mobile property may shift liability to users if the owner is unknown.

Liability for a Corpse After a Group Fight or in Wilderness
Ruling on liability for corpse after fight and in wilderness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a corpse found after a group fight and distinguish when liability falls on the neighborhood versus when the corpse is h adar.

Core Idea
A corpse found after a fight places liability on the neighborhood, but a corpse in uninhabited wilderness is valueless unless the land is owned.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, we examine the Hanafi rule for assigning liability when a dead body is found after a group conflict. The ruling hinges on the location of the corpse and whether a specific claim is made by the legal guardian. Understanding this distinction is critical for cases of qasamah and diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage addresses two distinct scenarios: a corpse found after a group fight where the combatants withdraw, and a corpse found in a wilderness with no habitation or water nearby.
Each scenario has a different ruling based on the presence of a responsible party.
2
Apply the default rule for the fight scenario
The default ruling is that the liability for qasamah and diyah falls on the people of the neighborhood (ahl al-mahallah). This is because the slain is found among them and they are responsible for protection in their locality.
Even if the apparent killer is an outsider, the difficulty of ascertaining the actual killer shifts liability to the neighborhood based on the apparent cause (qareenah).
3
Analyze the exception: claim by the wali
If the legal guardian (wali) of the deceased makes a specific claim against a particular person or the entire group, the neighborhood is exonerated. However, the accused are not automatically liable; they are only liable if the wali produces evidence (bayyinah) because of the hadith: 'If people were given according to their claims, they would claim lives and wealth of others, but evidence is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier.'
The wali's claim is only proof against himself, not against others.
4
Distinguish wilderness cases
If a corpse is found in an uninhabited wilderness (bariyyah) with no cultivation or water nearby, it is h adar (valueless) – no qasamah or diyah is due. The rationale is that no one is in control or ownership of that land, so no one can be held responsible.
However, if the wilderness is privately owned, then diyah and qasamah are on the aqila (liable group) of the owner.
5
Understand the rationale and conditions
The passage explains that 'nearness' in the wilderness case is defined by the hearing of sound; if help cannot reach, no one is negligent. For the fight scenario, the liability is based on the apparent cause (existence of a corpse in the neighborhood) because the actual killer is unknown.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of assigning liability based on apparent circumstances when certainty is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قوم التقوا بالسيوف وأجلوا عن قتيل على أهل المحلة إلا أن يدعي الولي على القوم أو على معين منهم، فإن وُجِدَ في بريَّةٍ لا عمارة بقربها ، أو ماء يمر به فهدر. (۱) قوله : على أهل المحلة ; لأنّ القتيل بينهم والحفظ عليهم، فإن قيل : إنّ الظاهر أنّ قاتله غير أهل المحلة، فإنّه من خصمائه. أجيب عنه : بأنه قد تعذَّر الوقوف على قاتله حقيقة، فيتعلق بالسبب الظاهر، وهو وجود قتيل في محلّهم. كذا في «العناية). [٢] قوله : إلا أن يدعي الولي.... الخ ; فلم يكن على أهل المحلة شيء; لأنَّ هذه تضمنت براءة أهل المحلة على القسامة، ولا على أولئك حتى يقيموا البينة; لأنه بمجرد الدعوى لا يثبت الحق ; للحديث الذي رواه الترمذي، وهو قوله : «لو يعطى الناس بدعواهم لادّعى قوم دماء قوم وأموالهم، لكن البيئة على المدعي، واليمين على من أنكر ) ، أما يسقط به الحق عن أهل المحلة ; لأن قوله حجة على نفسه لا على غيره. [٣]قوله : بقربها ; وتفسير القرب على ما ذكر استماع الصوت ; لأنه إذا كان بهذه الحالة لا يلحقه الغوث من غيره فلا يوصف أحد بالتقصير، وهذا الحكم إذا لم تكن البرية مملوكة ، أما إذا كانت مملوكة ، فالدية والقسامة على عاقلة المالك. هكذا في «الهداية» (۳). [٤]قوله : فهدر ; لأنّ كلّ واحدٍ ليس في يد أحد ولا في ملك أحد حتى يجب عليه القسامة.
And regarding a people who fought with swords and withdrew leaving a corpse, [liability is] upon the people of the neighborhood, unless the legal guardian makes a claim against the group or a specific individual among them. So if it is found in a wilderness with no cultivation near it, or water passing by, then it is of no value (h adar). [1] His statement: 'upon the people of the neighborhood' because the slain is among them and protection is upon them. If it is said: the apparent situation is that his killer is not from the neighborhood, rather he is from his opponents. The answer is: it is impossible to ascertain the actual killer, so liability is based on the apparent cause, which is the existence of a slain person in their locality. Thus in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'unless the legal guardian claims...' etc.; then there is nothing on the people of the neighborhood, because this implies innocence of the neighborhood from qasamah, and nothing on those individuals until they produce evidence, because a claim alone does not establish a right; due to the hadith narrated by al-Tirmidhi: 'If people were given according to their claims, they would claim lives and wealth of others, but evidence is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier.' As for the right being voided from the neighborhood, it is because his statement is proof against himself, not against others. [3] His statement: 'near it'; the interpretation of 'near' is as mentioned: the hearing of sound, because if it is in this state, help cannot reach from others, so no one is described as negligent. This ruling applies if the wilderness is not owned; but if it is owned, then diyah and qasamah are upon the aqila of the owner. Thus in al-Hidayah. [4] His statement: 'then it is h adar' because every one is not in anyone's hand nor in anyone's ownership to necessitate qasamah upon them.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for a corpse found after a group fight where the combatants disperse leaving a dead body. The default rule: liability (qasamah and diyah) falls on the inhabitants of the neighborhood (ahl al-mahallah) because they are responsible for security in their area. If the legal guardian (wali) of the deceased specifically claims against a particular person or the group, then the neighborhood is exonerated, but the accused are not automatically liable unless they provide evidence (bayanah) or the case follows the hadith principle of evidence being on the claimant. The text then discusses the case of a corpse found in a wilderness (bariyyah) with no nearby habitation or water—this is h adar (valueless), meaning no qasamah or diyah, because no one has control or ownership over that land. However, if the wilderness is privately owned, then the owner's aqila (liable group) bears the qasamah and diyah. The commentary explains the rationale: the hearing of sound determines 'nearness'; if no help can arrive, no one is negligent. The hadith from al-Tirmidhi establishes the legal maxim that claims require evidence.

Key Terms
محلة (mahallah): A neighborhood or locality; the residents collectively responsible for safety in that area.
ولي (wali): The legal guardian of the deceased, who has the right to claim blood-money or prosecute.
هدر (h adar): A term meaning 'valueless' or 'irrecoverable'; here, no diyah or qasamah is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is a corpse found in a wilderness considered 'h adar' (no diyah or qasamah)?
A) When the wilderness is owned by someone
B) When there is no habitation or water nearby
C) When the corpse is from a previous fight
D) When the wali makes a claim

Key Takeaway
Default liability for a corpse found after a fight rests on the neighborhood; only a specific claim with evidence shifts it.

intermediate
8 min
Liability for a Slain Person Found in a Locality or Desert
Ruling with proof on locality liability for diyat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyat when a slain person is found in a locality versus a desert, and explain the condition under which the locality is exonerated.

Core Idea
Liability attaches to the locality unless the guardian proves a specific killer.

This passage from the chapter on diyat addresses a common scenario: after a clash, a slain body is found. Whose responsibility is the blood money? The Hanafi school establishes a default on the locality's 'aqilah, but with important exceptions. We will unpack the ruling, its proof from hadith, and the conditions that shift liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is finding a slain person after a group fight. The default ruling is that the blood money (diyah) is wajib upon the 'aqilah of the locality (mahallah).
This is based on apparent cause: the body is in their area, so they are responsible for protection.
2
Locate the exception when guardian claims
If the guardian (wali) claims against a specific person or the whole group, the locality's liability is not automatically removed. The claim must be supported by evidence (bayyinah) or qasamah to exonerate the locality.
The hadith cited: 'If people were given what they claim...' underlines that a mere claim does not prove guilt.
3
Analyze the desert exemption
If the body is found in a desert (barriyyah) with no habitation nearby, or near a waterway with no owner, the blood is disregarded (hader) – no diyah and no qasamah.
The condition 'near' is defined as beyond ear-shot; then no one can be held negligent.
4
Consider the owned desert exception
If the desert is owned (e.g., private property), liability shifts to the owner's 'aqilah, because the owner is responsible for that land.
This is from al-Hidayah; the passage mentions it as an exception to the exemption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قوم التقوا بالسيوف وأجلوا عن قتيل على أهل المحلة إلا أن يدعي الولي على القوم أو على معين منهم، فإن وُجِدَ في بريَّةٍ لا عمارة بقربها ، أو ماء يمر به فهدر. التقوا بالسيوف وأجلوا عن قتيل : أي انكشفوا عنه. [١] قوله : على أهل المحلة ؛ لأنّ القتيل بينهم والحفظ عليهم، فإن قيل : إنّ الظاهر أنّ قاتله غير أهل المحلة، فإنّه من خصمائه. أجيب عنه : بأنه قد تعذَّر الوقوف على قاتله حقيقة، فيتعلق بالسبب الظاهر، وهو وجود قتيل في محلّهم. كذا في «العناية». [٢] قوله : إلا أن يدعي الولي.... الخ ؛ فلم يكن على أهل المحلة شيء؛ لأنَّ هذه تضمنت براءة أهل المحلة على القسامة، ولا على أولئك حتى يقيموا البينة؛ لأنه بمجرد الدعوى لا يثبت الحق ؛ للحديث الذي رواه الترمذي، وهو قوله : «لو يعطى الناس بدعواهم لادّعى قوم دماء قوم وأموالهم، لكن البيئة على المدعي، واليمين على من أنكر)، أما يسقط به الحق عن أهل المحلة ؛ لأن قوله حجة على نفسه لا على غيره. [٣]قوله : بقربها ؛ وتفسير القرب على ما ذكر استماع الصوت ؛ لأنه إذا كان بهذه الحالة لا يلحقه الغوث من غيره فلا يوصف أحد بالتقصير، وهذا الحكم إذا لم تكن البرية مملوكة ، أما إذا كانت مملوكة ، فالدية والقسامة على عاقلة المالك. هكذا في «الهداية». [٤]قوله : فهدر ؛ لأنّ كلّ واحدٍ ليس في يد أحد ولا في ملك أحد حتى يجب عليه القسامة.
And concerning a group who clashed with swords and then retreated from a slain person, (the responsibility is) upon the people of the locality unless the guardian makes a claim against the group or a specific person among them. If found in a wilderness with no habitation near it, or water that passes by it, then it is disregarded. 'They clashed with swords and retreated from a slain person' means they uncovered him. [1] His saying: 'upon the people of the locality' because the slain person is among them and protection is upon them. If it is said: apparently his killer is not from the people of the locality, for he is one of his adversaries. The answer is: it has become impossible to ascertain the actual killer, so it attaches to the apparent cause, which is the existence of a slain person in their locality. Thus in al-'Inayah. [2] His saying: 'unless the guardian claims...' etc.; then there is nothing upon the people of the locality, because this (claim) would have included the exoneration of the people of the locality through qasamah, and (there is nothing) upon those (accused) until they establish evidence; because the right is not established by mere claim, due to the hadith narrated by al-Tirmidhi, which is his saying: 'If people were given what they claim, they would claim blood of people and their wealth, but the evidence is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the denier.' As for the right being dropped from the people of the locality, because his statement is a proof against himself and not against others. [3] His saying: 'near it'; and the interpretation of 'near' is as mentioned: being within hearing of sound; because if it is in such a state, no one can be described as negligent, as relief cannot reach him from others. This ruling applies if the wilderness is not owned; but if it is owned, then the blood money and qasamah (responsibility) are upon the 'aqilah of the owner. Thus in al-Hidayah. [4] His saying: 'then it is disregarded' because there is no one who has custody or ownership over it such that qasamah would be obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on diyat (blood money) explaining the liability of a locality when a slain person is found after a fight. The matn (base text) states: If a group fights with swords and then retreats, leaving a slain person, the blood money is upon the people of the locality unless the guardian (wali) claims against the group or a specific person. If the body is found in a desert (barriyyah) with no habitation nearby, or near a stream that has no owner, then the blood is disregarded (hader) — no qasamah and no diyah. The sharh (commentary) expands: The liability on the locality is because the slain is among them and they are responsible for protection. If someone objects that the killer might be an outsider, the reply is that since the true killer is unknown, the law attaches to the apparent cause: the presence of the body in their locality. The guardian's claim against a specific person does not automatically exonerate the locality; if the guardian claims, the alleged killer must be exonerated by qasamah or evidence. The hadith of Tirmidhi is cited: 'If people were given what they claim, they would claim blood and wealth; evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier.' This shows that a mere claim does not prove guilt. The commentary defines 'near' as being within earshot, meaning that if help could not arrive, no one is negligent. If the desert is owned, the liability shifts to the owner's 'aqilah. Finally, for water or desert, since there is no owner or custodian, no qasamah applies.

Key Terms
محلة (mahallah): A residential locality or neighborhood where people live; the area where the slain is found.
بريّة (barriyyah): A wilderness or desert with no buildings or inhabitants nearby.
قسامة (qasamah): A procedure involving oaths from the locality to exculpate themselves; if they refuse, they are liable for diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slain person is found in a desert with no habitation nearby, what is the ruling?
A) The nearest town must pay diyah.
B) It is disregarded (hader) with no qasamah or diyah.
C) The guardian must take an oath to determine liability.
D) The owner of the desert, if any, is liable.

Key Takeaway
The default liability for a slain person of unknown killer is on the locality's 'aqilah, unless the body is found in an unowned desert or waterway where it is disregarded.

Oath of the Mustahlaf Who Accuses Another
Ruling on the mustahlaf's oath procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the oath required of a mustahlaf who claims another person killed the victim and explain why his accusation does not exempt him from swearing.

Core Idea
Accusing another of murder does not remove the mustahlaf's own obligation to swear innocence.

In Hanafi qasamah, when a murder victim is found in a locality, the residents must swear innocence. If one resident says 'Zaid killed him,' this does not exonerate him from swearing about himself. The sharh explains the reasoning: the accuser's statement is an attempt to drop litigation, which is not accepted; he must still swear his own innocence and lack of knowledge of any other killer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mustahlaf
The mustahlaf is a person from whom an oath is demanded in qasamah—typically a resident of the locality where the body was found. Understand his role as someone who must swear to deny involvement.
The term 'mustahlaf' is passive (المفعول), indicating he is the one being asked to swear.
2
State the claim he makes
He says: 'Zaid killed him.' His intention is to shift blame and remove litigation from himself. Note that this is an accusation against a specific person.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
The law does not accept his statement as sufficient to drop the oath. He must still swear about himself, because the accusation is not a denial of his own involvement. The sharh says: 'he intends to drop litigation from himself by that statement, so it is not accepted.'
This is a key procedural point: accusations do not replace personal oaths.
4
Derive the exact oath wording
The required oath covers two things: (1) 'By Allah, I did not kill him' (denial of own act), and (2) 'I do not know any killer of him other than Zaid' (acknowledging that Zaid is the only other known suspect).
The oath is comprehensive: it clears the mustahlaf himself and limits the accusation to Zaid.
5
Understand the underlying logic
By confessing that Zaid killed him, the mustahlaf has exempted himself from the oath only regarding Zaid—i.e., he does not need to deny Zaid's involvement. But the ruling for others (including himself) remains: he must still swear that he himself is not guilty.
The principle: an admission about a third party does not extinguish one's own liability to swear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومستحلف قال : قتله زيد حُلف بالله ما قتلتُ ولا عرفت لهُ قاتلاً غير زيد [١] قوله : ومستحلف ; أي رجل من أهل المحلة أو الدار، ممن يطلب منه الحلف على صيغة المفعول، أي إذا قال المستحلف : قتله زيد استحلف بالله ما قتلت ولا عرفت له قاتلاً غير زيد يعني لا يسقط اليمين عنه بقوله : قتله زيد ; لأنه يريد إسقاط الخصومة عن نفسه بذلك القول، فلا يقبل، فيحلف على ما ذكرنا ; لأنه لما أقرّ بالقتل على أحد صار مستثنى عن اليمين، فبقي حكم من سواه فيحلف عليه.
And a person from whom an oath is demanded who says: 'Zaid killed him' is made to swear: 'By Allah, I did not kill him, and I do not know any killer of him other than Zaid.' [1] His saying: 'and a person from whom an oath is demanded' i.e., a man from the people of the locality or house, from whom the oath is sought, in the passive form. That is, if the demanded one says: 'Zaid killed him', he is made to swear: 'By Allah, I did not kill him, and I do not know any killer of him other than Zaid.' This means that the oath is not dropped from him by his statement that Zaid killed him, because he intends to drop the litigation from himself by that statement, so it is not accepted. Rather, he swears as we mentioned, because when he confessed the killing upon someone, he becomes exempt from the oath, but the ruling for others remains, so he swears concerning himself.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a procedural ruling within the rules of qasamah. The matn says: If a person from whom an oath is demanded (mustahlaf) says, 'Zaid killed him,' he is still required to swear: 'By Allah, I did not kill him, and I do not know any killer besides Zaid.' The sharh explains: The mustahlaf is a resident of the locality who is being asked to take an oath of innocence. If he claims that another specific person (Zaid) committed the killing, this does not excuse him from swearing about his own involvement. The reason is that his statement is an attempt to remove litigation from himself, but it is not accepted regarding himself. By confessing that Zaid killed him, he has exempted himself from the oath only with respect to Zaid's share, but he is still required to swear that he himself is not guilty. The oath covers both: his own innocence and his lack of knowledge of any other killer besides Zaid.

Key Terms
مستحلف (mustahlaf): A person from whom an oath is demanded in qasamah proceedings; a resident of the locality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mustahlaf says 'Zaid killed him,' what does he swear?
A) 'By Allah, Zaid killed him.'
B) 'By Allah, I did not kill him and I do not know any killer besides Zaid.'
C) 'By Allah, I am innocent.'
D) He does not need to swear because his statement is accepted.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qasamah, a mustahlaf who accuses another of the killing must still swear that he himself did not kill and that he knows no other killer besides the one he named.

Dispute on Testimony of Locals When Guardian Claims Against Outsiders
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony of locals in qasamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the testimony of locals in qasamah and apply the ruling of Abu Hanifa (rejection) to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Locals are constructive opponents; their testimony is rejected even if the claim is against an outsider.

This passage examines a key procedural dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the admissibility of testimony from members of a locality (mahallah) in qasamah cases. When a guardian claims against an outsider, can locals testify? Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ based on competing analogies. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the school weighs constructive opponents in evidence law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling: testimony of locals is not accepted when the guardian claims against an outsider.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent's view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the testimony is accepted, because the locals' potential to become opponents is removed by the claim against an outsider.
They analogize to a dismissed wakeel.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze the Sahibayn's analogy: a wakeel for litigation, if dismissed before the case, can testify because his opponent status is gone. Similarly, locals' potential to become opponents is removed when the guardian claims against an outsider.
The key point is 'ba-radiyyah an yaseeroo khusamaa' – they were potential opponents.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa's counter-analogy: a wasi (guardian) who exits guardianship (due to child's maturity or dismissal) cannot testify because he was once a guardian and is considered constructively an opponent. Similarly, locals are considered opponents by construction (khusama' taqdeeran) due to their potential negligence; this status is not removed by the claim against an outsider.
The text states: 'وله : أنهم جعلوا خصماء تقديراً'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if two locals testify against an outsider in a qasamah case, Abu Hanifa rejects the testimony, while the Sahibayn accept it.
If the guardian claims against a specific local, there is consensus on rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل شهادة بعض أهل المحلة بقتل غيرهم، أو واحدٍ منهم [١] قوله : وبطل شهادة بعض أهل المحلة... الخ ; أي لا يقبل : أي إذا ادعى المولى على رجل من غير أهل المحلّة، وشهد اثنان منهم عليه، لم تقبل عنده. وقالا : تقبل ; لأنهم كانوا بعرضيّة أن يصيروا خصماء، وقد بطل ذلك بدعواه على غيرهم : كالوكيل بالخصومة إذا عزل قبلها. وله : أنهم جعلوا خصماء تقديراً ; لإنزالهم قائلين للتقصير الصادر منهم، فإن خرجوا من جملة الخصوم فلا تقبل ; كالوصي إذا خرج من الوصاية ببلوغ الغلام أو بالعزل، وتمامه في «العناية»، وغيرها. أما لو ادعى الولي على واحدٍ منهم بعينه لم تقبل شهادتهما عليه إجماعاً، كما في «الملتقى» ; لأنّ الخصومة قائمة مع الكلّ ; لأنّ القسامة لم تسقط عنهم ، قال في «الخيرية»: إلا في رواية ضعيفة عن أبي يوسف لا يعمل بها.
And the testimony of some of the people of the locality is invalid regarding the killing of others, or one of them. [1] His saying: 'And the testimony of some of the people of the locality is invalid...' etc.; i.e., it is not accepted. That is, if the guardian claims against a man not from the people of the locality, and two of them testify against him, it is not accepted according to him (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is accepted, because they were potential (ba-radiyyah) to become opponents, and that has ceased due to his claim against others, like a wakeel for litigation when he is dismissed before it. And for him (Abu Hanifa): They are made opponents constructively (taqdeeran) due to their being considered negligent. If they exit from the category of opponents, their testimony is not accepted, like the wasi when he exits from wasayah due to the boy reaching maturity or dismissal. The rest of the discussion is in al-'Inayah and others. As for if the guardian claims against one specific person among them, their testimony against him is not accepted by consensus, as in al-Multaqa; because the litigation continues with all of them, as qasamah has not fallen from them. He said in al-Khayriyyah: except in a weak narration from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) which is not acted upon.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) regarding the validity of testimony from people of a locality when the guardian (wali) claims the killing against an outsider. The matn states: 'The testimony of some of the people of the locality is invalid concerning the killing of others, or one of them.' The sharh explains: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the guardian claims against a non-local, and two locals testify against that outsider, their testimony is not accepted. The Sahibayn say it is accepted. Their reasoning: The locals were only potential defendants (bi-'ardiyyah an yaseeroo khusamaa') before the claim; once the guardian claims against an outsider, that potential is removed, similar to a wakeel (agent) in litigation who is dismissed before the case begins. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: The locals are constructively considered opponents (khusamaa' taqdeeran) because they are viewed as having been negligent in protecting the slain. If they step out of the category of opponents (by the guardian claiming against another), their testimony is rejected, analogous to a wasi (guardian) who exits from his guardianship when the child reaches maturity or is dismissed. The text notes that if the guardian claims against one specific local, there is consensus that the testimony of other locals against that person is not accepted, because qasamah still binds the group. A weak narration from Abu Yusuf aligns with Abu Hanifa but is not relied upon.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahadah): Judicial testimony given by witnesses in a qasamah or diyah case.
خصماء تقديراً (khusama' taqdeeran): Considered opponents by legal construction, even if not actual litigants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the testimony of locals not accepted when the guardian claims against an outsider?
A) Because they are biased against the outsider.
B) Because they are considered constructive opponents (khusama' taqdeeran).
C) Because they did not witness the killing.
D) Because the guardian's claim is against them as well.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qasamah, locals are treated as constructive opponents; their testimony is rejected even when the claim is against an outsider, based on analogy to a wasi.

The Mustahlaf's Oath and Residents' Testimony in Qasama
Ruling on oath and testimony in qasama cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the mustahlaf's accusation does not exempt him from the oath, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute over residents' testimony against outsiders.

Core Idea
Accusing another does not drop your own oath; residents remain potential opponents in qasama.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses two nuanced rulings in the law of qasama (collective oath). First, when a defendant (mustahlaf) names another person as the killer, his oath is not waived—he must still swear that he did not kill and knows no other killer. Second, a key intra-Hanafi dispute arises: if residents of a neighborhood testify against a non-resident defendant, is their testimony accepted? Abu Hanifa rejects it, while the Sahibayn accept it. Understanding the reasoning behind each position is essential for applying qasama correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
The passage covers: (a) the oath requirement when the mustahlaf accuses another, and (b) the acceptance of residents' testimony against outsiders.
2
Analyze the mustahlaf's position
Explain why his accusation does not exempt him: he intends to drop the claim, but he only excludes the named killer from the oath; for others, the oath remains.
He is sworn: 'I did not kill and I do not know a killer other than Zayd.'
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's position on testimony
He rejects residents' testimony because they are considered potential opponents (khutum) by estimation (taqdiran). Analogy: like an executor removed from executorship.
Residents could be accused of negligence; thus they are opponents in effect.
4
Examine the Sahibayn's position
They accept the testimony because the potential opposition is nullified when the claim is against an outsider. Analogy: like a removed agent in litigation.
Once the claim is on an outsider, the residents' conflict of interest is gone.
5
Evaluate the tarjih for fatwa
Later scholars often prefer the Sahibayn's view due to public harm (rebels taking advantage), but some uphold Abu Hanifa's view as the original position in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The mufta bihi is generally the Sahibayn's view, though there is scholarly disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومستحلف قال : قتله زيد حُلف بالله ما قتلتُ ولا عرفت له قاتلاً غير زيد
وبطل شهادة بعض أهل المحلة بقتل غيرهم، أو واحد منهم زيد.
[1] قوله : ومستحلف ؛ أي رجل من أهل المحلة أو الدار، ممن يطلب منه الحلف على صيغة المفعول، أي إذا قال المستحلف : قتله زيد استحلف بالله ما قتلت ولا عرفت له قاتلاً غير زيد يعني لا يسقط اليمين عنه بقوله : قتله زيد ؛ لأنه يريد إسقاط الخصومة عن نفسه بذلك القول، فلا يقبل، فيحلف على ما ذكرنا ؛ لأنه لما أقرّ بالقتل على أحد صار مستثنى عن اليمين، فبقي حكم من سواه فيحلف عليه. [2) قوله : وبطل شهادة بعض أهل المحلة... الخ ؛ أي لا يقبل : أي إذا ادعى المولى على رجل من غير أهل المحلّة، وشهد اثنان منهم عليه، لم تقبل عنده. وقالا : تقبل ؛ لأنهم كانوا بعرضيّة أن يصيروا خصماء، وقد بطل ذلك بدعواه على غيرهم : كالوكيل بالخصومة إذا عزل قبلها. وله : أنهم جعلوا خصماء تقديراً ؛ لإنزالهم قائلين للتقصير الصادر منهم، فإن خرجوا من جملة الخصوم فلا تقبل ؛ كالوصي إذا خرج من الوصاية ببلوغ الغلام أو بالعزل، وتمامه في «العناية»)، وغيرها. أما لو ادعى الولي على واحدٍ منهم بعينه لم تقبل شهادتهما عليه إجماعاً، كما في «الملتقى» ؛ لأنّ الخصومة قائمة مع الكلّ ؛ لأنّ القسامة لم تسقط عنهم ، قال في «الخيرية»: إلا في رواية ضعيفة عن أبي يوسف الله لا يعمل بها. نقل الحموي عن المقدسي أنه قال : توقفت عن الفتوى القول الإمام الا الله ، ومنعت من إشاعته لما يترتب عليه من الضرر العام فإنّ من عرفه من المتمردين يتجاسر على قتل الأنفس في المحلات الخالية عن غير أهلها معتمداً على عدم قبول شهادتهم قلت : ينبغي الفتوى على قولهما، لا سيما والأحكام تختلف باختلاف الأيام، وقد خير المفتي إذا كان الصاحبان متفقين، وتمامه في «حاشية الرحمتي». لكن في «تصحيح العلامة قاسم : إن الصحيح قول الإمام على أن الضرر المذكور موجود في المسألة الثانية أيضاً، وقد علمت الاتفاق فيها إلا في رواية ضعيفة، نعم القلب يميل إلى ما ذكر، ولكن اتباع النقل أسلم. كذا في رد المحتار).
And one asked to swear said: Zayd killed him; he is sworn by Allah: I did not kill and I do not know a killer other than Zayd. And the testimony of some residents of the neighborhood against others, or one of them against Zayd, is invalidated. [1] His statement: 'And one asked to swear' means a man from the residents of the neighborhood or house, from whom the oath is demanded, in the passive voice, i.e., when the one asked to swear says: Zayd killed him, he is sworn by Allah: I did not kill and I do not know a killer other than Zayd. This means the oath is not dropped by his statement 'Zayd killed him' because he intends to drop the claim against himself by that statement, so it is not accepted; thus he swears as we mentioned, because when he confessed the killing upon one person, he became exempted from the oath regarding that person, so the ruling regarding others remains, and he swears upon that. [2] His statement: 'And the testimony of some residents of the neighborhood...' etc. means it is not accepted: i.e., when the guardian claims against a person not from the neighborhood, and two of them testify against him, it is not accepted according to him (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is accepted, because they were subject to becoming opponents, and that is nullified by his claim against others, like the agent in litigation if he is removed before it. And for him (Abu Hanifa): they have been made opponents by estimation, because they are treated as saying the negligence that came from them; so if they exit from the group of opponents, their testimony is not accepted, like the executor when he exits from executorship by the boy reaching puberty or by removal. Its completion is in al-‘Inayah and others. As for if the guardian claims against one particular person from them, their testimony against him is not accepted by consensus, as in al-Multaqa, because the litigation remains with all, because the qasama is not dropped from them. He said in al-Khayriyyah: except in a weak narration from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) which is not acted upon. Al-Hamawi narrated from al-Maqdisi that he said: I refrained from giving fatwa according to the Imam’s (Abu Hanifa) saying, and I prevented its dissemination because of the general harm that results from it, for whoever knows it from the rebellious becomes bold to kill people in neighborhoods empty of other residents, relying on the non-acceptance of their testimony. I said: the fatwa should be according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), especially since rulings differ with the difference of times, and the mufti is given a choice when the two companions agree. Its completion is in Hashiyat al-Rahmati. But in Tashih al-Allamah Qasim: the correct is the Imam’s saying, that the mentioned harm also exists in the second case, and you have known the consensus there except in a weak narration. Yes, the heart inclines to what was mentioned, but following transmitted knowledge is safer. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute covering two related rulings in qasama. First, the ruling on a defendant (mustahlaf) who claims another person killed: his claim does not exempt him from swearing an oath that he did not kill and knows no other killer except the one named. The commentary explains that this is because he is trying to drop the claim against himself by shifting blame; his confession on one person only exempts him from the oath regarding that person, but he must still swear regarding all others. Second, the ruling on the testimony of residents of a neighborhood where a body is found: according to Abu Hanifa, if the guardian sues an outsider, the testimony of two residents against that outsider is rejected because the residents are considered potential opponents (khutum). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad accept such testimony, analogizing to a removed agent. The commentary then discusses the preferred position for fatwa, noting that later scholars often adopt the view of the two companions due to practical harm, but some still maintain Abu Hanifa's view. The discussion ends with a note that the mufti may choose between the two views when the two companions agree.

Key Terms
مستحلف (mustahlaf): The person who is asked to swear an oath in a qasama case, typically a resident of the locality where the body is found.
قسامة (qasama): A collective oath taken by residents of a locality to deny responsibility for a killing, or to establish liability.
خصم (khasm): Opponent or adversary in litigation; a person who has a direct interest in the case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the testimony of residents against a non-resident in qasama?
A) Both agree it is accepted.
B) Both agree it is rejected.
C) Abu Hanifa accepts, Sahibayn reject.
D) Abu Hanifa rejects, Sahibayn accept.

Key Takeaway
The mustahlaf's oath remains even if he accuses another, and the testimony of residents is subject to intra-Hanafi dispute over their status as opponents.
