Qasama and Diya When a Wounded Person Is Moved and Dies Bedridden
Ruling on qasama and diya when a wounded person is moved and later dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on qasama when a wounded person is moved and remains bedridden, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Bedridden from wound to death ties liability to the original locality; recovery severs it.

When a person is wounded in one area and then dies in another after being bedridden, who bears the qasama and diya? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the core condition of 'remaining bedridden' (dha firash) and the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principles of qasama and the significance of continuous causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central case and ruling
State the scenario: a person wounded in one locality (al-hayy) is moved, remains bedridden until death. What is the ruling on qasama and diya?
The ruling is given as a conditional: if bedridden, then on the locality.
2
Extract the key condition: dha firash
Note why the author restricted the ruling to 'dha firash'. What is the alternative scenario and its consequence?
If the victim recovers and later dies elsewhere, no liability on the original locality.
3
Compare the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa holds qasama and diya on the living area. Abu Yusuf says no qasama and no diya. Identify the basis of each view.
Abu Yusuf's reason may be that movement severs connection; Abu Hanifa sees continuous confinement as linking death to the wound.
4
Identify the mufta bihi and authority
Which position is relied upon? The commentary implies Abu Hanifa's view is the established one (Zahir al-Riwayah).
Later fatwa works follow Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن جُرح في حي فنقل فبقي ذا فراش حتى مات فالقسامة والدية على الحي
[1] قوله : فبقي ذا فراش ؛ إنّما قيّد به ؛ لأنه لو كان صحيحاً يحيى أو يذهب حين جرح ثم مات في أهله، لا شيء فيه. كذا في «المبسوط». هكذا في «الكفاية». ٢ قوله : على الحي؛ هذا قول أبي حنيفة لله ، وقال أبو يوسف : لا قسامة ولا دية فيه، وتمامه في «الهداية».
And whoever is wounded in a living area and then moved, and remains bedridden until death, then qasama and diya are upon the living area. [1] His statement: 'remains bedridden' — it is restricted by this because if he was healthy and lived or went when wounded and then died in his own home, there is nothing upon it. Thus in al-Mabsut. Thus in al-Kifayah. [2] His statement: 'upon the living area' — this is the saying of Abu Hanifa. And Abu Yusuf said: there is no qasama and no diya in it. Its completion is in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on qasama when a person is wounded in one locality (the 'living area' where the incident occurs) and is then moved to another place, remaining bedridden until death. The key condition is that the victim remains bedridden (dha firash) continuously from the wound until death; if he recovers and later dies, the ruling does not apply. According to Abu Hanifa, qasama and diya are imposed on the original locality (the 'living area') because the wound and initial confinement are there. Abu Yusuf disagrees, ruling that no qasama or diya is due in this case, presumably because the movement severs the connection. The commentary notes this ikhtilaf and refers to fuller discussions in al-Mabsut, al-Kifayah, and al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
ذا فراش (dha firash): Bedridden; condition where the victim is confined to bed due to the wound until death.
قسامة (qasama): Oath taking by the locality to deny liability or by the victim's family to claim blood money.
دية (diya): Blood money paid to the victim's family in lieu of retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, on whom is qasama and diya when a wounded person is moved and remains bedridden until death?
A) On the place where he died.
B) On the original living area where he was wounded.
C) On both localities equally.
D) Neither; no qasama or diya.

Key Takeaway
When a wounded person is moved and remains bedridden until death, the Hanafi mufta bihi places qasama and diya on the original locality where the wound occurred.

Diya When One of Two Men Alone in a House Is Killed
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diya liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on diya when one of two men alone in a house is found killed, and explain the legal basis (al-zahir vs. possibility) for each position.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf presumes no suicide so the survivor pays; Muhammad doubts suicide so no payment.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding liability for diya when two people are alone and one is found dead. Abu Yusuf applies the apparent presumption (al-zahir) that a person does not kill himself, while Muhammad considers the possibility of suicide or a third party to create doubt. Understanding this ikhtilaf illustrates how legal presumptions and doubt affect liability in Hanafi criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing Hanafi positions
State the ruling of Abu Yusuf (the survivor guarantees the diya) and the ruling of Muhammad al-Shaybani (the survivor does not guarantee the diya). Note that both are recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is a purely intra-Hanafi disagreement; no other madhhab is involved.
2
Examine the proof of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf argues that the apparent (al-zahir) is that a person does not kill himself. Therefore, the death must be attributed to the other man, making him liable for the diya. This is a rational presumption based on common experience.
Al-zahir is a Hanafi usul term referring to a default assumption that remains valid until contrary evidence appears.
3
Examine the proof of Muhammad
Muhammad al-Shaybani argues that there is a possibility (ihtimal) that the deceased killed himself or that a third party was involved. Since liability cannot be established beyond doubt, the survivor is not liable. Certainty is required for imposing financial liability.
Muhammad's reasoning reflects a stricter standard for proof in criminal liability.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference is whether the survivor must pay diya or not. Under Abu Yusuf's view, he pays; under Muhammad's view, he does not. The dispute directly affects the financial outcome.
This is a clear example of how a difference in legal presumption leads to a different ruling.
5
Note the authority and practice
The text does not specify a tarjih, but later Hanafi practice often relies on Abu Yusuf's view, as it aligns with the general principle of qasama (oath and presumptions). However, both opinions are valid within the school.
A mufti may choose either opinion depending on circumstances, but the stronger view is often Abu Yusuf's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي رجلين في بيت بلا ثالث وجد أحدهما قتيلاً ضَمِنَ الآخرُ ديتَهُ عند أبي يوسف، خلافا لمحمد ) ، فإنَّه لا يضمن عنده لاحتمال أنه قتل نفسه ، ولأبي يوسف له : أنَّ الظاهر أنَّ الإنسانَ لا يقتلُ نفسَه
And regarding two men in a house with no third, one of them found killed, the other guarantees his diya according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, for he does not guarantee according to him due to the possibility that he killed himself. And for Abu Yusuf: the apparent is that a person does not kill himself.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where two men are alone in a house and one is found dead. Abu Yusuf rules that the surviving man must pay the diya (blood money) because the default assumption is that a person does not commit suicide; thus the death was likely caused by the other. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, reasoning that the possibility of suicide or a third party exists, so liability cannot be established with certainty, and therefore no diya is due. The ikhtilaf here is purely intra-Hanafi, and the commentary notes Abu Yusuf's argument based on the general practice (al-zahir) that people do not kill themselves. This case is analogous to the general qasama principle but with only two individuals.

Key Terms
ظاهر (zahir): The apparent or default assumption; used by Abu Yusuf to argue that people do not kill themselves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the other man does not guarantee diya?
A) Because the killer is unknown.
B) Because of the possibility that the deceased killed himself.
C) Because the house rules exempt him.
D) Because Abu Yusuf is always wrong.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when two men are alone and one is found killed, Abu Yusuf presumes the survivor is liable, while Muhammad rejects liability due to doubt.

Ruling on Qasama for a Body Found in a Woman's Village
Ruling on repetition of oath when a body is found in a village owned by a woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasama when a body is found in a village owned by a woman and identify who must swear the oath.

Core Idea
If a body is found in a woman's village, she must swear the repeated oath — ownership triggers qasama liability.

In Hanafi law, qasama is a procedure used when a slain person is found in a locality and the killer is unknown. The oath is imposed on the residents of that locality. This short passage clarifies that when the locality is a village owned by a woman, she is the one upon whom the oath is repeated. This teaches us that ownership of the land determines the party responsible for the qasama oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Recognise the scenario: a killed person is found in a village that belongs to a woman.
The village is in her ownership (milk).
2
Recall the general qasama rule
Qasama requires the oath to be sworn by the residents of the locality where the body is found.
The oath is repeated fifty times (kuwwira).
3
Apply to the woman as owner
Since the woman owns the village, she is considered the resident for liability purposes, so the oath is repeated upon her.
This is a specific application of the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قتيل قرية امرأة كُوِّرَ الحلف عليها
[4] قوله : قرية امرأة ؛ أي قرية هي في ملك المرأة).
And regarding a killed person in a woman's village, the oath is repeated upon her. [4] His statement: 'a woman's village' means a village that is in the ownership of the woman.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if a dead body is found in a village that belongs to a woman, the qasama oath is administered repeatedly to her. The commentary explains that 'a woman's village' means a village owned by the woman. This implies that the woman is considered the responsible party (like the residents) for the purposes of qasama. The ruling is straightforward and not qualified here further. It likely relates to the fact that the owner of the land is treated as the resident for liability.

Key Terms
كُوِّرَ (kuwwira): The oath is repeated or intensified; in qasama, it refers to administering the oath multiple times (e.g., fifty oaths).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a body found in a village owned by a woman, what is the ruling regarding the oath?
A) No oath is required.
B) The oath is sworn by the residents, not the woman.
C) The oath is repeated upon the woman.
D) The woman pays diya directly.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qasama, when a body is found in a village owned by a woman, the oath is repeated upon her as the owner, establishing her as the responsible party.

Liability When a Wounded Person is Moved to Another Quarter and Dies Bedridden
Ruling and dispute on qasamah for moved victim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasamah and diyah when a wounded person is moved to another quarter and dies bedridden, and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
If a wounded person dies in the quarter he was moved to, the quarter bears qasamah and diyah—except according to Abu Yusuf who says no.

This lesson examines the first of three scenarios from the source: a person wounded in one quarter is moved to another and remains bedridden until death. The Hanafi school splits on whether the new quarter is liable. Understanding the condition of 'bedridden' is key to distinguishing when liability attaches.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act
Recognize that the act is a homicide where the victim dies after being moved to a different quarter. The ruling concerns whether the new quarter is liable for qasamah (oaths) and diyah (compensation).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its condition
Abu Hanifa holds that the quarter where the victim dies bedridden is liable. The critical condition is that the victim 'remained bedridden' (bāqī dhā firāsh). If he had been healthy and walked away, there would be no liability.
The condition is derived from the phrase 'فبقي ذا فراش'.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf rejects liability entirely: no qasamah and no diyah on the quarter. His reasoning is that the original injury did not occur in that quarter, so the new quarter cannot be held responsible.
The text refers to al-Hidayah for his full argument.
4
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the inhabitants of the quarter must swear fifty oaths and pay diyah. Under Abu Hanifa, they do; under Abu Yusuf, they do not.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa, as later works like al-Multaqa and al-Zayla'i adopt it.
This is stated in the margin of the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
ومَن جُرح في حي فنقل فبقي ذا فراش حتى مات فالقسامة والدية على الحي، وفي رجلين في بيت بلا ثالث، وجد أحدهما قتيلاً ضَمِنَ الآخِرُ ديتَهُ عند أبي يوسف، خلافاً لمحمد، وفي قتيل قرية امرأة كُوِّرَ الحلف عليها
قوله : فبقي ذا فراش؛ إنّما قيّد به؛ لأنه لو كان صحيحاً يحيى أو يذهب حين جرح ثم مات في أهله، لا شيء فيه. كذا في «المبسوط». هكذا في «الكفاية». قوله : على الحي؛ هذا قول أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف: لا قسامة ولا دية فيه، وتمامه في «الهداية». قوله : لاحتمال أنه قتل نفسه؛ أو قتله غيرُه، فلا يضمن الآخر بالشك. قوله : لا يقتل نفسه؛ فكان التوهم ساقطاً، وصار كما إذا وجد قتيل في محلة. قوله : قرية امرأة؛ أي قرية هي في ملك المرأة.
وتدي عاقلتها، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وعند أبي يوسف: القسامة على العاقلة أيضاً؛ لأنَّ القسامة على أهل النصرة والمرأة ليست من أهلها. قوله : وتدى عاقلتها؛ أي أقرب القبائل إليها نسباً لا جواراً، قال المتأخرون: والمرأة تدخلُ في التحمّل مع العاقلة في هذه المسألة. كذا في «الملتقى»، وهو الأصح. ذكره الزيلعي وإنما قيد بقوله في هذه المسألة؛ لأنَّ المرأة لا تدخلُ في العواقل في تحمّل الدِّيةِ في صورة من الصور على ما يجيء في المعاقل، وتدخل في هذه المسألة؛ لأنا جعلناها قاتلة، والقاتلة تشارك العاقلة؛ لأنها لما وجبت على غير المباشر فعلى المباشر أولى. وموضوع المسألة فيما إذا وجد قتيل في دار امرأة في مصر ليس فيه من عشيرتها أحد، أما إذا كانت عشيرتها تدخلُ معها في القسامة. اهـ. «كفاية». قوله : والمرأة ليست من أهلها؛ لضعف بنيتها؛ ولهذا لا يلزمها القسامة فيما يوجد في المحلة، ولهما: إنّ الاستخلاف لتهمة القتل، وهي متحققة في حق المرأة، لا في حق العاقلة؛ لأنهم لم يكونوا في القربة فيلزمها القسامة.
Benefit of al-Kifayah for the end of Sharh al-Wiqayah. Whoever is wounded in a residential quarter and then moved and remained bedridden until death: the qasamah and the diyah are upon the quarter. And regarding two men in a house with no third, one of them is found killed: the other must pay his diyah according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. And regarding a killed person in a woman's village: the oath is repeated upon her. His saying: 'remained bedridden' – it is only restricted by this because if he was healthy, he would live or go when wounded and then die in his family, there is nothing upon him. Thus in al-Mabsut. Thus in al-Kifayah. His saying: 'upon the quarter' – this is the statement of Abu Hanifa, and Abu Yusuf said: no qasamah and no diyah upon him, and its completion is in al-Hidayah. His saying: 'due to the possibility that he killed himself' – or someone else killed him, so the other is not liable due to doubt. His saying: 'he does not kill himself' – so the doubt is dropped, and it becomes like when a killed person is found in a quarter. His saying: 'a woman's village' – meaning a village that is in the ownership of the woman. And (his saying): and her aqilah pays the diyah – this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and according to Abu Yusuf: the qasamah is also upon the aqilah because qasamah is upon the people of support, and the woman is not from them. His saying: 'and her aqilah pays the diyah' – meaning the closest tribes to her by lineage, not by neighborhood. The later scholars said: the woman enters into bearing (the diyah) with the aqilah in this case. Thus in al-Multaqa, and it is the most correct. Al-Zayla'i mentioned it. And he restricted it by his saying 'in this case' because the woman does not enter into the aqilah in bearing the diyah in any situation as will come in the chapter of aqilah, and she enters in this case because we have made her a killer, and the killer shares with the aqilah because when (the diyah) is obligated upon the non-direct (actor), then it is even more so upon the direct (actor). The subject of the case is when a killed person is found in the house of a woman in a city where there is no one from her tribe. However, if her tribe enters with her in the qasamah. End from al-Kifayah. His saying: 'and the woman is not from them (i.e., the people of support)' – due to the weakness of her constitution; for this reason, qasamah is not required of her regarding what is found in the quarter. And for them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the appointment of a successor is due to the accusation of killing, and that is established in the case of the woman, not in the case of the aqilah because they were not in proximity, so qasamah is required of her.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the application of qasamah in three distinct scenarios. First: if a person is wounded in a village/quarter and then is moved and remains bedridden until death, according to Abu Hanifa the qasamah and diyah are upon the villagers. This is because the victim was in their midst and they are responsible for protecting him. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that no qasamah or diyah is due because the original wounding did not occur in the village after the move. The qualification 'remained bedridden' is crucial: if the victim had been healthy and went elsewhere before dying, there would be no liability on the villagers. Second: two men alone in a house, one found dead. Abu Yusuf holds the survivor liable for the diyah because the apparent fact is that a person does not kill himself, so the other must have done it. Muhammad rejects this because there is a possibility of suicide or an unknown third party, and liability cannot be established by doubt. Third: a dead body found in a village owned by a woman; the oaths are repeated on her, i.e., she is required to swear 50 oaths to deny responsibility. On the issue of who pays the diyah (if she does not swear), Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say her aqilah (male paternal relatives) pay. Abu Yusuf argues that the aqilah should also take the qasamah because qasamah is meant for those who can support the accused, and a woman is weak and not capable of bearing arms. However, the later scholars explain that the woman is considered a direct killer in this context because the body was found in her exclusive domain, so she must contribute to the diyah alongside her aqilah. The ruling applies specifically when the woman lives in a city where no one from her tribe resides; if her tribe is present, they share in the qasamah.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasamah): A procedure of fifty oaths taken in cases of homicide where there is evidence but no clear witness, used to establish liability for diyah.
الدية (al-diyah): Monetary compensation paid to the heirs of a homicide victim, equivalent to 100 camels or their value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what condition is necessary for the quarter to which a wounded person is moved to be liable for qasamah and diyah?
A) The victim must die immediately upon arrival.
B) The victim must remain bedridden until death.
C) The wound must be inflicted in the same quarter.
D) The victim must be a resident of that quarter.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, if a wounded person is moved to a quarter and remains bedridden until death, that quarter must perform qasamah and pay diyah, but this is disputed by Abu Yusuf who denies any liability.

Dispute on Liability When One of Two Men is Found Dead in a House
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability of survivor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding liability for diyah when two men are alone in a house and one is found dead, and apply the principle that liability cannot be established by doubt.

Core Idea
If two men are alone and one dies, Abu Yusuf holds the survivor liable; Muhammad rejects it due to possibility of suicide or unknown killer.

This lesson addresses the second scenario from the source: two men are alone in a house, and one is found dead. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on whether the survivor must pay diyah. Understanding their arguments sheds light on how Hanafi jurists weigh presumptions against doubt in homicide cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two men are alone in a house with no third person. One is found killed (قتيل). The issue is whether the other must pay the diyah.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position and proof
Abu Yusuf holds the survivor liable. His proof is the principle: الظاهر أن الإنسان لا يقتل نفسه (the apparent fact is that a person does not kill himself). Therefore, the other must have killed him, and thus must pay diyah.
This is a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī).
3
State Muhammad's position and proof
Muhammad rejects liability. His proof: because it is possible (iḥtimāl) that the dead man killed himself or that a third party entered and killed him. Liability cannot be established by doubt (الشك لا يثبت به الحكم).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Muhammad
The text responds to Muhammad's doubt by saying: the doubt is dropped because the 'apparent fact' (zāhir) is that a person does not kill himself. So it becomes like a body found in a residential quarter (where the quarter is liable).
The response is: 'فكان التوهم ساقطاً، وصار كما إذا وجد قتيل في محلة'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later fatwa prefers the view of both disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) together, meaning no liability on the survivor, because the doubt is not fully resolved. This is noted in the margin (Radd al-Muhtar).
The main text does not give tarjih; the footnote is cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
ومَن جُرح في حي فنقل فبقي ذا فراش حتى مات فالقسامة والدية على الحي، وفي رجلين في بيت بلا ثالث، وجد أحدهما قتيلاً ضَمِنَ الآخِرُ ديتَهُ عند أبي يوسف، خلافاً لمحمد، وفي قتيل قرية امرأة كُوِّرَ الحلف عليها
قوله : فبقي ذا فراش؛ إنّما قيّد به؛ لأنه لو كان صحيحاً يحيى أو يذهب حين جرح ثم مات في أهله، لا شيء فيه. كذا في «المبسوط». هكذا في «الكفاية». قوله : على الحي؛ هذا قول أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف: لا قسامة ولا دية فيه، وتمامه في «الهداية». قوله : لاحتمال أنه قتل نفسه؛ أو قتله غيرُه، فلا يضمن الآخر بالشك. قوله : لا يقتل نفسه؛ فكان التوهم ساقطاً، وصار كما إذا وجد قتيل في محلة. قوله : قرية امرأة؛ أي قرية هي في ملك المرأة.
وتدي عاقلتها، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وعند أبي يوسف: القسامة على العاقلة أيضاً؛ لأنَّ القسامة على أهل النصرة والمرأة ليست من أهلها. قوله : وتدى عاقلتها؛ أي أقرب القبائل إليها نسباً لا جواراً، قال المتأخرون: والمرأة تدخلُ في التحمّل مع العاقلة في هذه المسألة. كذا في «الملتقى»، وهو الأصح. ذكره الزيلعي وإنما قيد بقوله في هذه المسألة؛ لأنَّ المرأة لا تدخلُ في العواقل في تحمّل الدِّيةِ في صورة من الصور على ما يجيء في المعاقل، وتدخل في هذه المسألة؛ لأنا جعلناها قاتلة، والقاتلة تشارك العاقلة؛ لأنها لما وجبت على غير المباشر فعلى المباشر أولى. وموضوع المسألة فيما إذا وجد قتيل في دار امرأة في مصر ليس فيه من عشيرتها أحد، أما إذا كانت عشيرتها تدخلُ معها في القسامة. اهـ. «كفاية». قوله : والمرأة ليست من أهلها؛ لضعف بنيتها؛ ولهذا لا يلزمها القسامة فيما يوجد في المحلة، ولهما: إنّ الاستخلاف لتهمة القتل، وهي متحققة في حق المرأة، لا في حق العاقلة؛ لأنهم لم يكونوا في القربة فيلزمها القسامة.
Benefit of al-Kifayah for the end of Sharh al-Wiqayah. Whoever is wounded in a residential quarter and then moved and remained bedridden until death: the qasamah and the diyah are upon the quarter. And regarding two men in a house with no third, one of them is found killed: the other must pay his diyah according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. And regarding a killed person in a woman's village: the oath is repeated upon her. His saying: 'remained bedridden' – it is only restricted by this because if he was healthy, he would live or go when wounded and then die in his family, there is nothing upon him. Thus in al-Mabsut. Thus in al-Kifayah. His saying: 'upon the quarter' – this is the statement of Abu Hanifa, and Abu Yusuf said: no qasamah and no diyah upon him, and its completion is in al-Hidayah. His saying: 'due to the possibility that he killed himself' – or someone else killed him, so the other is not liable due to doubt. His saying: 'he does not kill himself' – so the doubt is dropped, and it becomes like when a killed person is found in a quarter. His saying: 'a woman's village' – meaning a village that is in the ownership of the woman. His saying: 'and her aqilah pays the diyah' – meaning the closest tribes to her by lineage, not by neighborhood. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the application of qasamah in three distinct scenarios. First: if a person is wounded in a village/quarter and then is moved and remains bedridden until death, according to Abu Hanifa the qasamah and diyah are upon the villagers. Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when two men are alone and one dies, the survivor is not liable for diyah because doubt about the cause of death precludes liability, although Abu Yusuf argued otherwise based on presumption.

Qasamah and Diyah for a Dead Body Found in a Woman's Village
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qasamah and aqilah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rulings on qasamah and diyah when a dead body is found in a woman's village, and differentiate the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on who must swear oaths and who pays diyah.

Core Idea
If a dead body is found in a woman's village, she must swear 50 oaths; her aqilah pays diyah if she fails, but Abu Yusuf requires the aqilah to also swear.

The third scenario in the source involves a dead body found in a village owned by a woman. The Hanafi imams disagree on whether the woman must perform qasamah alone or whether her aqilah (male relatives) must also take the oath. This lesson breaks down the positions and the reasoning behind the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A dead body is found in a village owned by a woman. The question is who must perform qasamah and who pays diyah.
The village is in her exclusive ownership (قرية هي في ملك المرأة).
2
State Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position
They hold that the woman must repeat the oath (الحلف عليها) alone. If she fails to swear or refuses, her aqilah (relatives by lineage, not neighbors) must pay the diyah. Qasamah is not required from the aqilah.
The woman is considered the direct 'killer' because the body was found in her domain.
3
State Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that the qasamah must also be performed by the aqilah because qasamah is upon the 'people of support' (أهل النصرة) – those capable of bearing arms and defending. The woman is weak and not from them, so her tribe must support her in the oath.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirūn) respond: the woman is considered a killer in this context because the body was in her exclusive control. Therefore, she must bear the oath, and the aqilah only pays diyah – they do not swear because they were not proximate to the crime.
The text says: 'والقاتلة تشارك العاقلة' (the killer shares with the aqilah).
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its condition
The relied-upon position (al-mu'tamad) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the woman swears, and her aqilah pays diyah. However, this applies only when her tribe is not present in the city. If her tribe lives there, they share in the qasamah.
The restriction is explicitly mentioned in al-Kifayah: 'إذا كانت عشيرتها تدخل معها في القسامة'.

Diyah and Qasama for a Woman Found Killed: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qasama for a woman's 'aqila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah and qasama for a woman found killed in her house, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
For a woman found dead alone, her 'aqila pays diyah, but qasama is not required from her or them – except in Abu Yusuf's view.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: when a woman is found dead in her house in a city where she has no relatives, who pays the blood money and who must take the oath of denial? The matn rules that her 'aqila pays the diyah, but the commentators disagree on whether the 'aqila must also perform qasama. This disagreement reveals deeper principles about the nature of qasama, the role of women in legal responsibility, and the relationship between direct and indirect liability.

Guided Path
1
State the agreed ruling
All Hanafi scholars agree that the 'aqila (distant relatives by lineage) pay the diyah for a woman found killed in her house, as stated in the matn 'watadi 'aaqilatuha'.
The 'aqila is defined as the closest relatives by lineage, not by neighborhood.
2
Identify the point of dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that qasama (the oath of denial) is not required from the 'aqila. Abu Yusuf holds that qasama is required from the 'aqila as well.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, involving the two main companions of Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the proof of the majority
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that qasama is only required from 'ahl al-nusra' (people capable of giving assistance), and women are not from that category due to physical weakness. Since the woman herself cannot be required, her 'aqila also are not required.
The weakness of the woman's constitution (da'f binyatiha) is cited as the reason.
4
Analyze the proof of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf argues that qasama is based on suspicion of killing (tuhmat al-qatl). That suspicion is realized in the woman because she was near the scene (in the house), not in the 'aqila who were absent. Therefore, the qasama should be on her, but since she cannot perform it, it falls on her 'aqila by substitution (istikhlaf).
Thus, Abu Yusuf's illah is the presence of suspicion, not the capacity for assistance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the tarjih
The practical difference: if the 'aqila must perform qasama, they must produce fifty oath-takers to deny involvement; if not, they only pay the diyah. Later Hanafi scholars (al-Mutakhkhirin) ruled that the woman enters into bearing the diyah with her 'aqila in this specific case, and qasama is not required, following the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This is the mufta bihi position according to al-Multaqa and al-Zayla'i.

[1] His statement: 'her 'aqila pays' – meaning the closest tribes to her by lineage, not by neighborhood. He only restricted it with 'in this issue' because the woman does not enter into the 'awaqil (plural of 'aqila) in bearing the diyah in any other instance as will come in the book of Ma'aqil, but she enters in this issue because we consider her a killer, and the killer shares with the 'aqila; because when it is due upon the indirect (i.e., the 'aqila), upon the direct (the killer) it is more primary. The subject of this issue is when a slain person is found in the house of a woman in a city where there is none of her relatives. As for when her relatives are present, they enter with her in the qasama. End from Kifaya. Likewise in Radd al-Muhtar (3). (2) [2] His statement: 'the woman is not from them' – due to the weakness of her constitution; and for this reason qasama is not required of her regarding what is found in the neighborhood. And for them (Abu Yusuf): Indeed the substitution (istikhlaf) is due to suspicion of killing, and that is realized in the case of the woman, not in the case of the 'aqila, because they were not near (the scene), hence qasama is required of her. Likewise in al-Jalabi, quoting from al-'Inaya.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific scenario: a woman is found killed in her house in a city where she has no relatives. The core ruling is that the 'aqila (distant relatives) pay the diyah (blood money). However, there is disagreement about who takes the qasama (oath of denial). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the qasama is not upon the 'aqila, because qasama is required from people capable of giving assistance (ahl al-nusra), and women are not considered capable due to their weak constitution. Therefore, the qasama is not required from the woman either. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, holds that the qasama is upon the 'aqila as well, because the requirement of qasama is based on suspicion of killing, which is present in the woman (she is in the house), not in the 'aqila. The commentary clarifies that the term 'aqila refers to the closest relatives by lineage, not by neighborhood. Later Hanafi scholars ruled that the woman enters into bearing the diyah with the 'aqila in this specific case because we consider her as the killer; the 'aqila are secondary, so the direct killer should bear the primary responsibility. This ruling applies only when the woman has no relatives in the city; if she does, they share the qasama with her.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aaqila): The group of distant relatives responsible for paying diyah on behalf of a killer; in this passage, the closest relatives by lineage.
قسامة (qasama): An oath taken by fifty people from the 'aqila to deny responsibility for an unexplained killing in their vicinity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They must pay the diyah and perform the qasama
B) They must only pay the diyah; qasama is not required
C) They must only perform the qasama; diyah is not required
D) Neither diyah nor qasama is required

Key Takeaway
For a woman found dead alone in her house, her 'aqila pays diyah, but qasama is not required from them according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Definition of 'Aqila: Diwan vs. Kinship
Defines 'aqila and contrasts Hanafi view with Shafi'i, referencing 'Umar's practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'aqila and explain how it differs from the Shafi'i definition, citing the proof from 'Umar's practice.

Core Idea
The 'aqila is defined by functional assistance (nusra), not fixed kinship – it adapts to social organization.

The category of those responsible for paying blood money (diyah) is a point of significant divergence between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. This passage from the Hanafi sharh tradition presents the Hanafi position that the 'aqila are the people of the diwan (military register), countering the Shafi'i view that they are the tribe ('ashira). Understanding this dispute reveals a deep principle: the law follows the underlying cause (assistance) rather than a fixed form.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the 'aqila
Identify what the Hanafi school holds: the 'aqila are the people of the diwan (ahl al-diwan), i.e., the army whose names are registered in the military payroll.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing school and its proof
Al-Shafi'i holds that the 'aqila are the 'ashira (kinship group), based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the claim that no abrogation occurred after him.
The Shafi'i proof is a dalil naqli: the established sunnah.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof: practice of 'Umar
The Hanafi proof is the action of Caliph 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) who, after organizing the diwans, assigned the 'aql (diyah) upon the people of the diwan in the presence of the Companions, indicating tacit consensus (ijma suktuti).
This is a zanni al-thubut dalil but qati' al-dalala according to the Hanafi school.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
The Hanafi scholar argues that 'Umar's action is not abrogation (naskh) but a determination (taqrir) of the underlying meaning: the 'aql is upon the people of assistance (nusra). In the Prophet's time, assistance was through kinship; in 'Umar's time, it was through the diwan, so the ruling adapts while preserving the cause.
The response refutes the Shafi'i claim of no abrogation by reinterpreting the change as a shift in the manifestation of the cause, not abrogation of the original rule.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In the Hanafi school, the 'aqila can be based on institutional affiliation (diwan, guild, alliance), whereas the Shafi'i school restricts it to blood relatives. This affects who pays diyah in societies where mutual assistance is organized through institutions rather than kinship.
The passage also mentions that if people assist each other through a craft, the 'aqila are the people of that craft, following the same principle.

Source Text

Book of Ma'aqil. The 'aqila: the people of the diwan for those who are among them. [1] The 'aqila: the people of the diwan for those who are among them, meaning the army whose names are written in the diwan. This is according to us (Hanafis). And according to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him): they are the people of the tribe, because it was like that in the time of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and no abrogation occurred after him. And our proof: that 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) when he organized the diwans, made the 'aql (diyah) upon the people of the diwan, in the presence of the Companions. So this is not abrogation, but rather a determination of the meaning that the 'aql is upon the people of assistance, and it used to be through types of kinship and the like. [1] His statement: 'Book of Ma'aqil...' – my brother Jalabi said: It is with a fatha on the meem, on the pattern of makarim, the plural of ma'qila: with fatha on meem, sukun on 'ayn, and dammah on qaf, meaning 'aql (blood money), and it is the diyah. It was only named thus because the camels of blood money used to be tied (tu'qal) in the courtyard of the heir of the slain, then this name was generalized and the diyah was called ma'qila, even if it was dirhams or dinars. This is in al-Ghaya. [2] His statement: 'al-diwan' – with a kasra, the gathering place of scrolls, and the book in which the people of stipend and army are written. This is in al-Qamus. (3) [3] His statement: 'so this is not abrogation...' – a response to the statement of al-Shafi'i, the summary: that the action of 'Umar is not abrogation, but rather a determination of the meaning; because 'aql was upon the people of assistance, and assistance used to be through types: kinship, alliance, clientage, and custom. And in the time of 'Umar, it became through the diwan, so he made it upon its people in following the meaning. Therefore they said: If today there is a group that you assist through a craft, then their 'aqila are the people of the craft; and if through alliance, then its people. This is in al-Hidaya (4).
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the definition of 'aqila. The Hanafi school defines 'aqila as 'ahl al-diwan' (people registered in the military payroll), while the Shafi'i school defines it as the 'ashira (kinship group). The Hanafi proof is the practice of Caliph 'Umar, who after organizing the diwans assigned blood money to the people of the diwan in the presence of the Companions, which constitutes a tacit consensus (ijma suktuti). The Hanafi scholar argues that this is not abrogation (naskh) but rather a determination of the underlying meaning: the 'aql is upon those who provide assistance (nusra), and in the time of the Prophet, assistance was through kinship, but in 'Umar's time, it became through the diwan structure. Therefore, the ruling adapts to the changing social structure while preserving the core principle. This has practical implications: in a context where people assist each other through trade guilds, the 'aqila would be the members of the guild, not the relatives. The passage also provides linguistic notes: 'ma'aqil' plural of 'ma'qila' meaning diyah, originally from tying camels, and 'diwan' meaning the register of soldiers and stipends.

Key Terms
معاقل (ma'aqil): Plural of ma'qila, meaning blood money (diyah); also the name of the book discussing the rules of the 'aqila.
ديوان (diwan): The register of soldiers and stipendiaries; the basis for determining the 'aqila in Hanafi law.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revelation or consensus; here, the Hanafi argue that 'Umar's action is not naskh but a determination of the underlying meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The people of the diwan (military register)
C) The neighbors
D) The tribe of the Prophet (peace be upon him)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines the 'aqila functionally as the group providing assistance (nusra), adapting to social structures like the diwan, rather than fixing it to kinship.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
The 'Aqila: Collection of Blood Money (Diyah) over Three Years
Explains 'aqila, payment period, and member contributions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'aqila's payment of diyah, explain the three-year installment system, and identify the differences with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Diyah is paid by the 'aqila in three annual installments, with the killer included as a contributor.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the mechanism of collecting blood money from the 'aqila. The Hanafi school, based on the practice of the Companions, organizes payment over three years from stipends. It also addresses situations where the 'aqila is based on craft or diwan, and clarifies that the killer himself contributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the obligation of the 'aqila to pay diyah. The hukm level is wajib based on established precedent. The passage specifies the time frame and method.
The ruling applies to accidental killing (manslaughter).
2
Locate the dalil
The text references the practice during Umar's caliphate ('فصارت في عهد عمر بالديوان'), which serves as a proof (istidlal) from the actions of the Companions.
This is a dalil naqli from the practice of the Sahaba.
3
Trace the method of payment
Diyah is taken from the 'ataya (stipends) of the 'aqila over three years. Each member pays a maximum of 3-4 dirhams total; the most correct view is 1 1/3 dirham per year. If the 'aqila is insufficient, closer tribes are added by lineage.
The killer is included as one of the 'aqila members ('والقاتل كأحدهم').
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i opposes the three-year installment and excludes the killer from payment. The Hanafi response relies on the established practice of the Companions and the principle of collective responsibility.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in the Shafi'i school, the killer must pay the full diyah immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المعاقل بيان العاقلة و تؤخذ من عطاياهم في ثلاث سنين، فإن خرجت لأكثر منها أو أقل أخذ منه، وحيه لمن ليس منهم تؤخذ من كلِّ في ثلاث سنين ثلاثة دراهم أو أربعة فقط، كل سنة درهم أو مع ثلث هو الأصح. فصارت في عهد عمر الله بالديوان، وكذا لو كانت بالحرف، فالعاقلةُ على أهلِ الحرفة. وإن لم يتسع الحي ضم إليه أقرب الأحياء نسباً، الأقرب فالأقرب كما في العصبات، والقاتل كأحدهم، وللمعتق حي سيده
The chapter of al-ma'aqil: explanation of the 'aqila. And it is taken from their stipends in three years. If it extends to more than that or less, it is taken from him. And his tribe, for those not among them, is taken from each in three years three dirhams or four only, each year a dirham or with a third, that is the most correct. So it became in the era of Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, by the diwan, and similarly if it was by craft, so the 'aqila is upon the people of the craft. And if the tribe does not suffice, the closest tribes in lineage are added to it, the nearest then the nearest as in the 'asabat, and the killer is like one of them. And for the freed slave, the tribe of his master.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the rulings of the 'aqila (the collective responsible for paying blood money). The first ruling states that the diyah (blood money) is collected from the stipends ('ataya) of the 'aqila over a period of three years. This is the Hanafi position; al-Shafi'i holds that it is due immediately. If the total amount is more or less than expected, adjustments are made. For someone who is not part of the diwan (government register) or a specific craft, the payment is taken from each member of his tribe over three years, with a maximum of three or four dirhams total, either one dirham per year or one and a third dirham per year, the latter being the more correct view according to the author. The passage then notes that during the caliphate of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him), the 'aqila system was organized through the diwan (military register), and similarly for those in a craft, the 'aqila falls on the people of that craft. If the tribe (hayy) is insufficient to pay the full diyah, then the closest tribes by lineage are added, going from nearest to farthest, analogous to the order of 'asabat (agnatic heirs). The killer himself is included as one of the contributors, even though he caused the death; this is the Hanafi view, whereas al-Shafi'i excludes the killer entirely. Finally, for a freed slave (mu'taq), his 'aqila is the tribe of his master who freed him, because they are responsible for his support and protection.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'āqila): The collective group (usually male agnatic relatives) responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a member who commits manslaughter or accidental killing.
الدية (ad-diya): Financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim in cases of homicide or bodily injury where retaliation (qisas) is not applicable.
العطاء (al-'aṭā'): Stipend or salary given to soldiers or registered individuals in the diwan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, over how many years is the diyah collected from the 'aqila?
A) One year
B) Two years
C) Three years
D) Immediately

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila pays diyah over three years, the killer contributes, and the organization follows the diwan or craft system established by Umar.

Exceptions to 'Aqila Liability: Unconfirmed Settlement, Confession, and Intentional Killing with Doubt
Three exceptions to 'aqila liability with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three exceptions where the 'aqila does not bear diyat and explain the condition that if the 'aqila confirms the settlement or confession, they become liable.

Core Idea
The 'aqila does not bear what they did not agree to—settlement or confession—unless they confirm; intentional killing with doubt never shifts to the 'aqila.

The 'aqila bears diyat for manslaughter and semi-intentional killing. However, this obligation has clear limits. This lesson examines three categories where the 'aqila is not responsible: (1) diyat due by settlement (sulh) or confession (iqrar) not confirmed by the 'aqila, and (2) intentional killing that would have required qisas but was reduced due to a doubt (shubhah). We will also see the condition that if the 'aqila does confirm, the ruling changes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptions
Read the opening line: 'No, what is due by settlement or confession not confirmed by the 'aqila, or intentional killing whose qisas falls due to doubt.' These are the three cases.
The Arabic uses 'la' (no) meaning the 'aqila does not bear.
2
Understand why the 'aqila is not bound by settlement/confession
The commentator explains that the 'aqila has limited authority (qusur al-wilaya). They were not parties to the settlement or confession, so they are not bound unless they confirm.
Confirmation (tasdiq) makes them liable because they have authority over themselves.
3
Note the exception: if confirmed, they become liable
The text explicitly says: 'if they confirm, they would bear it.' This is because the refusal was due to their own right, and they have the authority to waive that right.
4
Distinguish intentional killing with doubt
Intentional killing (amd) does not allow mitigation via 'aqila liability. Even if qisas falls due to doubt (shubhah), the diyat is on the killer's own wealth, not the 'aqila.
An intra-Hanafi view that the 'aqila bears when some guardians forgive is rejected as contrary to the reliable transmission.
5
Recognize the proof from the text
The proof is implicit: the 'aqila was established for accidental and semi-intentional killings; intentional killing is excluded by its nature. The hadith of Ibn Abbas (in the next lesson) also supports this.
The relied-upon position is that the 'aqila does not bear for amd at all.

No, what is due by settlement or confession not confirmed by the 'aqila, or intentional killing whose qisas falls due due to doubt. It is said: there is an objection here, and it is that if someone kills another's slave by mistake, and its value is less than the blood-money of the forehead wound, then the 'aqila bears it. The answer to this objection: the intent [of the ruling] is concerning [injuries] below the [level of] life, but concerning life, the text came with the obligation of diya upon the 'aqila, and this is diya because the value of the slave stands in place of the diya of a free person. Thus in al-Hidayah (4). He said in al-Ashbah: the 'aqila does not bear intentional killing except in the case where some of the legal guardians forgive or settle, then the share of the remaining ones is converted into property, and the 'aqila bears it. I say: we have previously mentioned it in the chapter of qisas for [injuries] below life from the scholar Qasim: it is contrary to the transmission, and no one has said it, and what is in other books is that it is in the wealth of the killer, so be aware. [1] His saying: 'or he kills... etc.': meaning if the father kills his son intentionally, the 'aqila does not bear it, and if he said 'like killing him' it would be better to serve as an example of doubt, and from these doubtful cases is if two people kill a man and one is a child or insane and the other is a sane adult, or one uses iron and the other uses a stick. This is what the author of Radd al-Muhtar has conveyed. [2] His saying: 'and not for an injury by a slave... etc.': meaning the 'aqila does not bear an injury by a slave or intentional killing, and what is less than the blood-money of a forehead wound, based on what we have narrated earlier from Ibn Abbas as a stopped narration and raised to the Messenger of Allah [SAW]: 'The 'aqila does not bear... etc.' (3) His saying: 'rather the perpetrator... etc.': meaning it is in the wealth of the perpetrator. The analogy in it is to make equal between what is less than the blood-money: which is little, and other than it: which is much, so that all is obligatory on the 'aqila as al-Shafi'i holds, or equality in that nothing is obligatory on the 'aqila except (we) abandoned the analogy due to what we have narrated. And by what has been narrated that he obligated the blood-money of the fetus on the 'aqila, which is half a tenth of the price of a man as has passed in the chapter of diyat, so what is less than that is treated according to the treatment of property, because justice must be implemented, just as liability for property is obligatory by valuation; therefore it is in the wealth of the perpetrator as per analogy. Thus in al-Hidayah (2). (1) Radd al-Muhtar (6:643). (2) al-Hidayah (4:229).
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of what the 'aqila (the group bearing the diyat for manslaughter) does not bear. It begins by stating that the 'aqila does not bear diyat that becomes obligatory due to a settlement (sulh) or confession (iqrar) if the 'aqila does not confirm it. The reason is that the 'aqila did not participate in the settlement or confession, and they have limited authority. However, if they confirm it, they become liable. The exception is that the diya due by confession is paid in three years, while settlement for intentional killing is immediate unless a term is agreed. The passage then addresses an objection: if someone kills another's slave by mistake and the slave's value is less than the value of a forehead wound (mudihah), a previous ruling said that the 'aqila bears it even for amounts below that threshold? The response clarifies that the ruling 'below the threshold applies only to injuries short of the slave's full value, but for the full value (which is equivalent to diya of a free person), the 'aqila bears it because the slave's value stands in place of diya. Then, more exceptions are listed: the 'aqila does not bear diyat for intentional killing (including a father killing his son intentionally), injury by a slave, any intentional injury, and any injury whose value is less than that of a mudihah (a wound that lays bare the bone). In these cases, the perpetrator pays from his own wealth. The text also discusses the analogy (qiyas) that would make the 'aqila bear all amounts equally, as Imam al-Shafi'i held, but the Hanafis abandon this qiyas due to the hadith of Ibn Abbas that the 'aqila does not bear for minor injuries. Additionally, the text notes that even though the 'aqila bears the value of a fetus (which is half a tenth of the blood-money of a man), any amount smaller than that is treated as ordinary property and thus falls on the perpetrator. This is an example of istihsan overriding qiyas.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'āqila): The group of relatives (e.g., 'asaba) who bear the diyat for manslaughter on behalf of the killer.
صلح (ṣulḥ): A settlement between the parties in a homicide case, which may involve paying a sum in lieu of qisas.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession of the killer that he committed the homicide; if the 'aqila does not confirm, they are not liable.
عمد (ʿamd): Intentional killing, which requires qisas and is not borne by the 'aqila.
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt that prevents qisas, such as the killer being a child or insane, or using a weapon that could be accidental; the 'aqila may bear diyat in such cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this lesson, what happens if the 'aqila confirms a settlement in a manslaughter case?
A) The 'aqila does not pay because they were not present at the settlement.
B) The 'aqila becomes liable to pay the diyat.
C) The perpetrator must still pay from his wealth.
D) The settlement is void.

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila is only bound by what they explicitly confirm or what falls under manslaughter/semi-intentional killing; unconfirmed settlements, confessions, and intentional killing with doubt remain on the perpetrator.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
The Objection Regarding Slave Value: When Full Value is Diyat
Response to an objection about slave value vs. mudihah threshold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the apparent contradiction between the rule that the 'aqila does not bear for amounts below mudihah and the rule that the 'aqila bears the full value of a slave, even if that value is small.

Core Idea
The full value of a slave is diyat by substitution, so 'aqila bears it; smaller injuries are property claims, borne by the perpetrator.

An objection arises: If someone kills another's slave by mistake, and the slave's value is less than the threshold of a forehead wound (mudihah), the earlier rule says the 'aqila does not bear for such small amounts. Yet the 'aqila does bear the slave's value. How is this consistent? The response clarifies that the slave's full value is treated as diyat (blood money), not as an ordinary small injury. This lesson explains the key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector points to a scenario: killing a slave by mistake where the slave's value is less than the mudihah threshold. If the 'aqila does not bear for amounts below mudihah, why do they bear the slave's value?
The slave's value is treated as diyat because it replaces the diyat of a free person.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes between the full value of a slave (which is diyat for the slave) and injuries to a free person below the mudihah threshold. The full value of the slave is considered diyat, so the 'aqila bears it. The earlier rule about 'below mudihah' applies only to bodily injuries that are not equivalent to full diyat.
The text says: 'the intent is concerning [injuries] below the [level of] life, but concerning life, the text came with the obligation of diya upon the 'aqila.'
3
Identify the proof for this distinction
The proof is that the value of a slave stands in the place of the diyah of a free person. Since the 'aqila bears diyat for a free person, they bear the substitute for a slave as well, regardless of the amount.
This is an application of the principle that full diyat (or its substitute) is always on the 'aqila, not on the perpetrator.
4
Apply the lesson to new cases
If the injury to the slave is less than full value (e.g., a wound), then the same rule applies as for a free person: amounts below mudihah are on the perpetrator.
The response only covers the case of full value (death); partial injuries to a slave follow the general threshold rule.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of what the 'aqila (the group bearing the diyat for manslaughter) does not bear. It begins by stating that the 'aqila does not bear diyat that becomes obligatory due to a settlement (sulh) or confession (iqrar) if the 'aqila does not confirm it. The reason is that the 'aqila did not participate in the settlement or confession, and they have limited authority. However, if they confirm it, they become liable. The exception is that the diya due by confession is paid in three years, while settlement for intentional killing is immediate unless a term is agreed. The passage then addresses an objection: if someone kills another's slave by mistake and the slave's value is less than the value of a forehead wound (mudihah), a previous ruling said that the 'aqila bears it even for amounts below that threshold? The response clarifies that the ruling 'below the threshold applies only to injuries short of the slave's full value, but for the full value (which is equivalent to diya of a free person), the 'aqila bears it because the slave's value stands in place of diya. Then, more exceptions are listed: the 'aqila does not bear diyat for intentional killing (including a father killing his son intentionally), injury by a slave, any intentional injury, and any injury whose value is less than that of a mudihah (a wound that lays bare the bone). In these cases, the perpetrator pays from his own wealth. The text also discusses the analogy (qiyas) that would make the 'aqila bear all amounts equally, as Imam al-Shafi'i held, but the Hanafis abandon this qiyas due to the hadith of Ibn Abbas that the 'aqila does not bear for minor injuries. Additionally, the text notes that even though the 'aqila bears the value of a fetus (which is half a tenth of the blood-money of a man), any amount smaller than that is treated as ordinary property and thus falls on the perpetrator. This is an example of istihsan overriding qiyas.

Key Terms
قيمة العبد (qīmat al-‘abd): The full market value of a slave, which, if killed by mistake, is due as diyat by substitution.
أرش الموضحة (arsh al-mūḍiḥah): The fixed blood-money for a wound that lays bare the bone: 500 dirhams or 1/20 of full diya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the 'aqila bear the full value of a slave killed by mistake even if it is less than the mudihah threshold?
A) Because the 'aqila bears all property claims.
B) Because the full value of a slave is treated as diyat by substitution.
C) Because the threshold does not apply to slaves.
D) Because the perpetrator is too poor.

Key Takeaway
The full value of a slave is diyat by substitution, so the 'aqila bears it; the threshold for minor injuries only applies to wounds, not to death.

Diyat — Blood Money
advanced
10 min
Threshold of Mudihah: Qiyas vs. Hadith on 'Aqila Liability
Rule for injuries below mudihah threshold with proof and rebuttal of Shafi'i qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position that the 'aqila does not bear injuries below the mudihah threshold from the Shafi'i position that it bears all amounts, and apply the Hanafi reliance on the hadith of Ibn Abbas and istihsan.

Core Idea
The Hanafis abandon qiyas for the explicit hadith that the 'aqila does not bear for minor injuries; small amounts are property not diyat.

The 'aqila does not bear any injury whose blood-money is less than that of a forehead wound (mudihah). This lesson examines the proof for this threshold: the hadith of Ibn Abbas (marfu' and mawquf) that 'the 'aqila does not bear' for such amounts. The Hanafis rely on this text over the qiyas that would make the 'aqila bear all amounts, as argued by Imam al-Shafi'i. The ruling on the fetus (value half a tenth) is offered as additional evidence that the 'aqila bears for full diyat amounts but not for lesser sums.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
The rule is: the 'aqila does not bear any injury whose arsh (blood money) is less than that of a mudihah (forehead wound exposing bone). The perpetrator pays from his own wealth.
This comes in the list of exceptions after 'or injury by a slave or intentional killing'.
2
Locate the Hanafi proof
The text cites the hadith of Ibn Abbas (marfu' and mawquf) that 'the 'aqila does not bear...' and also the case of the fetus: the 'aqila bears the full value of the fetus (half a tenth of full diya) but nothing smaller.
The hadith is a hadith ahad, so it is zanni al-thubut but still authoritative in the absence of a stronger proof.
3
Understand the qiyas that would contradict this
The qiyas (analogy) would make the 'aqila bear all amounts equally, because diyat is due in all cases. Imam al-Shafi'i held this view: if the 'aqila bears for large amounts, they should also bear for small ones.
The qiyas is based on the commonality of the cause (illa): that it is all blood money.
4
See how the Hanafis abandon qiyas
the Hanafis abandon the qiyas because of the explicit hadith, and also because of istihsan: small amounts are treated like ordinary property claims, which are not borne by the 'aqila. The text says: 'we abandoned the analogy due to what we have narrated'.
This is a classic example of takhsis al-qiyas bi al-nass (specifying analogy by text).
5
Apply the fetus ruling as supporting evidence
The 'aqila bears the value of the fetus (half a tenth of full diya) because it is considered a full diyat for a fetus. But any amount smaller than that remains on the perpetrator.
This shows that the threshold is not arbitrary: the 'aqila bears what is considered diyat by the shari'ah, not minor property claims.

Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of what the 'aqila (the group bearing the diyat for manslaughter) does not bear. It begins by stating that the 'aqila does not bear diyat that becomes obligatory due to a settlement (sulh) or confession (iqrar) if the 'aqila does not confirm it. The reason is that the 'aqila did not participate in the settlement or confession, and they have limited authority. However, if they confirm it, they become liable. The exception is that the diya due by confession is paid in three years, while settlement for intentional killing is immediate unless a term is agreed. The passage then addresses an objection: if someone kills another's slave by mistake and the slave's value is less than the value of a forehead wound (mudihah), a previous ruling said that the 'aqila bears it even for amounts below that threshold? The response clarifies that the ruling 'below the threshold applies only to injuries short of the slave's full value, but for the full value (which is equivalent to diya of a free person), the 'aqila bears it because the slave's value stands in place of diya. Then, more exceptions are listed: the 'aqila does not bear diyat for intentional killing (including a father killing his son intentionally), injury by a slave, any intentional injury, and any injury whose value is less than that of a mudihah (a wound that lays bare the bone). In these cases, the perpetrator pays from his own wealth. The text also discusses the analogy (qiyas) that would make the 'aqila bear all amounts equally, as Imam al-Shafi'i held, but the Hanafis abandon this qiyas due to the hadith of Ibn Abbas that the 'aqila does not bear for minor injuries. Additionally, the text notes that even though the 'aqila bears the value of a fetus (which is half a tenth of the blood-money of a man), any amount smaller than that is treated as ordinary property and thus falls on the perpetrator. This is an example of istihsan overriding qiyas.

Key Terms
أرش الموضحة (arsh al-mūḍiḥah): The fixed blood-money for a wound that lays bare the bone: 500 dirhams or 1/20 of full diya.
جنين (janīn): Fetus; its blood-money (half a tenth of full diya) is borne by the 'aqila.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The qiyas of larger injuries.
B) The hadith of Ibn Abbas that the 'aqila does not bear for such amounts.
C) The ijma of the Companions.
D) The custom of the people.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab sets a minimum threshold for 'aqila liability (arsh al-mudihah) based on explicit hadith, and small amounts are treated as personal property claims.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
When the 'Aqila is Exempt from Blood-Money
Ruling on 'Aqila non-liability with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where the 'Aqila is not liable in Hanafi law and explain how the hadith overrides the qiyas in the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The 'Aqila is exempt for intentional killing, slave's injury, and minor wounds; the offender pays.

This lesson examines the precise boundaries of 'Aqila liability in Hanafi fiqh. While the 'Aqila generally bears blood-money for accidental killings and injuries, there are clear exceptions. The source passage from al-Hidayah, as commented upon in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, identifies three situations where the 'Aqila is not liable and the offender must pay from his own wealth. We will dissect each exception, the proof from hadith, and the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over the role of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptions
The passage lists: (1) a father killing his son intentionally, (2) any injury caused by a slave or any intentional killing, (3) injuries with blood-money less than a mawdihah (head wound exposing bone). State each exception clearly.
Intentional acts and slave injuries are never accidental, hence no 'Aqila liability.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith from Ibn Abbas (mawquf and marfu') 'La ta'qil al-'awaqil' is the textual proof. The negation indicates non-liability for these specific cases.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection via qiyas
Imam al-Shafi'i argues by qiyas: if the 'Aqila is liable for large amounts, they should also be liable for small amounts (taswiya bain al-qalil wa al-kathir). This would make the 'Aqila liable for minor injuries.
This qiyas would also apply to the other exceptions by extension.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response: Istihsan
The Hanafis abandon the qiyas (istihsan) because the hadith directly contradicts it. Also, minor amounts are treated as property (mal) rather than blood-money, so they fall on the offender like any property damage.
The fetus example (1/20 diya) is on the 'Aqila, showing a threshold: amounts above which are on 'Aqila; lesser amounts are treated as property.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Given the three exceptions, test hypotheticals: e.g., a man accidentally injures another with a minor wound; the 'Aqila does not pay if the arsh is less than mawdihah. Or a slave intentionally kills; the 'Aqila does not pay – the slave's owner pays or the slave is surrendered.
This demonstrates the thamarah al-khilaf: real-world difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المعاقل بيان العاقلة
أو قتله ابنه عمداً، ولا بجناية عبد أو عمد، وما دون أرش موضحة، بل الجاني.
[1] قوله: أو قتله... الخ؛ أي لو قتل الأب ابنه عمداً فلا تتحمل العاقلة، وإن قال كقتله لكان أولى؛ ليكون تمثيلاً للشبهة، ومنها ما إذا قتلا رجلاً وأحدهما صبي أو معتوه، والآخر عاقل بالغ، أو أحدهما بحديد والآخر بعصا، هذا ما أفاده صاحب «ردّ المحتار».
[2] قوله: ولا بجناية... الخ؛ أي لا تتحمل العاقلة جناية عبد أو عمد، وما دون أرش موضحة لما روينا آنفاً عن ابن عباس موقوفاً عليه، ومرفوعاً إلى رسول الله ﷺ: «لا تعقل العواقل...» الخ.

Chapter of 'Aqila: Explanation of the 'Aqila
Or if he killed his son intentionally, and not for the injury of a slave or intentional [killing], and what is less than the blood-money of a mawdihah. Rather the offender.
[1] His statement: 'or if he killed...' etc.; meaning if the father killed his son intentionally, then the 'Aqila does not bear, and if he said 'like killing him' it would be more appropriate to be an example of ambiguity; and among these is if two killed a man and one of them is a child or insane and the other is a sane adult, or one with iron and the other with a stick. This is what the author of Radd al-Muhtar mentioned.
[2] His statement: 'and not for the injury...' etc.; meaning the 'Aqila does not bear the injury of a slave or intentional [killing], or what is less than the blood-money of a mawdihah, because of what we narrated earlier from Ibn Abbas as a mawquf hadith, and marfu' to the Messenger of Allah ﷺ: 'The 'awaqil do not bear...' etc.
[3] His statement: 'Rather the offender...' etc.; meaning it shall be from the wealth of the offender. By analogy there is equality between what is less than the blood-money of a mawdihah—which is little, and other—which is abundant, so all would be obligatory on the 'Aqila as al-Shafi'i held; or equality in that nothing is obligatory on the 'Aqila except we have abandoned analogy because of what we narrated. And by what was narrated that he required the blood-money of a fetus on the 'Aqila, it is one-twentieth of the value of a man, as passed in diyat, and what is less than that is treated in the manner of wealth; because it is necessary to implement justice, just as it is necessary to guarantee property by valuation; therefore it is in the wealth of the offender, taking by analogy. This is in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of the 'Aqila (the group responsible for paying blood-money) in Hanafi fiqh. It is a ruling stating that the 'Aqila is not liable in three cases: (1) a father killing his son intentionally, (2) injuries caused by a slave or intentional killing, and (3) injuries less than the blood-money of a mawdihah (a head wound that exposes bone). In these cases, the offender pays from his own wealth. The commentary explains that the 'Aqila does not bear responsibility for a father killing his son intentionally, as this is considered an ambiguous situation (shubha). Similarly, the 'Aqila does not bear for a slave's injury because the slave's owner is liable, and for intentional acts because they are deliberate, not accidental. For minor injuries, the 'Aqila is not liable because the amount is too small. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Abbas that the 'awaqil (plural of 'Aqila) do not bear such cases. The commentary also addresses a potential analogy: since the 'Aqila is liable for large amounts, it should also be liable for small amounts by qiyas. However, the Hanafi school abandons this analogy (istihsan) based on the narrated hadith. The Shafi'i school holds that the 'Aqila should be liable for all amounts by qiyas, but the Hanafis prefer the textual evidence. This discussion shows the Hanafi method of preferring hadith over qiyas when they conflict.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('Aqila): The group of agnates (asaba) who are collectively responsible for paying blood-money (diya) for accidental killings or injuries caused by a member of the group.
موضحة (Mawdihah): A wound on the head or face that exposes the bone; its blood-money is fixed at five camels (0.5% of full diya).
عمد ('Amd): Intentional act; the 'Aqila is not liable for intentional killing or injury because it is not accidental.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, for which of the following is the 'Aqila NOT liable?
A) A father killing his son intentionally
B) An injury caused by a slave
C) An injury with blood-money less than a mawdihah
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The 'Aqila is not liable for intentional killing, slave injuries, or minor wounds below the arsh of mawdihah; the offender pays, as per the hadith 'La ta'qil al-'awaqil' overriding qiyas.
